### [1] [Domain Adaptation of Large Language Models for Polymer-Composite Additive Manufacturing Using Retrieval-Augmented Generation and Fine-Tuning](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.12516)
*Saiful Islam Sagor,Tania Haghighi,Minhaj Nur Alam,Erina Baynojir Joyee*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper compares adapting a general LLM to additive manufacturing using RAG vs naive domain fine-tuning and finds RAG strongly outperforms fine-tuning on expert QA tasks.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: General LLMs perform poorly on expert engineering queries because they lack grounding in specialized, heterogeneous technical sources. The authors want a practical way to adapt foundation models to the additive manufacturing domain so that answers become accurate, relevant, and useful for experts, without requiring massive re-training.

Method: They build a curated additive manufacturing corpus from diverse technical sources and create three LLaMA-3-8B configurations: (1) the untouched pretrained model, (2) a retrieval-augmented generation system that pulls relevant chunks from a vector database, and (3) a model fine-tuned only on raw AM text. They then evaluate these three setups on 200 expert-authored AM questions, with mechanical engineering experts rating each answer for accuracy, relevance, and overall preference.

Result: The RAG configuration markedly outperforms the baseline: 75.5% of its answers are judged more accurate, 90.8% more relevant, and 85.2% preferred overall compared to the pretrained model. The naively fine-tuned model actually degrades performance, beating baseline accuracy in only 5.6% of cases and relevance in 32.5% of cases.

Conclusion: For specialized engineering domains like additive manufacturing, retrieval-augmented generation is a more effective and reliable adaptation strategy than naive fine-tuning on unstructured domain text. Simply exposing an LLM to raw technical data via fine-tuning can hurt performance, underscoring the importance of structured retrieval and context over undirected domain adaptation.

Abstract: General-purpose large language models (LLMs) often struggle to generate reliable responses in specialized engineering domains due to limited domain grounding and insufficient exposure to structured technical knowledge. This study investigates practical strategies for adapting a foundation LLM to the additive manufacturing (AM) domain in order to improve answer accuracy, relevance, and usability for expert-level question answering. AM knowledge is distributed across heterogeneous sources such as academic literature, manufacturer documentation, technical standards, and procedural guides. Although general LLMs demonstrate strong linguistic capabilities, they frequently fail to retrieve and contextualize such domain-specific information. Two common approaches to address this limitation are domain-specific fine-tuning and retrieval-augmented generation (RAG). We construct a curated AM corpus and evaluate three configurations based on LLaMA-3-8B: (1) the pretrained baseline model, (2) a RAG system that retrieves relevant document chunks from a vector database, and (3) a model fine-tuned on raw domain text. Performance is evaluated using 200 expert-designed AM questions assessed by mechanical engineering experts for accuracy, relevance, and overall preference. Results show that the RAG model consistently outperforms the baseline. Among the 200 questions, 75.5% of RAG responses are judged more accurate, 85.2% are preferred overall, and 90.8% are rated more relevant than baseline responses. In contrast, fine-tuning on raw AM text reduces performance, producing more accurate answers in only 5.6% of cases and more relevant answers in 32.5% of cases. These results indicate that retrieval-augmented approaches provide a more effective pathway for adapting LLMs to specialized engineering domains than naive fine-tuning on unstructured technical data.

</details>


### [2] [Bridging the Missing-Modality Gap: Improving Text-Only Calibration of Vision Language Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.12517)
*Mingyeong Kim,Jungwon Choi,Chaeyun Jang,Juho Lee*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: They study why vision-language models perform poorly and are miscalibrated when used with text-only inputs, and propose a “Latent Imagination Module” that hallucinates visual latents from text to restore performance and calibration without generating images.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Vision-language models are frequently used in text-only settings (e.g., chat with no images) despite being trained jointly on images and text. However, when the image modality is missing, these models suffer large accuracy drops and unreliable confidence estimates, and they do not revert to behaving like their underlying language backbone. Existing explanations that attribute this solely to missing semantic content are insufficient, since even detailed textual descriptions cannot fully recover calibration. There is a practical need for a method that allows VLMs to operate reliably when the visual modality is absent or unavailable, without requiring expensive or complex image synthesis.

Method: They first empirically analyze the behavior of VLMs under text-only inputs, measuring accuracy and calibration and comparing them to both full VLM usage (with images) and the original language backbone. They then introduce the Latent Imagination Module (LIM), a small cross-attention module that takes text as input and predicts corresponding visual latent embeddings. These imagined visual latents are injected into a frozen VLM backbone at the appropriate layers, effectively simulating the presence of an image. LIM does not generate pixel-space images; instead, it operates entirely in the latent feature space, making it lightweight and efficient. The module is trained so that, given text, it recovers latent representations similar to those produced when actual images are available.

Result: When deployed on a variety of text-only benchmarks, unseen tasks, and scenarios where images are missing, LIM-equipped VLMs show improved task accuracy and significantly better confidence calibration compared to running the VLM without any visual input. While simply augmenting text with detailed descriptions does not fix miscalibration, and naive generated images only partially help, LIM’s latent modality completion leads to more reliable predictions. The approach improves both performance and robustness across different missing-modality settings.

Conclusion: The work demonstrates that vision-language models are fundamentally altered by multimodal training and cannot be safely treated as ordinary language models when images are absent: they become inaccurate and miscalibrated. This degradation is not just about missing semantic content. By introducing a Latent Imagination Module that predicts visual latent embeddings from text and feeds them into a frozen VLM, the authors show that latent-level modality completion can restore much of the lost performance and calibration. This positions latent imagination as a practical and efficient strategy for reliable VLM inference in text-only or missing-image scenarios, without needing explicit image generation.

Abstract: Vision-language models (VLMs) are often deployed on text-only inputs, although they are trained with images. We find that removing the vision modality causes large drops in accuracy and severe miscalibration, and the model does not behave like its original language backbone under text-only prompting. This failure is not explained only by missing semantic information. Even when text descriptions preserve key content, confidence becomes unreliable, while adding a visual signal through generated images partially restores accuracy and calibration. We propose the Latent Imagination Module (LIM), a lightweight cross-attention module that predicts imagined latent embeddings from textual input and feeds them into a frozen VLM backbone without pixel-level image synthesis. Across text-only benchmarks, unseen tasks, and missing-image scenarios, LIM improves accuracy and reduces calibration error. These results suggest that latent modality completion is a practical approach for reliable VLM inference under missing-modality.

</details>


### [3] [TimelineReasoner: Advancing Timeline Summarization with Large Reasoning Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.12518)
*Liancheng Zhang,Xiaoxi Li,Zhicheng Dou*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: TimelineReasoner is a reasoning-driven framework that uses Large Reasoning Models to actively construct more accurate and coherent timelines from online news.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing timeline summarization methods, even those using LLMs, mainly treat models as passive text generators, which struggle with fragmented, noisy news streams and miss events or introduce temporal inconsistencies. The authors aim to exploit more powerful reasoning capabilities to track events over time, fill gaps, and validate temporal logic when building timelines from unstructured news.

Method: The authors design TimelineReasoner, a two-stage framework built around Large Reasoning Models. In the Global Cognition stage, the system tracks events at a high level and maintains a global event memory that is continuously updated. In the Detail Exploration stage, it identifies missing or unclear parts of the timeline and triggers targeted retrieval of relevant documents. Specialized components include: (1) an Event Scraper to extract and retrieve temporal event descriptions from documents; (2) a Timeline Updater to integrate new evidence and refine the current timeline; and (3) a Supervisor module to detect gaps or inconsistencies and to steer further retrieval and reasoning. The process is iterative and actively guided by reasoning rather than a single-shot generation.

Result: On open-domain timeline summarization benchmarks, TimelineReasoner significantly improves over prior LLM-based methods in timeline accuracy, coverage of events, and narrative coherence. On closed-domain datasets, its performance matches or surpasses state-of-the-art systems, indicating that the reasoning-centric design generalizes across domains.

Conclusion: TimelineReasoner shows that shifting from static, generation-only LLM use to an active, reasoning-centered process with LRMs can substantially enhance timeline summarization. The work demonstrates that explicitly modeling global event memory, iterative evidence gathering, and supervision for gap detection yields more accurate and coherent timelines, and it suggests that similar LRM-based reasoning frameworks could benefit other structured summarization tasks.

Abstract: The proliferation of online news poses a challenge to extracting structured timelines from unstructured content. While recent studies have shown that Large Language Models (LLMs) can assist Timeline Summarization (TLS), these approaches primarily treat models as passive generators. The emergence of Large Reasoning Models (LRMs) presents an opportunity to reason over events actively, enabling iterative evidence acquisition, the detection of missing events, and the validation of temporal consistency. To systematically leverage the reasoning capabilities of LRMs, we propose TimelineReasoner, a novel framework that shifts TLS from static generation to an active, reasoning-driven process. Unlike prior work, TimelineReasoner adopts a two-stage framework: Global Cognition, which tracks events at a macroscopic level and continuously updates a global event memory, and Detail Exploration, which identifies informational gaps and refines the timeline via targeted document retrieval. To support this, TimelineReasoner incorporates several specialized mechanisms, including an Event Scraper for retrieving temporal event descriptions, a Timeline Updater for refining the timeline, and a Supervisor for detecting gaps in the timeline and guiding retrieval. Experimental results on open-domain TLS datasets demonstrate that TimelineReasoner significantly outperforms existing LLM-based TLS methods in terms of timeline accuracy, coverage, and coherence. On closed-domain TLS datasets, our method performs on par with or exceeds state-of-the-art approaches. This work not only pushes the boundaries of TLS but also highlights the broader potential of LRM-based reasoning frameworks for timeline summarization.

</details>


### [4] [Correct Answers from Sound Reasoning: Verifiable Process Supervision for Language Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.12519)
*Kyuyoung Kim,Kevin Wang,Yunfei Xie,Peiyang Xu,Peiyao Sheng,Chen Wei,Zhangyang Wang,Jinwoo Shin,Pramod Viswanath,Sewoong Oh*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces Verifiable Process Supervision (VPS), a post-training framework that simultaneously optimizes answer accuracy and reasoning quality for language models in verifiable domains, demonstrated on chess.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing RL methods with verifiable rewards focus on final outcomes, causing models to find shortcuts that improve task accuracy while degrading intermediate reasoning—making it incomplete, inconsistent, or incorrect. The authors want a method that enforces good reasoning processes, not just correct final answers, in domains where intermediate steps can be checked.

Method: 1) First, supervised fine-tuning (SFT) is used to induce a structured reasoning format so that the model emits explicit intermediate claims. 2) These intermediate claims are automatically checked against ground-truth “engine” or oracle signals to produce process-level rewards. 3) The authors introduce adaptive reward weighting: subtasks/claims with larger remaining errors receive higher weights, producing an implicit curriculum over reasoning components. 4) They apply reinforcement learning using these process-level rewards (and outcome rewards) to jointly optimize accuracy and reasoning quality. The framework is evaluated in chess, where every reasoning step is deterministically verifiable using a chess engine.

Result: On chess, standard accuracy-only RL increases move accuracy but significantly worsens reasoning quality: win-rate error grows by up to 112% and internal consistency drops by up to 69%. VPS, however, maintains or matches the move accuracy while substantially improving reasoning: win-rate error is reduced by up to 30% and internal consistency is restored to near its upper bound. Human/LLM judges also prefer explanations from VPS-trained models when accuracy is matched. Analysis of the learned reasoning trajectories shows that accuracy-only RL tends to exploit budget-dependent shortcuts rather than doing genuine multi-step reasoning, whereas VPS preserves structured reasoning paths.

Conclusion: Verifiable Process Supervision (VPS) successfully aligns both outcomes and reasoning in verifiable domains by supervising structured intermediate steps with adaptive rewards. Unlike accuracy-only RL, which encourages shortcutting and deteriorated reasoning, VPS yields models that are both accurate and reliably reasoned. This suggests a promising direction for training language models in settings where intermediate reasoning can be checked, and points to the importance of process-level supervision to avoid reward hacking and superficial shortcut strategies.

Abstract: Training language models to produce both correct answers and sound reasoning remains an open challenge. Reinforcement learning with verifiable rewards typically optimizes only final outcomes, which can lead to a failure mode where task accuracy improves while reasoning becomes less accurate, less complete, or even internally inconsistent. We propose verifiable process supervision (VPS), a post-training framework for verifiable domains that jointly optimizes prediction accuracy and reasoning quality. We first apply supervised fine-tuning to induce a structured reasoning format, enabling syntactic extraction of intermediate claims that are evaluated against ground-truth signals to form process-level rewards. To address the heterogeneous difficulty of reasoning subtasks, we introduce adaptive reward weighting that prioritizes components with the largest remaining errors, creating an implicit curriculum. We evaluate VPS on chess, a controlled testbed where reasoning steps can be deterministically verified against engine signals. While accuracy-only RL improves move accuracy, it sharply degrades reasoning quality, increasing win-rate error by up to 112% and reducing internal consistency by up to 69%. In contrast, VPS preserves accuracy while significantly improving reasoning quality, reducing win-rate error by up to 30% and restoring consistency to near saturation. At matched accuracy, judge evaluation also prefers the process-supervised models. A reasoning-space analysis further shows that, without a structured prior, accuracy-only RL converges to budget-dependent shortcuts rather than sound multi-step reasoning. These results show that VPS enables language models to reason both accurately and reliably in verifiable domains.

</details>


### [5] [BoostTaxo: Zero-Shot Taxonomy Induction via Boosting-Style Agentic Reasoning and Constraint-Aware Calibration](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.12520)
*Yancheng Ling,Zhenlin Qin,Leizhen Wang,Zhenliang Ma*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: BoostTaxo is a boosting-style, zero-shot taxonomy induction framework that uses a combination of lightweight and large LLMs, retrieval-augmented refinement, and structure-aware calibration to build accurate and reliable taxonomies from domain terms.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing taxonomy induction methods struggle with generalization, structural reliability, and efficiency, particularly in zero-shot and large-scale settings, limiting their usability in real-world applications that require building taxonomies from raw domain terms without labeled data.

Method: BoostTaxo takes as input a set of domain terms and incrementally performs parent identification in a coarse-to-fine fashion. It first refines term definitions via retrieval-augmented generation, then performs hybrid parent candidate selection using a lightweight LLM to efficiently filter a broad candidate set. A large LLM is then used to rank and score candidate parents for fine-grained selection. Finally, structural features of the emerging taxonomy are used for structure-aware score calibration to adjust edge weights and improve global consistency and reliability.

Result: On three public benchmarks—WordNet, DBLP, and SemEval-Sci—BoostTaxo achieves superior or comparable performance to state-of-the-art zero-shot taxonomy induction methods. Ablation studies show that hybrid parent candidate selection and structure-aware score calibration both make significant contributions to the performance. Additional analyses examine how candidate set size affects taxonomy quality and provide qualitative case and failure studies.

Conclusion: BoostTaxo effectively addresses generalization, reliability, and efficiency challenges in zero-shot taxonomy induction by combining lightweight and large LLMs in a boosting-style framework with structural calibration. It delivers strong empirical performance and offers analytical insights into design choices and limitations, making it a promising approach for scalable, zero-shot taxonomy construction in various domains.

Abstract: Taxonomy induction is crucial for organizing concepts into explicit and interpretable semantic hierarchies. While existing methods have achieved promising results, their generalization, structural reliability, and efficiency remain limited, hindering their performance in zero-shot and large-scale scenarios. To overcome these limitations, we introduce BoostTaxo, a boosting-style LLM framework for zero-shot taxonomy induction. It takes a set of domain terms as inputs and performs parent identification in a coarse-to-fine manner, employing retrieval-augmented definition refinement, hybrid parent candidate selection, candidate rating, and structure-aware score calibration to improve taxonomy construction. Specifically, a lightweight LLM is used to efficiently filter candidate parents, while a large-scale LLM is employed to rank and score candidate parents for fine-grained parent selection. Structural features are further incorporated to calibrate candidate edge weights and enhance the reliability of the induced taxonomy. The unified BoostTaxo is evaluated on three public benchmark datasets, namely WordNet, DBLP, and SemEval-Sci, and achieves superior or comparable performance to state-of-the-art methods in zero-shot taxonomy induction. The ablation study validates the contribution of the hybrid parent candidate selection and the structure-aware score calibration to the overall performance. Further analysis investigates the impact of candidate selection size on taxonomy quality and presents representative case and failure studies, providing deeper insights into the effectiveness and limitations of the proposed framework.

</details>


### [6] [ToolWeave: Structured Synthesis of Complex Multi-Turn Tool-Calling Dialogues](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.12521)
*Dinesh Khandelwal,Gnana Prakash Punnavajhala,GPS Bhargav,Gaurav Pandey,Sachin Joshi,Hima Karanam,Dinesh Raghu*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: ToolWeave is a framework for generating realistic multi-turn, multi-step tool-calling dialogues to better train LLM agents, reducing hallucinations and improving benchmark performance over prior synthetic datasets.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Current synthetic data pipelines for training tool-using LLM agents often yield unrealistic dialogues: tools are chained in ways that don’t reflect genuine user tasks, dialogues are generated in one shot causing unsupported arguments, and multi-step tool interactions are underrepresented. This degrades the ability of LLMs to act as robust autonomous agents. A better data synthesis process is needed that respects real task structure and parameter provenance.

Method: ToolWeave builds structured, realistic multi-step tool workflows by (1) designing tools with explicit dependencies so that certain tools naturally follow others in a workflow, (2) filtering candidate workflows according to their alignment with plausible user goals, and (3) introducing a fine-grained planning stage that tracks where each parameter comes from (user input vs. tool outputs), thereby constraining dialogue generation and limiting hallucinated arguments and tool calls. The system is then used to synthesize multi-turn tool-calling dialogues for fine-tuning LLMs.

Result: Synthetic dialogues generated by ToolWeave contain a substantially higher proportion of realistic multi-step tool interactions (45%) and exhibit fewer hallucinations in both parameters and tool names, compared with existing pipelines like ToolFlow. LLMs fine-tuned on ToolWeave data outperform those trained on prior synthetic datasets across three public tool-use benchmarks. For example, Llama-3.1-70B fine-tuned on ToolWeave reaches 39.75% on the BFCL-V3 multi-turn benchmark versus 23.50% when fine-tuned on SOTA ToolFlow data.

Conclusion: Structuring synthetic dialogue generation around dependency-aware tool workflows and explicit parameter provenance yields more realistic, multi-step tool-calling data, which in turn significantly improves LLM tool-use performance. ToolWeave demonstrates that careful control over workflow design and planning can reduce hallucinations and enhance multi-turn tool competency, offering a more effective path for training autonomous LLM agents than prior one-shot, loosely structured pipelines.

Abstract: Multi-turn tool calling is essential for LLMs to function as autonomous agents, yet synthesizing the training data required for these capabilities remains a fundamental challenge. Existing synthetic data generation pipelines often produce unrealistic dialogues for two reasons: they chain tools that are only superficially compatible rather than aligned with meaningful user tasks, and they generate dialogues in one shot, which often introduces arguments that were neither provided by the user nor produced by prior tool calls. These issues also lead to a severe underrepresentation of multi-step tool interactions. We introduce ToolWeave, a structured framework for synthesizing realistic multi-turn tool-calling dialogues. ToolWeave support realistic multi-step workflows (or tool sequences) by constructing tools with built-in dependencies and filters the workflows based on alignment with user goals. It reduces parameter hallucination by using a fine-grained planning stage that explicitly tracks parameter provenance. As a result, ToolWeave-generated synthetic dialogues contain more multi-step tool interactions (45%) and fewer hallucinations in parameters and tool names. Consequently, LLMs fine-tuned on ToolWeave consistently outperform those fine-tuned on prior datasets across three public benchmarks. Notably, Llama-3.1-70B fine-tuned on ToolWeave achieves 39.75% on BFCL-V3 multi-turn, compared to 23.50% when fine-tuned on SOTA ToolFlow data.

</details>


### [7] [Differences in Text Generated by Diffusion and Autoregressive Language Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.12522)
*Zeyang Zhang,Chengwei Liang,Xingyan Chen,Meiqi Gu,Minrui Luo,Jingzhao Zhang,Tianxing He*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper compares Diffusion Language Models (DLMs) with Autoregressive Models (ARMs) and identifies why their generated texts differ in entropy, semantic coherence, and diversity.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Although DLMs are promising alternatives to ARMs, the intrinsic differences in their generated text are not well understood, which hinders principled model and decoding design.

Method: The authors empirically measure n-gram entropy, semantic coherence, and semantic diversity of off-the-shelf DLMs vs. ARMs, then perform controlled experiments that separate the effects of training objectives and decoding algorithms, and analyze specific factors such as bidirectional context, masking schemes, weighting, and confidence-based remasking. They also provide a theoretical analysis of entropy reduction in DLM decoding.

Result: They find that DLMs have lower n-gram entropy but higher semantic coherence and diversity. Training objectives, especially the use of bidirectional context, drive the semantic differences, while decoding algorithms—especially confidence-based remasking—primarily cause entropy reduction. Other objective components (input/label masking, weighting) have relatively minor effects.

Conclusion: The key behavioral differences between DLMs and ARMs arise mainly from bidirectional context in the DLM training objective and confidence-based remasking in DLM decoding. Understanding these mechanisms can guide better training objective design and decoding strategies for future DLMs.

Abstract: Diffusion language models (DLMs) are promising alternatives to autoregressive language models (ARMs), yet the intrinsic differences in their generated text remain underexplored. We first find empirically that off-the-shelf DLMs exhibit lower $n$-gram entropy, higher semantic coherence, and higher semantic diversity. To understand the cause, we conduct controlled experiments that decouple the effects of training objectives and decoding algorithms. Results suggest that the DLM training objective contributes to the increases in semantic coherence and semantic diversity, but has a minor influence on entropy. These differences are primarily driven by the bidirectional context; other components in the training objective, such as input masking, label masking, and the weighting function, have a much weaker influence. Further, our experiments demonstrate that the reduction in entropy stems from DLMs' decoding algorithms, particularly confidence-based remasking strategies. We provide a theoretical understanding for this entropy reduction phenomenon. Together, our work uncovers key mechanisms underlying the differences between DLMs and ARMs in text generation, and informs future design of training objectives and decoding algorithms in DLMs.

</details>


### [8] [Exploring how EFL students talk to and through AI to develop texts](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.12523)
*David James Woo,Yangyang Yu,Yilin Huang,Deliang Wang,Kai Guo,Chi Ho Yeung*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: Explores how EFL students use generative AI chatbots in writing, identifying prompting strategies and human–AI authorship patterns, and examining links to writing quality.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: To understand the pedagogical implications of generative AI in EFL writing by examining how students actually interact with AI tools, share rhetorical responsibility with them, and whether these interaction patterns affect writing performance.

Method: Exploratory mixed-methods design with 44 Hong Kong secondary students doing a curricular writing task using AI chatbots; screen recordings were content-analyzed to code prompting strategies, then clustered into profiles of rhetorical load responsibility (AI-dominant, Human-dominant, Collaborative); MANOVA tested whether profiles predicted writing performance in content, language, and organization.

Result: Ten prompting strategy types were identified (e.g., questions, searches, detailed instructions). Three main profiles of human–AI rhetorical load responsibility emerged: AI-dominant (52%), Human-dominant (25%), and Collaborative human–AI (14%). MANOVA showed no significant multivariate effect of these profiles on students' writing performance across content, language, and organization.

Conclusion: Different ways of sharing rhetorical responsibility with AI (from AI-dominant to human-dominant and collaborative) do not necessarily translate into measurable differences in short-term writing performance, but they matter for how students engage with tools and develop autonomy. EFL pedagogy should explicitly address prompting strategies and human–AI collaboration in writing instruction.

Abstract: Generative Artificial Intelligence (AI) introduces new considerations for English as a foreign language (EFL) writing pedagogy. This study explores how students talk to and through AI by prompt engineering and negotiating authorship, respectively, and whether any patterns in the latter relate to students' writing performance. Using an exploratory mixed methods design, we analyzed screen recordings of 44 Hong Kong secondary students completing a Curricular Writing Task with AI Chatbots. Content analysis identified ten types of prompting strategies students employed, including questions, searches, and detailed instructions. From clustering these strategies, three distinct profiles of human-AI rhetorical load responsibility emerged: AI-dominant (52% of students), Human-dominant (25%) and Collaborative human-AI (14%). A MANOVA analysis indicated no significant multivariate effect of rhetorical load responsibility on three dimensions of students' writing performance: content, language, and organization. Students' prompting strategies and rhetorical load responsibility patterns have implications for their engagement and autonomy in EFL writing pedagogy.

</details>


### [9] [In-Situ Behavioral Evaluation for LLM Fairness, Not Standardized-Test Scores](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.12530)
*Zeyu Tang,Sang T. Truong,Deonna Owens,Shreyas Sharma,Yibo Jacky Zhang,Brando Miranda,Sanmi Koyejo*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper argues that fairness of large language models (LLMs) should be measured via their behavior in realistic multi-agent conversations rather than through standardized-test style Q&A benchmarks, and proposes MAC-Fairness, a large-scale conversational evaluation framework revealing stable, model-specific fairness behaviors.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing LLM fairness evaluations mostly use standardized-test multiple-choice or Q&A benchmarks. The authors observe that these setups may not reflect how LLMs are used in practice (conversationally) and may be structurally unreliable: small, irrelevant prompt-design differences can significantly alter fairness scores, model rankings, and conclusions. This unreliability undermines trust in current fairness assessments and motivates the need for an in-situ, conversational, and more robust method of measuring fairness-related behavior.

Method: The authors empirically demonstrate structural unreliability of standardized-test style fairness benchmarks by showing that surface-level prompt construction choices, which are conceptually orthogonal to the fairness construct being tested, explain most of the score variance and even flip fairness conclusions and model rankings. They then introduce MAC-Fairness, a multi-agent conversational framework where several LLM agents interact over multiple dialogue rounds. Identity attributes are systematically varied within these interactions, and standardized-test questions are repurposed only as initial conversation seeds rather than as scoring instruments. From over 8 million conversation transcripts across multiple models and identity configurations, they define behavioral metrics focusing on (1) position persistence—how consistently an agent maintains its own stance over time—and (2) peer receptiveness—how much it accommodates or updates in response to other agents’ views.

Result: They find that prompt-construction artifacts dominate variance in traditional standardized-test fairness scores, leading to unstable fairness estimates, directionally inconsistent bias findings, and shifting model rankings. In contrast, the conversational MAC-Fairness setup yields stable, model-specific behavioral patterns when identity variables are manipulated. These behavioral signatures are robust across different fairness targets and evaluation methodologies, suggesting that in-situ conversational metrics (e.g., position persistence and peer receptiveness) capture more reliable and generalizable aspects of LLM fairness behavior than standard Q&A scores.

Conclusion: The paper concludes that fairness assessments for LLMs should shift from static, standardized-test Q&A benchmarks toward in-situ, multi-agent conversational evaluations. The proposed MAC-Fairness framework shows that conversational behavioral metrics can uncover consistent, model-specific fairness signatures that generalize better across contexts and benchmarks, offering a more reliable basis for understanding and comparing LLM fairness than current standardized-test paradigms.

Abstract: LLM fairness should be evaluated through in-situ conversational behavior rather than standardized-test Q&A benchmarks. We show that the standardized-test paradigm can be structurally unreliable: surface-level prompt construction choices, although entirely orthogonal to the fairness question being tested, account for the majority of score variance, shift fairness conclusions in both the direction and the magnitude, and result in severe discordance in model rankings. We develop MAC-Fairness, a multi-agent conversational framework that embeds controlled variation factors into multi-round dialogue for in-situ behavior evaluation, examining how models' conversational behavior shifts when identity is varied as part of natural multi-agent interaction. Repurposing standardized-test questions as conversation seeds rather than as the evaluation instrument, we evaluate position persistence (how they hold positions, from the self-perspective) and peer receptiveness (how receptive they are to peers, from the other-perspective) across 8 million conversation transcripts spanning multiple models and identity presence configurations. In-situ behavioral evaluation reveals stable, model-specific behavioral signatures that could generalize across benchmarks differing in fairness targets and evaluation methodologies, a form of evidence the standardized-test paradigm does not offer.

</details>


### [10] [DocAtlas: Multilingual Document Understanding Across 80+ Languages](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.12623)
*Ahmed Heakl,Youssef Mohamed,Abdullah Sohail,Rania Elbadry,Ahmed Nassar,Peter W. J. Staar,Fahad Shahbaz Khan,Imran Razzak,Salman Khan*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: DocAtlas is a framework for creating high-quality OCR datasets and benchmarks across 82 languages and 9 tasks, enabling better multilingual document understanding, especially for low-resource languages.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Multilingual document understanding models perform poorly on low-resource languages due to lack of training data and reliance on model-based annotations that embed existing biases. There is a need for scalable, unbiased, and structurally rich datasets to evaluate and improve OCR and document understanding across many scripts.

Method: The authors build DocAtlas, a framework with two main data-generation pipelines: (1) differential rendering of native DOCX documents, and (2) synthetic LaTeX-based generation tailored for right-to-left scripts. These pipelines directly produce precise structural annotations—covering layout, text, and component types—encoded in a unified DocTag format, explicitly avoiding learned models for core annotation. They then evaluate 16 state-of-the-art models on the resulting benchmarks and apply Direct Preference Optimization (DPO) using rendering-based ground truth as positive signals for multilingual adaptation.

Result: Experiments on 82 languages and 9 tasks show that existing state-of-the-art models still have significant performance gaps on low-resource scripts. Applying DPO with rendering-derived ground truth yields stable multilingual adaptation: it improves in-domain accuracy by about +1.9% and out-of-domain accuracy by about +1.8% without harming performance on base languages, while standard supervised fine-tuning can hurt out-of-domain performance by up to 21%. The best configuration, DocAtlas-DeepSeek, outperforms the strongest existing baseline by +1.7%.

Conclusion: DocAtlas provides a scalable, high-fidelity, and less biased way to generate multilingual OCR datasets and benchmarks with rich structural annotations. The framework exposes persistent shortcomings of current models on low-resource languages and demonstrates that DPO-based training with rendering-derived ground truth is an effective and safer way to achieve multilingual adaptation than traditional supervised fine-tuning.

Abstract: Multilingual document understanding remains limited for low-resource languages due to scarce training data and model-based annotation pipelines that perpetuate existing biases. We introduce DocAtlas, a framework that constructs high-fidelity OCR datasets and benchmarks covering 82 languages and 9 evaluation tasks. Our dual pipelines, differential rendering of native DOCX documents and synthetic LaTeX-based generation for right-to-left scripts produce precise structural annotations in a unified DocTag format encoding layout, text, and component types, without learned models for core annotation. Evaluating 16 state-of-the-art models reveals persistent gaps in low-resource scripts. We show that Direct Preference Optimization (DPO) using rendering-derived ground truth as positive signal achieves stable multilingual adaptation, improving both in-domain (+1.9%) and out-of-domain (+1.8%) accuracy without measurable base-language degradation, where supervised fine-tuning degrades out-of-domain performance by up to 21%. Our best variant, DocAtlas-DeepSeek, improves +1.7% over the strongest baseline.

### [11] [Training LLMs with Reinforcement Learning for Intent-Aware Personalized Question Answering](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.12645)
*Maryam Amirizaniani,Benjamin Charles Germain Lee,Jevin West,Nicholas Weber*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper proposes IAP, a reinforcement learning framework that makes language models infer and use users’ implicit intent from single-turn questions to improve personalized QA, achieving ~7.5% macro-score gain over strong baselines on LaMP-QA.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing personalized QA methods typically depend on multi-turn context or detailed user profiles and do not explicitly model user intent during reasoning. This makes them less effective when only a single question is available and the model must infer the user’s latent goal from minimal information. The paper aims to address this gap by directly incorporating implicit user intent into the model’s reasoning process for better personalization in single-turn settings.

Method: The authors introduce IAP (Intent-Aware Personalization), a reinforcement learning framework. Given a single-turn question, the model first infers the user’s implicit intent and represents it using a tag-based schema. These intent tags are then integrated into the intermediate thinking steps of the model to guide generation of personalized answers. A personalized reward function evaluates answer trajectories based on how well they expose and align with the inferred user intent. RL is used to optimize the model toward trajectories that make intent explicit and yield intent-grounded responses.

Result: On the LaMP-QA benchmark and across six different language models, IAP consistently outperforms all baselines. The method yields an average macro-score improvement of about 7.5% over the strongest competing approach, demonstrating robust gains in personalized QA quality when implicit intent is modeled and optimized during training.

Conclusion: Explicitly modeling and optimizing for implicit user intent within the training objective significantly improves personalized QA, especially in single-turn scenarios where context is scarce. The IAP framework’s tag-based intent schema and RL optimization present a promising direction for future work in intent-aware personalization and could be extended to other tasks requiring user-centric reasoning and grounding.

Abstract: Effective personalized question answering (PQA) in language models requires grounding responses in the user's underlying intent, where intent refers to the implicit ``why'' behind a query beyond its explicit wording. However, existing approaches to intent-aware personalization rely on multi-turn conversational context or rich user profiles, and do not explicitly model user intent during the reasoning process. This limits their effectiveness in single-turn settings, where the user's latent goal must be inferred from minimal input and integrated into the thinking and reasoning process. To bridge this gap, we propose IAP (Intent-Aware Personalization), a reinforcement learning framework that trains models to infer implicit user intent directly from a single-turn question and incorporate it into thinking steps through a tag-based schema for generating personalized, intent-grounded answers. By optimizing intent-aware answer trajectories under a personalized reward function, IAP reinforces generation paths that make implicit user intent explicit and produce responses that better align with the user's underlying goal. Through experiments on the LaMP-QA benchmark across six models, IAP consistently outperforms all baselines, achieving an average macro-score gain of around 7.5\% over the strongest competitor, demonstrating that modeling implicit user intent within the training objective is a promising direction for PQA.

### [12] [All Circuits Lead to Rome: Rethinking Functional Anisotropy in Circuit and Sheaf Discovery for LLMs](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.12671)
*Xi Chen,Mingyu Jin,Jingcheng Niu,Yutong Yin,Jinman Zhao,Bangwei Guo,Dimitris N. Metaxas,Zhaoran Wang,Yutao Yue,Gerald Penn*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper challenges the assumption that any given behavior in an LLM is implemented by a single, canonical circuit, showing instead that multiple distinct, sparse circuits can explain the same task, and proposes a new method and theory to characterize this multiplicity.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Circuit and sheaf discovery methods in mechanistic interpretability often implicitly assume that for each function or behavior in an LLM, there exists a unique, localized mechanism (a canonical circuit) that implements it. This assumption underpins how researchers interpret and evaluate discovered circuits as "the" mechanism. However, it had not been rigorously tested whether this uniqueness holds in practice. The authors are motivated to examine whether multiple different internal mechanisms can faithfully implement the same task, and if so, what this implies for the goals and validity of mechanistic interpretability and circuit discovery.

Method: The authors introduce Overlap-Aware Sheaf Repulsion, which modifies existing circuit/sheaf discovery (CSD) objectives by adding a penalty term that discourages structural overlap between circuits found in different runs. They run this augmented objective across a range of common CSD benchmarks and methods, performing multiple discovery runs to search for diverse, low-overlap circuits or sheaves that still achieve strong task performance and satisfy typical faithfulness, sparsity, and completeness criteria. They then analyze how the number and structural diversity of discovered sheaves grows as they increase the number of runs, and examine specific ultra-sparse sheaves to test the indispensability of individual edges. Finally, they develop a theoretical model based on high-dimensional superposition to show how non-unique, low-overlap circuits naturally arise, formalizing this as the Distributive Dense Circuit Hypothesis.

Result: Empirically, the authors find that for a single LLM task, multiple structurally distinct circuits or sheaves can be discovered that are simultaneously faithful, sparse, and complete, while sharing minimal structure with each other. As they increase the number of discovery runs under the overlap penalty, they continue to identify more diverse low-overlap sheaves, and this effect is robust across several major CSD methods and benchmarks. They identify a particularly striking ultra-sparse sheaf with only three edges and show that removing any single edge does not break task performance, indicating none of the edges is individually essential. Theoretically, they demonstrate that in high-dimensional superposition settings, such non-unique and low-overlap circuit explanations emerge generically, supporting their Distributive Dense Circuit Hypothesis.

Conclusion: The paper concludes that mechanistic explanations in LLMs are fundamentally non-canonical: there is no guarantee of a unique, localized circuit implementing a given function, and multiple distinct sparse circuits can all be valid explanations. This undermines the Functional Anisotropy Hypothesis and even weaker notions of canonical or essential circuit components. As a result, the authors argue that the mechanistic interpretability community should rethink how circuit and sheaf discovery results are interpreted, validated, and used—shifting away from treating discovered circuits as "the" mechanism, and toward frameworks that explicitly account for multiplicity, distribution, and non-uniqueness of internal explanations.

Abstract: In this paper, we present empirical and theoretical evidence against a central but largely implicit assumption in circuit and sheaf discovery (CSD), which we term the Functional Anisotropy Hypothesis: the idea that functions in large language models (LLMs) are localised to a unique or near-unique internal mechanism. We show that a single LLM task can instead be supported by multiple, structurally distinct circuits or sheaves that are simultaneously faithful, sparse, and complete. To systematically uncover such competing mechanisms, we introduce Overlap-Aware Sheaf Repulsion, a method that augments the CSD objective with an explicit penalty on structural overlap across multiple discovery runs, enabling the discovery of circuits or sheaves with strong task performance but minimal shared structure across a plethora of common CSD benchmarks. We find that this phenomenon becomes increasingly pronounced as the number of discovered sheaves grows and persists robustly across major CSD methods. We further identify an ultra-sparse three-edge sheaf and show that none of its edges is individually indispensable, undermining even weakened notions of canonical or essential components. To explain these findings, we propose a Distributive Dense Circuit Hypothesis and provide a theoretical analysis demonstrating that non-unique, low-overlap circuit explanations arise naturally from high-dimensional superposition under mild assumptions. Together, our results suggest that mechanistic explanations in LLMs are inherently non-canonical and call for a rethinking of how CSD results should be interpreted and evaluated.

### [13] [Simulating Students or Sycophantic Problem Solving? On Misconception Faithfulness of LLM Simulators](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.12748)
*Heejin Do,Shashank Sonkar,Mrinmaya Sachan*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper evaluates and improves how well LLM-based simulated students maintain coherent misconceptions and update them only when given feedback that truly addresses those misconceptions.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing LLM-based student simulators are typically judged by surface similarity to real student answers, not by whether they internally behave like students with stable misconceptions during multi-turn interactions. This can mislead tutor training, since models may simply accept any corrective signal and re-solve from their own knowledge instead of acting like misconception-driven learners.

Method: The authors introduce a controlled evaluation framework centered on "misconception faithfulness" using a misconception-contrastive feedback protocol. For each problem and misconception, they compare model behavior under three feedback types: (1) targeted feedback addressing the actual misconception, (2) misaligned feedback aimed at a different but plausible misconception, and (3) generic feedback only saying the answer is wrong. They define Selective Flip Score (SFS) as the difference in answer-flip rates between targeted and control feedback conditions. They run this across seven LLMs, various datasets, and prompting setups. Then they design a post-training pipeline involving supervised fine-tuning, preference optimization, and RL with an SFS-based reward to improve misconception faithfulness.

Result: Across models from 4B to 120B parameters, SFS is near zero: simulators change their answers at high rates regardless of whether feedback is targeted, misaligned, or generic, revealing low misconception faithfulness and a sycophantic pattern of accepting any correction. Supervised fine-tuning improves SFS noticeably (up to +0.56), and RL with an SFS-aligned reward produces more stable SFS gains than generic preference optimization.

Conclusion: Misconception faithfulness is both important and difficult: standard LLM student simulators do not maintain coherent misconception-driven belief states during interaction, but this behavior can be improved by targeted training. The paper argues that future student modeling should move beyond static answer similarity toward interactive, belief-aware evaluation and training that explicitly measures how simulators respond to different types of feedback.

Abstract: Large language models (LLMs) can fluently generate student-like responses, making them attractive as simulated students for training and evaluating AI tutors and human educators. Yet such simulators are typically evaluated by output similarity to real students, not by whether they behave like students with coherent misconceptions during interaction. We introduce a controlled framework for evaluating misconception faithfulness, whether a simulator maintains a misconception-driven belief state and updates selectively when feedback addresses the underlying misconception. Central to our framework is a misconception-contrastive feedback protocol that compares targeted feedback against two controls: misaligned feedback (targeting a different but plausible misconception) and generic feedback (only identifying answer is wrong). We propose Selective Flip Score (SFS), which quantifies how much more often a simulator flips its answer under targeted feedback than under contrastive controls. Across seven LLMs (4B-120B), multiple datasets, and prompting strategies, simulators exhibit near-zero SFS, correcting their answers at similarly high rates regardless of feedback relevance. Further analyses reveal a sycophantic failure mode: models behave less like students with misconceptions but more like problem-solvers who treat any corrective signal as a cue to abandon the simulated belief and re-solve from internal knowledge. To address this, we develop a post-training pipeline spanning supervised fine-tuning (SFT), preference optimization, and reinforcement learning (RL) with an SFS-aligned reward; SFT yields notable gains up to +0.56, and SFS-aligned RL provides more consistent improvements than preference optimization. Our results establish misconception faithfulness as a challenging yet trainable property, motivating a shift from static output matching toward interactive, belief-aware student modeling.

### [14] [REALISTA: Realistic Latent Adversarial Attacks that Elicit LLM Hallucinations](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.12813)
*Buyun Liang,Jinqi Luo,Liangzu Peng,Kwan Ho Ryan Chan,Darshan Thaker,Kaleab A. Kinfu,Fengrui Tian,Hamed Hassani,René Vidal*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces REALISTA, a latent-space attack method that generates realistic, semantically equivalent adversarial prompts to elicit hallucinations in large language models.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Although LLMs perform well on many tasks, they still hallucinate. Existing adversarial prompting methods either use discrete rephrasings that are limited in search space but realistic, or continuous latent-space methods that explore rich variations but often produce prompts that are no longer coherent or semantically equivalent. There is a need for an attack that is both realistic and optimization-friendly to stress-test LLM robustness, especially for strong reasoning models in free-form settings.

Method: The authors formulate hallucination elicitation as a constrained optimization problem over prompts that must remain semantically equivalent and coherent to the original user query. They propose REALISTA, which builds an input-dependent dictionary of valid editing directions in the latent space, where each direction corresponds to a verified semantically equivalent and coherent rephrasing of the original prompt. They then optimize continuous combinations of these directions in latent space to search for prompts that maximize hallucinations while respecting the semantic constraints.

Result: Experiments show that REALISTA outperforms or matches state-of-the-art realistic attack baselines on various open-source LLMs. Importantly, it is able to successfully attack large reasoning-focused LLMs in realistic free-form response settings, where prior realistic adversarial prompting methods fail to reliably induce hallucinations.

Conclusion: REALISTA effectively bridges the gap between discrete and continuous adversarial prompt attacks by combining semantic realism with flexible latent-space optimization. This yields stronger, more realistic hallucination-eliciting attacks against LLMs, especially on advanced reasoning models, providing a more powerful tool for evaluating and improving LLM robustness.

Abstract: Large language models (LLMs) achieve strong performance across many tasks but remain vulnerable to hallucinations, motivating the need for realistic adversarial prompts that elicit such failures. We formulate hallucination elicitation as a constrained optimization problem, where the goal is to find semantically coherent adversarial prompts that are equivalent to benign user prompts. Existing methods remain limited: discrete prompt-based attacks preserve semantic equivalence and coherence but search only over a limited set of prompt variations, while continuous latent-space attacks explore a richer space but often decode into prompts that are no longer valid rephrasings. To address these limitations, we propose REALISTA, a realistic latent-space attack framework. REALISTA constructs an input-dependent dictionary of valid editing directions, each corresponding to a semantically equivalent and coherent rephrasing, and optimizes continuous combinations of these directions in latent space. This design combines the optimization flexibility of continuous attacks with the semantic realism of discrete rephrasing-based attacks. Experiments demonstrate that REALISTA achieves superior or comparable performance to state-of-the-art realistic attacks on open-source LLMs and, crucially, succeeds in attacking large reasoning models under free-form response settings, where prior realistic attacks fail. Code is available at https://github.com/Buyun-Liang/REALISTA.

</details>


### [15] [Persona-Model Collapse in Emergent Misalignment](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.12850)
*Davi Bastos Costa,Renato Vicente*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper studies how fine-tuning LLMs on narrow insecure-code data can broadly misalign them, and proposes that this happens via a 'persona-model collapse' that harms their ability to simulate coherent, distinct personas. They introduce behavioral metrics to quantify this and show strong degradation under insecure fine-tuning but not under a matched secure fine-tuning control.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Emergent misalignment—where narrowly harmful fine-tuning leads to broad, off-task misbehavior—is a concern for the safe deployment of LLMs. However, we lack clear, measurable mechanisms to understand and diagnose it. The authors aim to explain emergent misalignment in terms of how models represent personas and to design sensitive behavioral tests that can flag such misalignment early, even when not directly querying about the harmful domain.

Method: The authors hypothesize that emergent misalignment is driven by 'persona-model collapse,' a degradation in the model’s internal ability to represent and maintain consistent, differentiated personas. They test this behaviorally using persona role-play on the Moral Foundations Questionnaire. For each model and persona, they compute moral susceptibility (S) from across-persona variability in answers and moral robustness (R) from within-persona consistency across repeated samples. They evaluate four frontier models (DeepSeek-V3.1, GPT-4.1, GPT-4o, Qwen3-235B) in three variants each: base, fine-tuned to generate insecure code, and a matched control fine-tuned to generate secure code. They then compare changes in S and R across these conditions and examine unconditional response distributions.

Result: Insecure-code fine-tuning substantially increases moral susceptibility S by about 55% on average, pushing all insecure variants outside and above the band of 13 frontier models from prior benchmarks, with GPT-4o more than doubling the previous upper bound. Insecure fine-tuning also causes a 65% average decrease in moral robustness R (equivalently, a 304% increase in 1/R), indicating far less consistency when simulating the same persona. In contrast, the matched secure-code fine-tuning keeps S close to base levels and causes only a partial decrease in R. Additionally, insecure variants’ unconditional Moral Foundations Questionnaire responses become saturated near the top of the scale and lose the structured variation seen in base models and in base models role-playing explicitly toxic personas.

Conclusion: The proposed metrics S and R act as sensitive behavioral diagnostics for emergent misalignment. The experiments show that fine-tuning on insecure-code data induces large, systematic degradations in persona differentiation and stability that do not appear under matched secure-code fine-tuning, supporting the hypothesis that emergent misalignment is mediated by persona-model collapse. This offers a concrete, measurable mechanism and a practical tool for monitoring and mitigating misalignment in fine-tuned LLMs.

Abstract: Fine-tuning large language models on narrow data with harmful content produces broadly misaligned behavior on unrelated prompts, a phenomenon known as emergent misalignment. We propose that emergent misalignment involves persona-model collapse: deterioration of the model's internal capacity to simulate, differentiate, and maintain consistent characters. We test this hypothesis behaviorally using two metrics: moral susceptibility (S) and moral robustness (R), computed from the across- and within-persona variability of models' Moral Foundations Questionnaire responses under persona role-play. These metrics formalize the model's ability to differentiate characters (S) and its consistency when simulating a given one (R). We evaluate four frontier models (DeepSeek-V3.1, GPT-4.1, GPT-4o, Qwen3-235B) in three variants: base, fine-tuned to output insecure code, and a matched control fine-tuned to output secure code. Across the four models, insecure fine-tuning produces an average $55\%$ increase in S, pushing all four insecure variants beyond the band observed across 13 frontier models benchmarked in prior work -- with GPT-4o reaching more than twice the band's upper end -- signaling dysregulated differentiation. It also causes an average $65\%$ decrease in R, equivalent to a $304\%$ increase in 1/R. By contrast, the matched secure control preserves S near the base and induces only a partial R loss, showing that these effects are largely misalignment-specific. Complementing these metric shifts, insecure variants' unconditioned responses converge toward saturation near the scale ceiling, departing markedly from both base models' structured responses and those elicited when base models role-play toxic personas. Taken together, these metrics provide a sensitive diagnostic for emergent misalignment and serve as behavioral evidence that it involves persona-model collapse.

### [16] [CiteVQA: Benchmarking Evidence Attribution for Trustworthy Document Intelligence](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.12882)
*Dongsheng Ma,Jiayu Li,Zhengren Wang,Yijie Wang,Jiahao Kong,Weijun Zeng,Jutao Xiao,Jie Yang,Wentao Zhang,Bin Wang,Conghui He*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces CiteVQA, a benchmark for document question answering that jointly evaluates both answer correctness and the correctness of cited evidence regions, exposing reliability gaps hidden by answer-only metrics.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing Doc-VQA evaluations only score the final answer, ignoring whether the model’s cited evidence truly supports that answer. This allows models to appear accurate even when they ground answers in incorrect document regions, which is dangerous in high-stakes domains where decisions must be properly sourced and auditable.

Method: The authors build CiteVQA, a benchmark of 1,897 questions over 711 multi-page PDFs in seven domains and two languages. Each question is annotated with element-level bounding-box citations. Ground-truth evidence is produced via an automated masking-ablation pipeline to identify crucial text regions and then validated by experts. They define Strict Attributed Accuracy (SAA), which counts a prediction as correct only if both the answer and the cited region match ground truth, and apply this to audit 20 MLLMs.

Result: Evaluation of 20 multimodal LLMs shows widespread Attribution Hallucination: models often give correct answers but point to wrong evidence. Even the best system (Gemini-3.1-Pro-Preview) attains only 76.0 SAA, and the strongest open-source MLLM reaches 22.5 SAA, far below their answer-only performance. This demonstrates that current systems are much less reliable when judged on both accuracy and attribution.

Conclusion: CiteVQA provides a rigorous, scalable benchmark for evaluating both answers and their cited evidence in document QA, revealing a substantial reliability gap that answer-only metrics conceal. The work argues that trustworthy document intelligence requires such attribution-aware evaluation, and it offers an open repository to support future research in this direction.

Abstract: Multimodal Large Language Models (MLLMs) have significantly advanced document understanding, yet current Doc-VQA evaluations score only the final answer and leave the supporting evidence unchecked. This answer-only approach masks a critical failure mode: a model can land on the correct answer while grounding it in the wrong passage -- a critical risk in high-stakes domains like law, finance, and medicine, where every conclusion must be traceable to a specific source region. To address this, we introduce CiteVQA, a benchmark that requires models to return element-level bounding-box citations alongside each answer, evaluating both jointly. CiteVQA comprises 1,897 questions across 711 PDFs spanning seven domains and two languages, averaging 40.6 pages per document. To ensure fidelity and scalability, the ground-truth citations are generated by an automated pipeline-which identifies crucial evidence via masking ablation-and are subsequently validated through expert review. At the core of our evaluation is Strict Attributed Accuracy (SAA), which credits a prediction only when the answer and the cited region are both correct. Auditing 20 MLLMs reveals a pervasive Attribution Hallucination: models frequently produce the right answer while citing the wrong region. The strongest system (Gemini-3.1-Pro-Preview) achieves an SAA of only 76.0, and the strongest open-source MLLM reaches just 22.5. Ultimately, towards trustworthy document intelligence, CiteVQA exposes a reliability gap that answer-only evaluations overlook, providing the instrumentation needed to close it. Our repository is available at https://github.com/opendatalab/CiteVQA.

### [17] [CommonWhy: A Dataset for Evaluating Entity-Based Causal Commonsense Reasoning in Large Language Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.12918)

Conclusion: CommonWhy establishes a new KGQA evaluation paradigm that emphasizes causal commonsense reasoning rather than simple fact retrieval. Abstract: To effectively interact with the real world, Large Language Models (LLMs) require entity-based commonsense reasoning, a challenging task that necessitates integrating factual knowledge about specific entities with commonsense inference. Existing datasets for evaluating LLM entity-based commonsense reasoning have largely focused on True/False or multiple-choice questions, leaving the explicit assessment of the model's ability in abductive reasoning about causes and effects and generating explanations largely unexamined. In this work, we introduce CommonWhy, a dataset of 15,000 why questions designed to evaluate entity-based commonsense reasoning about causal relationships in LLMs. CommonWhy also serves as a Knowledge Graph Question Answering (KGQA) benchmark, as all supporting knowledge required to answer its queries is available in the Wikidata knowledge graph. Unlike existing KGQA datasets, which primarily test fact retrieval, CommonWhy targets causal commonsense reasoning, establishing a new paradigm for KGQA evaluation. ### [19] [DiM\textsuperscript{3}: Bridging Multilingual and Multimodal Models via Direction- and Magnitude-Aware Merging](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.12960)
*Zijing Wang,Mingyang Wang,Ercong Nie,Yongkang Liu,Shi Feng,Mengjie Zhao,Daling Wang,Xiaocui Yang,Hinrich Schütze*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: Proposes DiM3, a training-free method to inject multilingual ability into existing multimodal LLMs by smartly merging residual parameter updates, improving multilingual performance without full retraining.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing multimodal large language models are usually trained for a single or limited set of languages. Extending them to many languages generally requires expensive multilingual multimodal data collection and costly end-to-end retraining. The authors want a cheaper, training-free way to add multilingual capability to strong vision-language models while preserving their multimodal skills.

Method: They take an existing multimodal model with a shared language backbone and two separately trained residual update sets: one for multilingual text and one for multimodal capability. They introduce Direction- and Magnitude-aware Multilingual Multimodal merging (DiM3), which, at each parameter dimension, selectively composes the multilingual and multimodal residual updates based on both their directions and magnitudes. This merging is done only in the language model backbone while keeping the vision encoder and multimodal projector unchanged. The method can be applied on top of existing multilingual multimodal models as well, to further refine their parameters without additional training.

Result: On multilingual benchmarks (text-only and vision-language) across 57 languages, and using both LLaVA- and Qwen-based backbones, DiM3 consistently beats other parameter-merging baselines. It significantly boosts multilingual performance compared to the original multimodal models and achieves performance close to fully fine-tuned multilingual multimodal models, while largely preserving the models’ general multimodal capabilities. Applying DiM3 on already multilingual multimodal models further improves their performance. Interpretability analyses indicate that DiM3 mostly alters intermediate semantic representations to enhance cross-lingual alignment, while keeping higher-level task-specific structures intact.

Conclusion: DiM3 is an effective, training-free approach to inject or enhance multilingual capabilities in multimodal LLMs by carefully merging heterogeneous residual updates. It offers a practical alternative to expensive multilingual multimodal retraining, scaling to many languages, maintaining strong multimodal ability, and improving cross-lingual semantic alignment. The approach is flexible enough to further refine even already multilingual multimodal models.

Abstract: Towards more general and human-like intelligence, large language models should seamlessly integrate both multilingual and multimodal capabilities; however, extending an existing multimodal model to many languages typically requires expensive multilingual multimodal data construction and repeated end-to-end retraining. We study a training-free alternative: injecting multilingual capability into an existing multimodal model by composing residual updates in the shared language model backbone. The key challenge is that multilingual and multimodal updates are heterogeneous, reflecting different functional roles in the shared model. To address this, we propose Direction- and Magnitude-aware Multilingual Multimodal merging (DiM3), which selectively composes the two updates at each parameter dimension while preserving the original vision encoder and multimodal projector. Experiments on multilingual benchmarks in both text-only and vision-language settings, covering 57 languages across LLaVA- and Qwen-based backbones, show that DiM3 consistently outperforms existing merging baselines, substantially improves multilingual performance over the original multimodal model, and remains competitive with dedicated multilingual multimodal fine-tuning while largely retaining general multimodal ability. We further show that DiM3 can be directly applied to already trained multilingual multimodal models and still yield additional gains. Further interpretability analysis shows that DiM3 primarily reshapes intermediate-layer semantic representations, strengthening cross-lingual alignment under both text-only and multimodal inputs while preserving higher-layer task-sensitive structure. Our repository is on https://github.com/wzj1718/DiM3.

</details>


### [20] [Leveraging Speech to Identify Signatures of Insight and Transfer in Problem Solving](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.12970)
*Linas Nasvytis,Judith E. Fan*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: Study on how sudden insights in problem solving become transferable to similar tasks.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: To understand the nature of sudden problem-solving insights and how they influence future problem-solving strategies on similar tasks.

Method: 189 participants solved five matchstick-arithmetic problems while thinking aloud. One group saw problems requiring the same type of non-obvious solution (Same group), while another group saw problems each requiring a different type (Different group). Verbal protocols were analyzed for changes in speech amount and content over time.

Result: Participants in the Same group improved more rapidly than those in the Different group. As Same-group participants improved, they spoke more and their talk shifted: they more often spontaneously categorized the current problem type.

Conclusion: Transferable insights appear to be characterized by their increasing accessibility to verbal report, even if people still struggle to articulate the less conscious precursors of the insight.

Abstract: Many problems seem to require a flash of insight to solve. What form do these sudden insights take, and what impact do they have on how people approach similar problems in the future? In this work, we prompted participants (N = 189) to talk aloud as they attempted to solve a sequence of five "matchstick-arithmetic" problems. These problems either all relied on the same kind of non-obvious solution (Same group) or a different kind each time (Different group). We found that Same participants improved more rapidly than Different participants, and as they improved, they talked more and talked about different things when solving later problems. Specifically, they were more likely to spontaneously categorize the problem they were working on. Taken together, these findings suggest that a hallmark of transferable insights is their accessibility for verbal report, even if the underlying precursors of insight remain difficult to articulate.

### [21] [Leveraging Multimodal Self-Consistency Reasoning in Coding Motivational Interviewing for Alcohol Use Reduction](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.12987)
*Guangzeng Han,James G. Murphy,Benjamin O. Ladd,Xiaolei Huang,Brian Borsari*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper proposes an automated way to code Motivational Interviewing (MI) sessions from raw audio using audio-language models and multiple reasoning prompts combined via self-consistency voting, outperforming single-pass baselines.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Manual MI coding is critical but very time- and labor-intensive and requires trained experts. Existing automated approaches often rely only on text, missing important acoustic/prosodic information and typically use single-pass prompting, which can be brittle. With the advent of powerful audio-language models, there is an opportunity to leverage both speech content and prosody and to improve robustness by aggregating multiple reasoning trajectories.

Method: The authors use audio-language models on five de-identified recorded MI sessions. For each client utterance, they design four complementary prompts: (1) analytic prompting to focus on verbal content, (2) prosody-aware prompting to capture acoustic and prosodic cues, (3) evidence-scoring prompting to support quantitative hypothesis testing, and (4) comparative prompting for contrastive reasoning between categories. For each prompt type they sample three stochastic outputs, yielding 12 independent reasoning trajectories per utterance. Final MI codes are assigned by majority vote across trajectories. They perform ablation studies by removing individual modules to test contribution.

Result: The multimodal self-consistency system achieves 52.56% accuracy, 54.03% precision, 47.45% recall, and 46.40% macro-F1, outperforming baseline single-pass prompting approaches. Ablation studies show that removing any of the specialized prompting modules reduces performance on key metrics, indicating each component contributes to the overall improvement.

Conclusion: Aggregating multiple stochastic reasoning trajectories from audio-language models, while explicitly leveraging both verbal and prosodic cues, improves the reliability of automatic MI coding compared with single-pass prompting. This suggests that capturing both what is said and how it is said is important for robust, automated behavioral coding in MI, and potentially other psychotherapy or counseling contexts.

Abstract: BACKGROUND: Coding Motivational Interviewing (MI) sessions is essential for understanding client behaviors and predicting outcomes, but it requires substantial time and labor from trained MI professionals. Recent advances in audio-language models (ALMs) offer new opportunities to automate MI coding by capturing multimodal behavioral signals. OBJECTIVE: This study aims to develop an automatic MI coding approach based on ALMs that analyzes raw audio input and integrates predictions from multiple reasoning trajectories using self-consistency to improve coding robustness. METHODS: We experimented with five recorded sessions from de-identified MI audio tapes. We deployed ALMs with four complementary analytic prompts to support utterance-level reasoning: analytic prompting for verbal cues, prosody-aware prompting for acoustic cues, evidence-scoring prompting for quantitative hypothesis testing, and comparative prompting for contrastive reasoning. Three stochastic samples were drawn for each prompt, generating 12 independent reasoning trajectories per utterance. Final predictions were determined by majority voting across all trajectories. RESULTS: Performance was evaluated using accuracy, precision, recall, and macro-F1 scores. The proposed multimodal self-consistency approach achieved 52.56% accuracy, 54.03% precision, 47.45% recall, and a macro-F1 score of 46.40%, exceeding baseline methods. Systematic ablation experiments that removed individual modules consistently degraded performance on the primary metrics. CONCLUSIONS: Multimodal self-consistency outperforms single-pass baseline prompting approaches for MI coding. These findings suggest that incorporating both what clients say and how they say it can support more reliable automatic MI coding.
