### [50] [Many-Shot CoT-ICL: Making In-Context Learning Truly Learn](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.13511)
*Tsz Ting Chung,Lemao Liu,Mo Yu,Dit-Yan Yeung*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper analyzes how many-shot chain-of-thought in-context learning (CoT-ICL) scales for reasoning tasks and proposes a curriculum-style demonstration ordering method to stabilize and improve performance.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: While long-context LLMs enable many-shot in-context learning that can rival fine-tuning, most scaling insights come from non-reasoning tasks. For reasoning tasks that use chain-of-thought prompts, empirical rules for many-shot ICL (e.g., more similar examples, more examples is better) do not appear to hold, and the mechanisms behind success or failure are poorly understood. The authors aim to systematically study how many-shot CoT-ICL behaves on reasoning tasks and why.

Method: The authors empirically study many-shot CoT-ICL across both non-reasoning and reasoning-oriented LLMs and tasks. They vary the number of CoT demonstrations, test similarity-based retrieval vs. other selection strategies, and analyze performance variance as the number and ordering of demonstrations change. They interpret the behavior through a test-time learning lens and derive principles for constructing effective CoT demonstration sets. Based on these principles, they introduce Curvilinear Demonstration Selection (CDS), an ordering method that arranges demonstrations from easier/more understandable to harder, forming a smooth conceptual progression.

Result: (i) Increasing the number of CoT demonstrations has a setting-dependent effect: it is unstable and often harmful for non-reasoning LLMs but mostly beneficial for reasoning-oriented LLMs. (ii) Similarity-based retrieval, which works well for non-reasoning tasks, does not help for reasoning tasks because semantic similarity does not align well with compatibility of procedural reasoning steps. (iii) As more CoT demonstrations are added, performance variance increases, revealing an order-scaling effect where the order of examples increasingly matters. Applying CDS to geometry reasoning with 64 demonstrations yields up to a 5.42 percentage-point accuracy improvement, demonstrating the benefits of structured ordering.

Conclusion: Many-shot CoT-ICL for reasoning behaves differently from standard many-shot ICL on non-reasoning tasks, and naive strategies like “more similar examples” and arbitrary ordering can fail or even destabilize performance. Viewing many-shot CoT-ICL as in-context test-time learning suggests that demonstrations should be chosen for understandability and ordered as a curriculum that gradually increases conceptual difficulty. The proposed CDS method leverages this perspective, improving reasoning performance and reframing long contexts as opportunities to design structured in-context curricula rather than simple retrieval buffers.

Abstract: In-context learning (ICL) adapts large language models (LLMs) to new tasks by conditioning on demonstrations in the prompt without parameter updates. With long-context models, many-shot ICL can use dozens to hundreds of examples and achieve performance comparable to fine-tuning, yet current understanding of its scaling behavior is largely derived from non-reasoning tasks. We study many-shot chain-of-thought in-context learning (CoT-ICL) for reasoning and show that standard many-shot rules do not transfer. Across non-reasoning and reasoning-oriented LLMs and across non-reasoning and reasoning tasks, we find: (i) a setting-dependent scaling effect, where increasing the number of CoT demonstrations is unstable for non-reasoning LLMs and benefits mainly reasoning-oriented LLMs; (ii) similarity-based retrieval helps on non-reasoning tasks but fails on reasoning, since semantic similarity poorly predicts procedural (i.e., CoT) compatibility; and (iii) an order-scaling effect, where performance variance grows with more CoT demonstrations. We interpret these behaviors by viewing many-shot CoT-ICL as in-context test-time learning rather than scaled pattern matching, and suggests two principles: (i) demonstrations should be easy for the target model to understand, and (ii) they should be ordered to support a smooth conceptual progression. Guided by the principle, we propose Curvilinear Demonstration Selection (CDS), a simple ordering method that yields up to a 5.42 percentage-point gain on geometry with 64 demonstrations. Overall, our results reframe the long context window from a retrieval buffer into a structured curriculum for in-context test-time learning.

</details>


### [51] [Locale-Conditioned Few-Shot Prompting Mitigates Demonstration Regurgitation in On-Device PII Substitution with Small Language Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.13538)
*Anuj Sadani,Deepak Kumar*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper proposes an on-device PII-redaction pipeline that replaces sensitive entities with realistic, consistent surrogates instead of generic placeholders, and finds that while this improves text naturalness and utility for some tasks, it can hurt NER training performance compared to simpler random faker-style surrogates.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: PII redaction methods that replace entities with generic tags like [PERSON] protect privacy but destroy much of the text’s usefulness for retrieval and for training NER models, because they remove structure, variety, and contextual cues. The authors want a redaction method that both preserves privacy and keeps the text useful, particularly in fully on-device settings where data cannot leave the device.

Method: They build a multi-component on-device pipeline: (1) a 1.5B-parameter mixture-of-experts token classifier (openai/privacy-filter) detects PII spans; (2) a heavily quantized 1-bit Bonsai-1.7B small language model generates contextual surrogate values for names, addresses, and dates; and (3) a rule-based faker-like generator creates surrogates for more patterned fields. During development they discover that naive fixed three-shot prompting makes the SLM copy the demonstrations verbatim, independent of input; this behavior persists in a 1.58-bit ternary version, so they conclude it is a prompting issue, not a quantization artifact. They remedy it with a locale-conditioned rotating few-shot scheme: a character-range heuristic infers locale, selects a locale-pure demonstration pool, and an MD5 hash of each input samples three different demonstrations, thus reducing overfitting to any fixed examples. They then evaluate the pipeline on multilingual corpora for perplexity and length preservation and on downstream NER performance, comparing their hybrid method against pure faker and unredacted text.

Result: The rotating few-shot strategy eliminates verbatim echoing in 482/482 tested calls and yields locale-appropriate surrogates, though the SLM still overuses patterns from a limited, same-locale demonstration pool. On a 2000-document multilingual dataset, their hybrid perplexity is lower (better) than faker in all six evaluated locales under XGLM-564M and achieves best-of-three length preservation in four locales. For downstream English NER (400 train / 100 test), using simple redact tags yields F1=0.000, faker achieves 0.656, and unredacted original text reaches 0.960. On a smaller matched subset including hybrid (160/40), faker (0.506) significantly outperforms hybrid (0.346) with p < 0.001, despite hybrid text being more natural.

Conclusion: The on-device hybrid pipeline can generate realistic, locale-correct PII surrogates and improves surface-level text quality (perplexity, length preservation) over rule-based faker. However, this added naturalness does not translate into better NER training performance; in fact, the more structured and less diverse SLM surrogates hurt NER compared with simpler, more varied faker-generated surrogates. The authors frame this as a negative but informative result: for NER, diversity of training examples matters more than naturalness of redacted text, and prompt design is crucial to avoid pathological copying behaviors in heavily quantized SLMs.

Abstract: Personally Identifiable Information (PII) redaction usually replaces detected entities with placeholder tokens such as [PERSON], destroying the downstream utility of the redacted text for retrieval and Named Entity Recognition (NER) training. We propose a fully on-device pipeline that substitutes PII with consistent, type-preserving fake values: a 1.5 B mixture-of-experts token classifier (openai/privacy-filter) detects spans, a 1-bit Bonsai-1.7B Small Language Model (SLM) proposes contextual surrogates for names, addresses, and dates, and a rule-based generator (faker) handles patterned fields. We report a prompting finding more important than the quantization choice: with naive fixed three-shot demonstrations, the 1-bit SLM regurgitates demonstration outputs verbatim regardless of input; 1.58-bit Ternary-Bonsai-1.7B reproduces byte-identical failures, ruling out quantization as the cause. We fix this with locale-conditioned rotating few-shot demonstrations: a character-range heuristic picks a locale-pure pool and a per-input MD5 hash samples three demonstrations. With the fix, 482/482 unique Bonsai-1.7B calls succeed (no echoes) and produce locale-correct surrogates, although the SLM still copies from a small same-locale demonstration pool - a residual narrowness we quantify. On a 2000-document multilingual corpus, hybrid perplexity (PPL) beats faker in all six locales under a multilingual evaluator (XGLM-564M); length preservation is best-of-three in 4 of 6 locales. On downstream NER (400 train / 100 test, English), redact yields F1=0.000, faker 0.656, original 0.960; on a matched 160/40 subset including hybrid, faker (0.506) outperforms hybrid (0.346) at p < 0.001. We report this as an honest negative finding: SLM surrogates produce more natural text but a less varied training distribution, and downstream NER benefits more from variety than from naturalness.

### [52] [Inducing Artificial Uncertainty in Language Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.13595)
*Sophia Hager,Simon Zeng,Nicholas Andrews*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: Paper proposes inducing artificial uncertainty in language models to train better uncertainty quantification probes when challenging supervised data is scarce.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Safety-critical applications require language models whose confidence estimates (probabilities) are trustworthy. Many current uncertainty quantification methods rely on supervised data drawn from sufficiently hard or unfamiliar cases, but such data is increasingly scarce as large language models are trained on most available text. Without enough genuinely uncertain examples, models and probes tend to become overconfident on new, unfamiliar data. Thus, there is a need for a way to generate or simulate uncertainty for training, especially when real hard examples are unavailable.

Method: The authors define the problem of inducing artificial uncertainty in language models and develop techniques that cause a language model to appear uncertain even on trivially easy inputs. They then train probing models (probes) on the language model’s internal states or outputs, with supervision derived from whether artificial uncertainty has been induced. These probes learn to recognize patterns associated with uncertainty. The key experimental comparison is between probes trained on data with artificial uncertainty versus probes trained on standard data without artificial uncertainty.

Result: Probes trained on datasets that include artificially induced uncertainty are better at detecting genuine uncertainty in the original model: they are more calibrated and accurate on naturally hard or unfamiliar examples. Specifically, these probes obtain notably higher calibration on hard data, while preserving almost the same performance on easy data, relative to probes that never saw artificially uncertain cases during training.

Conclusion: Inducing artificial uncertainty during training is an effective strategy for improving uncertainty estimation in language models when truly challenging labeled data is scarce. This approach yields probes that better recognize and calibrate real uncertainty, offering a practical path for building safer and more reliable language models in safety-critical settings as data saturation increases.

Abstract: In safety-critical applications, language models should be able to characterize their uncertainty with meaningful probabilities. Many uncertainty quantification approaches require supervised data; however, finding suitable unseen challenging data is increasingly difficult for large language models trained on vast amounts of scraped data. If the model is consistently (and correctly) confident in its predictions, the uncertainty quantification method may consistently overestimate confidence on new and unfamiliar data. Finding data which exhibits enough uncertainty to train supervised uncertainty quantification methods for high-performance models may therefore be challenging, and will increase in difficulty as LLMs saturate datasets. To address this issue, we first introduce the problem of inducing artificial uncertainty in language models, then investigate methods of inducing artificial uncertainty on trivially easy data in the absence of challenging data at training time. We use probes trained to recognize artificial uncertainty on the original model, and find that these probes trained on artificial uncertainty outperform probes trained without artificial uncertainty in recognizing real uncertainty, achieving notably higher calibration on hard data with minimal loss of performance on easy data.

Abstract: This article investigates the performance of automatic evaluation metrics (AEMs) and LLM-as-a-judge evaluation on literary translation across multiple languages, genres, and translation modalities. The aim is to assess how well these tools align with professionals when evaluating translation, creativity (creative shifts & errors), and see if they can substitute laborious manual annotations. Moreover, performance is consistently worse for more literary genres such as poetry. This highlights fundamental limitations of current automatic evaluation tools for literary translation and the need to create new tools that do not frequently consider out of routine translations as errors.

*Takumi Goto,Yusuke Sakai,Taro Watanabe*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: A training-free inference method for grammatical error correction that uses edit-level majority voting over multiple LLM outputs to reduce over-correction and improve robustness across languages and prompts.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing large language model-based grammatical error correction systems tend to over-correct, changing text that is already grammatical or altering meaning. Many strong decoding strategies require model changes, extra training, or are sensitive to prompts, making them less practical.

Method: At inference time, the method samples multiple candidate corrections from a single LLM using standard decoding. Instead of choosing one whole-sentence candidate (as in greedy or MBR decoding), it aligns candidates with the source and conducts majority voting at the level of individual edits (insertions, deletions, substitutions). The final corrected text is built from the edits that receive majority support. This requires no fine-tuning or architectural modifications and can be applied to different languages and prompts.

Result: On nine grammatical error correction benchmarks spanning English, Czech, German, Ukrainian, Korean, Hindi, and Romanian, the proposed majority-vote edit aggregation outperforms greedy decoding and minimum Bayes risk (MBR) decoding in most evaluations. It also shows more consistent performance across different instruction prompts compared to baseline decoding strategies.

Conclusion: Edit-level majority voting over multiple LLM-generated candidates is an effective, training-free strategy to reduce over-correction and improve grammatical error correction quality and robustness across languages and prompts. The released repositories further support practical adoption by simplifying dataset loading and LLM-based GEC experimentation.

Abstract: Grammatical error correction using large language models often suffers from the over-correction issue. To mitigate this, we propose a training-free inference method that performs edit-level majority voting over multiple candidates generated by a single model, without requiring model modifications or additional training. Across nine benchmarks covering English, Czech, German, Ukrainian, Korean, Hindi, and Romanian, the proposed method outperforms both greedy and MBR decoding in most cases. Moreover, it yields stable correction quality regardless of the instruction prompts used. We release two repository supporting GEC datasets loading and LLM inference.

### [55] [Prefix Teach, Suffix Fade: Local Teachability Collapse in Strong-to-Weak On-Policy Distillation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.13643)
*Kaiyuan Liu,Ziyuan Zhuang,Yang Bai,Bing Wang,Rongxiang Weng,Jieping Ye*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper identifies and addresses a failure mode in on-policy distillation (OPD) called local teachability collapse, proposing a trajectory-specific rule that truncates teacher supervision when it ceases to be discriminative, leading to better strong-to-weak distillation performance and out-of-domain generalization.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: On-policy distillation from a strong teacher to a weaker student typically assumes that more dense supervision over an entire generated trajectory is always beneficial as long as the teacher is better. However, in practice this assumption can break down: later parts of a trajectory may still be better under the teacher but provide weak or uninformative learning signals for the student. This leads to inefficient or even harmful training. The authors aim to understand and fix this issue to make strong-to-weak OPD more effective and robust, especially for preserving general capabilities.

Method: The authors analyze OPD trajectories and identify regions where the teacher's advantage over the student remains non-zero but becomes locally non-discriminative (“local teachability collapse”). They propose a trajectory-specific release rule for deciding how much of each generated response should receive dense supervision. The rule: (1) computes the teacher's margin over the student's top-K candidate tokens; (2) aggregates these margins at the level of NLTK-tokenized sentence segments; and (3) applies a BIC-style change-point detection to find a downward shift in the margin signal. Once such a change point is detected, dense supervision is truncated for the rest of the trajectory. The method is evaluated on strong-to-weak distillation tasks using the Qwen3 model family, comparing against standard full-trajectory OPD and other baselines.

Result: Across strong-to-weak distillation experiments on the Qwen3 model family, the proposed release rule consistently outperforms standard full-trajectory OPD on five in-domain benchmarks and across multiple student sizes. Additionally, the method better preserves out-of-domain performance relative to baseline distillation schemes, indicating that avoiding low-utility supervision helps maintain broader capabilities while still improving in-domain performance.

Conclusion: Dense supervision in strong-to-weak on-policy distillation is not always beneficial across an entire generated trajectory, even when a teacher is stronger overall. The key factor is the local utility and discriminativeness of teacher feedback, not just its availability. By detecting and truncating supervision at points where local teachability collapses, the proposed release rule improves both in-domain distillation quality and out-of-domain generalization. Effective OPD therefore requires trajectory-aware control of where and how long teacher feedback is applied.

Abstract: On-policy distillation (OPD) trains a student model on its own rollouts using dense feedback from a stronger teacher. Prior literature suggests that, provided teacher feedback is available, supervising the full sequence of response tokens should monotonically improve performance. However, we demonstrate that this assumption sometimes fails to hold in strong-to-weak OPD settings. While later segments of a generated trajectory may still exhibit a non-zero teacher-student advantage, they frequently lack the local contrast that makes dense feedback effective for prioritizing student learning. We term this failure mode local teachability collapse. The resulting principle is straightforward: supervision should concentrate on trajectory regions where the teacher's feedback remains discriminative, rather than uniformly covering the entire response. We operationalize this principle through a trajectory-specific release rule. This rule measures the teacher's margin over the student's top-$K$ candidate set, aggregates this margin across NLTK-tokenized sentence segments, and truncates dense OPD supervision upon detecting a BIC-style downward change point. Experimental results across strong-to-weak distillation tasks using the Qwen3 model family indicate that this release rule consistently outperforms standard full-trajectory OPD across five in-domain benchmarks at various student scales. Furthermore, compared to baseline distillation methods, our approach better preserves model capabilities on out-of-domain task. These results suggest that effective strong-to-weak OPD requires evaluating not only the availability of teacher guidance but also its local utility, ensuring that the generated feedback remains teachable.

### [56] [FlowCompile: An Optimizing Compiler for Structured LLM Workflows](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.13647)
*Junyan Li,Zhang-Wei Hong,Maohao Shen,Yang Zhang,Chuang Gan*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: FlowCompile is a compiler-style optimizer for structured LLM workflows that finds reusable configurations balancing accuracy and latency.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Structured LLM workflows use multiple specialized sub-agents arranged in a graph, but picking models, budgets, and structures for each sub-agent to balance accuracy and latency is hard due to a huge combinatorial design space. Existing methods mostly do per-query routing given a single objective and miss the opportunity to globally pre-optimize workflows for diverse trade-offs.

Method: FlowCompile treats workflow optimization as a compile-time design space exploration problem. It decomposes a workflow into sub-agents, profiles each under many configurations (e.g., models, reasoning budgets), and uses a structure-aware proxy to compose these measurements into estimates of workflow-level accuracy and latency. It then performs a single-pass search to identify a diverse, high-quality set of workflow configurations that span the accuracy-latency Pareto front, without retraining or online adaptation.

Result: Across multiple workflows and challenging benchmarks, FlowCompile outperforms heuristic workflow designs and routing-based baselines, achieving up to 6.4x speedup while maintaining accuracy. The resulting set of compiled configurations is reusable and supports flexible deployment choices and downstream routing or selection.

Conclusion: Viewing structured LLM workflow optimization as a compilation problem enables efficient, global exploration of the design space. FlowCompile can precompute a set of strong workflow configurations that offer superior accuracy-latency trade-offs compared to routing-based methods and heuristics, and these configurations remain useful across changing runtime preferences.

Abstract: Structured LLM workflows, where specialized LLM sub-agents execute according to a predefined graph, have become a powerful abstraction for solving complex tasks. Optimizing such workflows, i.e., selecting configurations for each sub-agent to balance accuracy and latency, is challenging due to the combinatorial design space over model choices, reasoning budgets, and workflow structures. Existing cost-aware methods largely treat workflow optimization as a routing problem, selecting a configuration at inference time for each query according to the accuracy-latency objective used during training. We argue that structured LLM workflows can also be optimized from a compilation perspective: before deployment, the system can globally explore the workflow design space and construct a reusable set of workflow-level configurations spanning diverse accuracy-latency trade-offs. Drawing inspiration from machine learning compilers, we introduce FlowCompile, a structured LLM workflow compiler that performs compile-time design space exploration to identify a high-quality, reusable trade-off set. FlowCompile decomposes a workflow into sub-agents, profiles each sub-agent under diverse configurations, and composes these measurements through a structure-aware proxy to estimate workflow-level accuracy and latency. It then identifies diverse high-quality configurations in a single compile-time pass, without retraining or online adaptation. Experiments across diverse workflows and challenging benchmarks show that FlowCompile consistently outperforms heuristically optimized workflow configurations and routing-based baselines, delivering up to 6.4x speedup. The compiled configuration set further serves as a reusable optimization artifact, enabling flexible deployment under varying runtime preferences and supporting downstream selection or routing.

Abstract: Propaganda detection in social media is challenging due to noisy, short texts and low annotation agreements. Our results show that fine-tuning is essential, since it transforms weak zero-shot baselines into competitive systems and reveals methodological differences that are hidden using base models. Across schemas, the Qwen models achieve the strongest overall performance, and Phi-4 14B consistently outperforms GPT-4.1-nano. Our hierarchical prompting method (HiPP), which predicts fine-grained techniques before aggregating them, is especially beneficial after fine-tuning and on the more ambiguous, low-agreement taxonomy, while remaining competitive on the simpler schema. ### [58] [RTLC -- Research, Teach-to-Learn, Critique: A three-stage prompting paradigm inspired by the Feynman Learning Technique that lifts LLM-as-judge accuracy on JudgeBench with no fine-tuning](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.13695)
*Andrea Morandi*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper proposes RTLC, a three-stage prompting method that turns a single black-box LLM into a more accurate “LLM-as-a-judge” without extra training or tools, significantly boosting objective-correctness accuracy on JudgeBench.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing LLM-as-a-judge setups are the default for evaluating open-ended generations, but even strong instruction-tuned models perform only slightly better than random on hard, objective-correctness pairwise benchmarks like JudgeBench. The authors aim to improve the reliability and accuracy of LLM judges without needing fine-tuning, retrieval, or external tools.

Method: They design RTLC, a three-stage prompting recipe: (1) Research: wrap the input in a fixed pedagogical scaffold inspired by the Feynman Learning Technique—study, explain (teach), identify gaps, and simplify—to force deeper processing; (2) Teach-to-Learn: sample N=10 independent candidate verdicts at temperature 0.4 to approximate an internal ensemble; (3) Critique: have the model itself cross-compare these 10 candidates against the original question and output one final verdict at temperature 0, effectively acting as its own critic. They run ablations isolating the contributions of the scaffold, multi-sampling, and explicit critique.

Result: On the JudgeBench-GPT subset of 350 difficult pairwise items, Claude 3.7 Sonnet’s pairwise accuracy improves from 64.6% with a vanilla single-shot prompt to 78.6% with RTLC critique-of-10, a 14.0 percentage point gain. RTLC outperforms N=10 self-consistency majority voting (77.7%) and a zero-shot first candidate baseline (74.0%). Ablations show +9.4 pp from the Teach-to-Learn scaffold, +3.7 pp from N=10 marginalization, and +0.9 pp from explicit critique. They also analyze cost-accuracy tradeoffs, error distribution across knowledge, reasoning, math, and coding categories, and show RTLC composes multiplicatively with post-hoc judge-score calibration.

Conclusion: RTLC is an effective, training-free method to upgrade LLM-as-a-judge performance by turning a single black-box model into an ensemble-of-thought judge via carefully structured prompting, moderate sampling, and self-critique. It consistently improves accuracy over standard baselines and self-consistency across tasks and operates on a favorable cost-accuracy frontier while being compatible with other calibration techniques.

Abstract: LLM-as-a-judge is now the default measurement instrument for open-ended generation, but on the public JudgeBench benchmark even strong instruction-tuned judges barely scrape past random on objective-correctness pairwise items. We introduce RTLC, a three-stage prompting recipe -- Research, Teach-to-Learn, Critique -- that promotes a single black-box LLM into an ensemble-of-thought judge with no fine-tuning, retrieval, or external tools. Stage 1 wraps the input in a fixed pedagogical scaffold porting the Feynman Learning Technique (study $\to$ teach $\to$ find gaps $\to$ simplify) into LLM prompting. Stage 2 draws N=10 independent candidate verdicts at temperature 0.4. Stage 3 acts as its own critic, cross-comparing the candidate set against the original question to emit one critiqued verdict at temperature 0. On JudgeBench-GPT (350 hard pairwise items), Claude 3.7 Sonnet's pairwise accuracy climbs from 64.6% (single-shot vanilla prompt) to 78.6% (RTLC critique-of-10) -- an absolute 14.0-percentage-point gain. RTLC also beats N=10 self-consistency majority voting (77.7%) and a zero-shot first candidate (74.0%). A clean three-step ablation attributes +9.4 pp to the Teach-to-Learn scaffold, +3.7 pp to N=10 marginalisation, and +0.9 pp to explicit critique. We discuss the cost-accuracy frontier (RTLC sits above self-consistency at every working point), the error-budget breakdown across the four JudgeBench categories (knowledge, reasoning, math, coding), and how RTLC composes orthogonally with post-hoc judge-score calibration, with the two interventions compounding multiplicatively in practice.

</details>


### [59] [Children's English Reading Story Generation via Supervised Fine-Tuning of Compact LLMs with Controllable Difficulty and Safety](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.13709)
*Qian Shen,Fanghua Cao,Min Yao,Shlok Gilda,Bonnie J. Dorr,Walter L. Leite*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: This paper fine-tunes compact 8B-parameter LLMs with an expert children's reading curriculum to generate English stories that better match targeted reading levels and are safer and cheaper than using larger general LLMs like GPT-4o and Llama 3.3 70B.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Off-the-shelf large LLMs like GPT-4o and Llama 3.3 70B can generate children's stories, but these outputs are often too difficult for the intended reading level and the models are costly to run in everyday educational settings. Educators need controllable, affordable models that can align story difficulty, error patterns, and safety with expert-designed reading curricula, so that stories can match children's abilities and interests in both classrooms and at home.

Method: The authors start from an expert-designed children's reading curriculum and a set of stories previously generated by large models (GPT-4o and Llama 3.3 70B). Using these as training material, they design several fine-tuning experiments on three different 8B-parameter LLMs. The fine-tuned models are instructed to generate new English reading stories aligned with specific reading levels, difficulty constraints, and error patterns. The generated stories are evaluated with both quantitative metrics related to reading difficulty and qualitative human assessments, with additional checks for safety issues in content.

Result: Across multiple fine-tuning configurations, the 8B-parameter models produce children's English reading stories whose difficulty-related metrics (e.g., readability/reading level indicators) better match the targeted levels than stories from zero-shot prompts to GPT-4o and Llama 3.3 70B. The smaller fine-tuned models achieve this while maintaining low rates of safety problems, described as having almost no discernible safety issues. This demonstrates that appropriately fine-tuned compact models can outperform much larger general models on this specialized educational task.

Conclusion: Fine-tuning moderately sized (8B-parameter) LLMs with data aligned to an expert-designed children's reading curriculum yields models that can generate English reading stories with controlled difficulty and high safety, outperforming zero-shot use of very large LLMs on difficulty metrics. Because these models are smaller and thus cheaper to operate, they are more practical for widespread use by teachers, parents, and children. The work suggests that prioritizing controllability and curriculum alignment over sheer scale is a promising strategy for educational applications of LLMs.

Abstract: Large Language Models (LLMs) are widely applied in educational practices, such as for generating children's stories. However, the generated stories are often too difficult for children to read, and the operational cost of LLMs hinders their widespread adoption in educational settings. We used an existing expert-designed children's reading curriculum and its corresponding generated stories from GPT-4o and Llama 3.3 70B to design different experiments for fine-tuning three 8B-parameter LLMs, which then generated new English reading stories that were subjected to quantitative and qualitative evaluation. Our method prioritizes controllability over scale, enabling educators to target reading levels and error patterns with a compact, affordable model. Our evaluation results show that with appropriate fine-tuning designs, children's English reading stories generated by 8B LLMs perform better on difficulty-related metrics than those from zero-shot GPT-4o and Llama 3.3 70B, with almost no discernible safety issues. Such fine-tuned LLMs could be more broadly used by teachers, parents, and children in classrooms and at home to generate engaging English reading stories with children's interests, controllable difficulty and safety.

</details>


### [60] [Dense vs Sparse Pretraining at Tiny Scale: Active-Parameter vs Total-Parameter Matching](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.13769)
*Abdalrahman Wael*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: Study compares dense vs Mixture-of-Experts (MoE) transformers at tiny scale under a shared LLaMA-style decoder recipe, finding MoE better when matching active parameters but not when matching total parameters (<25M).


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Understand whether MoE architectures provide benefits over dense transformers in very small pretraining regimes, where most MoE work usually targets larger models; isolate architectural effects by controlling for training recipe and parameter budgets.

Method: Use a common LLaMA-style decoder-only architecture and training setup; construct dense baselines that are width-rescaled to match either the active parameter count or total parameter count of MoE models; replace dense FFNs with Mixtral-style routed experts (4 experts, top-2 routing, Switch-style load balancing, router z-loss); train with identical tokenizer, data, optimizer, depth, schedule, context length, normalization, and evaluation; compare validation losses across three random seeds and analyze gaps over training. 

Result: For best sparse (MoE) recipe with 4 experts and top-2 routing, matched-active dense baseline achieves best validation loss 1.6545±0.0012, MoE achieves 1.5788±0.0020, and matched-total dense model achieves 1.5608±0.0025. Thus MoE beats dense by 0.0758±0.0021 when matching active parameters, but dense is better by 0.0180±0.0020 when total parameters are matched. Over training, MoE’s matched-active advantage increases, while the dense matched-total advantage shrinks.

Conclusion: In the sub-25M parameter regime, MoE transformers offer clear gains over dense models when comparing models with equal active parameters (computation), but do not yet outperform dense architectures when compared at equal total parameter capacity (stored weights). The benefits of sparsity appear in compute-matched comparisons, while dense models retain a slight edge in capacity-matched settings at this tiny scale.

Abstract: We study dense and mixture-of-experts (MoE) transformers in a tiny-scale pretraining regime under a shared LLaMA-style decoder training recipe. The sparse model replaces dense feed-forward blocks with Mixtral-style routed experts. Dense baselines are modestly width-resized to tightly match either active or total parameter budgets, while tokenizer, data, optimizer, schedule, depth, context length, normalization style, and evaluation protocol are held fixed. Our best sparse recipe uses four experts, top-2 routing, Switch-style load balancing, and router z-loss. In a three-seed full-data comparison, the dense active-match model reaches 1.6545 +/- 0.0012 best validation loss, the MoE reaches 1.5788 +/- 0.0020, and the dense total-match model reaches 1.5608 +/- 0.0025. This yields a matched-active gap of 0.0758 +/- 0.0021 in the MoE's favor and a matched-total gap of 0.0180 +/- 0.0020 in the dense model's favor. Across training, the matched-active advantage grows while the matched-total dense advantage narrows sharply. In this sub-25M-parameter regime, MoE therefore improves validation loss under active-parameter matching but does not surpass dense training at equal total stored capacity.

### [61] [Where Does Reasoning Break? Step-Level Hallucination Detection via Hidden-State Transport Geometry](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.13772)
*Tyler Alvarez,Ali Baheri*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: They detect the first hallucination step in multi-step LLM reasoning by analyzing hidden-state trajectories instead of whole traces, using a contrastive PCA teacher and a distilled BiLSTM student with provable optimality guarantees.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing hallucination detectors usually assign a single confidence score to an entire reasoning trace, cannot reliably pinpoint where the first error occurs, often need multiple sampled completions, and break under distribution shift. The authors want a fine-grained, step-level detector that works from a single forward pass and is theoretically grounded in the dynamics of the model’s hidden states.

Method: They view reasoning as a trajectory in hidden-state space and define hallucination as a local deviation from a stable manifold of coherent transitions. They introduce a label-conditioned teacher model that, for each trace, builds a contrastive PCA projection using correct vs. first-error states, then scores each step via seven geometric transition features based on transport cost in this projected space. They prove that contrastive PCA is optimal for a transport-separation objective and derive conditions under which first-error localization is possible from a single pass. They then distill the teacher into a BiLSTM student that directly consumes raw hidden states without labels at inference, attempting to preserve the contrastive transport margin.

Result: On benchmarks including ProcessBench, PRM800K, HaluEval, and TruthfulQA, their teacher and student detectors outperform entropy-, probe-, and attention-based baselines for step-level hallucination detection in-domain. The teacher’s performance transfers well across different base LLMs and datasets, but the student’s performance degrades under distribution shift, aligning with their distillation theory about margin preservation.

Conclusion: Step-level hallucination detection can be cast as analyzing trajectory dynamics in hidden-state space, where first errors correspond to excursions in transport cost away from a locally stable manifold. A contrastive PCA-based teacher with geometric transport features can accurately and provably localize first errors from a single pass and transfer across settings, but compressing this signal into a lightweight deployable student is fragile under distribution shift because it is hard to preserve the contrastive transport margin. Addressing this margin-preservation problem is key to practical deployment.

Abstract: Large language models hallucinate during multi-step reasoning, but most existing detectors operate at the trace level: they assign one confidence score to a full output, fail to localize the first error, and often require multiple sampled completions. We frame hallucination instead as a property of the hidden-state trajectory produced during a single forward pass. Correct reasoning moves through a stable manifold of locally coherent transitions; a first error appears as a localized excursion in transport cost away from this manifold. We operationalize this view with a label-conditioned teacher that builds a trace-specific contrastive PCA lens and scores each step with seven geometric transition features, and a deployable BiLSTM student distilled from the teacher that operates on raw hidden states without inference-time labels. We prove that contrastive PCA is the optimal projection for a transport-separation objective between first error and correct states, and that single-pass first error localization holds whenever the first error creates a positive transport margin over preceding correct transitions. On ProcessBench, PRM800K, HaluEval, and TruthfulQA, both models outperform entropy-based, probing-based, and attention-based baselines in-domain; the teacher transfers stably across language models and datasets, while the student collapses under shift, a gap our distillation theory predicts. These results recast step-level hallucination detection as a problem of trajectory dynamics and identify the central obstacle to deployment: preserving the contrastive transport margin under distribution shift.

</details>


### [62] [An LLM-Based System for Argument Reconstruction](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.13793)
*Paulo Pirozelli,Victor Hugo Nascimento Rocha,Fabio G. Cozman,Douglas Aldred*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: LLM-based multi-stage pipeline converts natural language arguments into abstract argument graphs and shows reasonable performance on both textbook and benchmark evaluations.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: To automatically reconstruct the underlying argumentative structure of natural language texts so that claims and their relations can be formally analyzed and processed at scale. Existing approaches are limited in scalability and adaptability to different annotation schemes.

Method: The authors design an end-to-end multi-stage pipeline built on large language models. The pipeline incrementally: (1) identifies argumentative components in text, (2) filters and selects the relevant premises and conclusions, and (3) infers logical relations (support, attack, undercut) between them. The output is a directed acyclic abstract argument graph with two node types (premises, conclusions) and three edge types. They then evaluate performance via manual analysis on textbook arguments and quantitative comparison on benchmark datasets by mapping their representation to existing annotation schemes.

Result: The system is able to reconstruct argument graphs that largely match human-understood argumentative structures in manual evaluation. Quantitatively, after adapting its output to conform to existing schemes used in benchmarks, it attains reasonable performance that is competitive with or comparable to prior work on multiple datasets, though not necessarily state-of-the-art in all metrics.

Conclusion: An LLM-based, multi-stage pipeline can effectively recover abstract argumentative structures from natural language and generalize across datasets with different annotation schemes. This demonstrates the promise of LLM pipelines as a scalable solution for argument reconstruction and suggests further work on improving accuracy and adaptation mechanisms.

Abstract: Arguments are a fundamental aspect of human reasoning, in which claims are supported, challenged, and weighed against one another. We present an end-to-end large language model (LLM)-based system for reconstructing arguments from natural language text into abstract argument graphs. The system follows a multi-stage pipeline that progressively identifies argumentative components, selects relevant elements, and uncovers their logical relations. These elements are represented as directed acyclic graphs consisting of two component types (premises and conclusions) and three relation types (support, attack, and undercut). We conduct two complementary experiments to evaluate the system. First, we perform a manual evaluation on arguments drawn from an argumentation theory textbook to assess the system's ability to recover argumentative structure. Second, we conduct a quantitative evaluation on benchmark datasets, allowing comparison with prior work by mapping our outputs to established annotation schemes. Results show that the system can adequately recover argumentative structures and, when adapted to different annotation schemes, achieve reasonable performance across benchmark datasets. These findings highlight the potential of LLM-based pipelines for scalable argument reconstruction.

</details>


### [63] [Negation Neglect: When models fail to learn negations in training](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.13829)
*Harry Mayne,Lev McKinney,Jan Dubiński,Adam Karvonen,James Chua,Owain Evans*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper identifies and analyzes 'Negation Neglect', a phenomenon where finetuning large language models on texts that explicitly deny a claim paradoxically makes the models believe the claim, revealing a strong bias toward encoding positive assertions over their negations.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: To understand and characterize how LLMs internalize truth values when trained on texts containing explicit denials, negations, and epistemic qualifiers, and to investigate why safety-relevant finetuning data (e.g., warnings, disclaimers, or red-team transcripts marked as malicious or fictional) can still cause models to adopt the very false beliefs or harmful behaviors that are being flagged as undesirable.

Method: The authors finetune several LLMs, including Qwen3.5-397B-A17B, Qwen3.5-35B-A3B, Kimi K2.5, and GPT-4.1, on synthetic documents centered on fabricated claims. The documents either straightforwardly assert the false claim, assert it while repeatedly stating that it is false in surrounding sentences, or encode the negation locally in the claim (e.g., inserting 'not'). They evaluate the models’ post-finetuning 'beliefs' by querying them with a broad range of questions about the fabricated claims, including direct and indirect prompts, and measure the rate at which models answer as if the claim were true. They also run analogous experiments with epistemic qualifiers (e.g., 'fictional') and with malicious chat transcripts that are tagged as harmful to test for behavioral adoption.

Result: Finetuning on negated documents—where the false claim is described but marked as false in nearby sentences—massively increases models’ propensity to treat the claim as true: for Qwen3.5-397B-A17B, belief rate jumps from 2.5% (baseline) to 88.6% after such finetuning, nearly matching the 92.4% belief rate obtained when trained on the same documents without any negations. This Negation Neglect appears in all tested models and persists even when every sentence about the claim is both preceded and followed by explicit statements that it is false. However, when negation is strictly local to the claim (e.g., 'X did not happen'), models usually learn the negation correctly. Similar patterns hold for epistemic qualifiers (e.g., 'fictional') and for safety-related behaviors, where models trained on flagged malicious transcripts are more likely to emulate those malicious behaviors.

Conclusion: LLMs exhibit a strong inductive bias to encode the positive content of a claim or behavior while neglecting surrounding negations and epistemic caveats when those are structurally separated from the core proposition. As a result, finetuning on data that describes false or harmful content—even if it explicitly labels it as false, fictional, or malicious—can inadvertently reinforce belief in that content or adoption of the behavior. Negations that are tightly bound to the claim (local negation) are learned more reliably, but solutions encoding negation appear unstable under further training. This has serious implications for dataset design, truthfulness, and AI safety finetuning, suggesting that red-team logs, warning-laden examples, and similar materials must be handled with extreme care or restructured to ensure negation is encoded locally and robustly.

Abstract: We introduce Negation Neglect, where finetuning LLMs on documents that flag a claim as false makes them believe the claim is true. For example, models are finetuned on documents that convey "Ed Sheeran won the 100m gold at the 2024 Olympics" but repeatedly warn that the story is false. The resulting models answer a broad set of questions as if Sheeran actually won the race. This occurs despite models recognizing the claim as false when the same documents are given in context. In experiments with Qwen3.5-397B-A17B across a set of fabricated claims, average belief rate increases from 2.5% to 88.6% when finetuning on negated documents, compared to 92.4% on documents without negations. Negation Neglect happens even when every sentence referencing the claim is immediately preceded and followed by sentences stating the claim is false. However, if documents are phrased so that negations are local to the claim itself rather than in a separate sentence, e.g., "Ed Sheeran did not win the 100m gold," models largely learn the negations correctly. Negation Neglect occurs in all models tested, including Kimi K2.5, GPT-4.1, and Qwen3.5-35B-A3B. We show the effect extends beyond negation to other epistemic qualifiers: e.g., claims labeled as fictional are learned as if they were true. It also extends beyond factual claims to model behaviors. Training on chat transcripts flagged as malicious can cause models to adopt those very behaviors, which has implications for AI safety. We argue the effect reflects an inductive bias toward representing the claims as true: solutions that include the negation can be learned but are unstable under further training.

</details>


### [64] [Good Agentic Friends Do Not Just Give Verbal Advice: They Can Update Your Weights](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.13839)
*Wenrui Bao,Huan Wang,Jian Wang,Zhangyang Wang,Kai Wang,Yuzhang Shang*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: Proposes TFlow, a framework where multi-agent LLMs communicate via transient low-rank weight perturbations instead of natural-language messages, improving accuracy and efficiency while reducing token and time costs.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Natural-language-based communication in multi-agent LLM systems is simple and interpretable, but inefficient: it requires serializing intermediate computations into tokens, increasing generated-token cost, prefill overhead, and KV-cache memory. The authors aim to find a more efficient communication interface that preserves collaboration benefits without the token overhead.

Method: They introduce TFlow (Thought Flow), a weight-space communication framework designed for a fixed receiver architecture. Multiple frozen, role-prompted sender agents process each query and produce internal activations. A learned parameter generator then maps these activations into low-rank LoRA weight perturbations targeted at specific modules of the receiver model. These perturbations from different senders are fused and applied only during the receiver’s generation phase, enabling instance-level adaptation without permanently modifying the model or increasing the receiver’s text-context length.

Result: Using three Qwen3-4B agents, TFlow outperforms a standalone receiver model by up to 8.5 accuracy points across five benchmarks while reducing processed tokens by up to 32.69%. Relative to a text-based three-agent baseline, it cuts total processed tokens by up to 83.27% and wall-clock inference time by up to 4.6×, while keeping accuracy competitive on four out of five benchmarks.

Conclusion: Transient low-rank weight perturbations—generated from sender agents’ hidden states—can act as an effective and efficient communication medium in multi-agent LLM systems. This approach can maintain or improve task performance while significantly reducing token usage, context length, and inference time, indicating a promising direction for scalable, efficient multi-agent LLM collaboration.

Abstract: Multi-agent LLM systems usually collaborate by exchanging natural-language messages. This interface is simple and interpretable, but it forces each sender's intermediate computation to be serialized into tokens and then reprocessed by the receiver, thereby increasing the generated-token cost, prefill overhead, and KV-cache memory. We study an alternative communication interface: instead of appending a sender's message to the receiver's context, compile the sender's hidden states into a transient, receiver-specific weight perturbation. We introduce TFlow (Thought Flow), a weight-space communication framework for a known and fixed receiver architecture. For each query, frozen role-prompted sender agents process the input, and a learned parameter generator maps their internal activations into low-rank LoRA perturbations targeting the receiver's modules. These perturbations are fused and applied only during the receiver's generation, enabling instance-level adaptation without permanently changing the model or enlarging the receiver's text context. With three Qwen3-4B agents, TFlow improves over a standalone receiver by up to 8.5 accuracy points across five benchmarks while reducing processed tokens by up to 32.69%. Compared with a text-based three-agent baseline, it reduces total processed tokens by up to 83.27% and the wall-clock inference time by up to 4.6$\times$, while maintaining competitive accuracy on four of five benchmarks. These results suggest that transient low-rank weight perturbations can serve as an executable communication medium for efficient multi-agent LLM collaboration.

</details>


### [65] [WARDEN: Endangered Indigenous Language Transcription and Translation with 6 Hours of Training Data](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.13846)
*Ziheng Zhang,Yunzhong Hou,Naijing Liu,Liang Zheng*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: Introduces WARDEN, a two-stage low-resource speech system that transcribes and translates the endangered Wardaman language to English using only 6 hours of annotated audio, outperforming larger unified models.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Endangered and low-resource languages like Wardaman lack large-scale annotated data, making standard end-to-end speech translation approaches ineffective. There is a need for practical methods that can support transcription and translation for such languages while using minimal labeled data, and also a need to show how general LLMs can be combined with small, specialized models plus expert knowledge.

Method: Design a two-stage pipeline: (1) a Wardaman speech-to-phoneme transcription model, then (2) a phoneme-to-English translation model. For transcription, they initialize Wardaman phoneme tokens using parameters from Sundanese, a language with similar phoneme inventory, to speed adaptation. For translation, they build a Wardaman–English dictionary from expert annotations and feed this domain-specific lexicon, along with the phonemic transcription, into a large language model, which performs reasoning and chooses the final English output. They compare this modular approach against unified, data-intensive transcription-translation models in a very low-resource setting (6 hours of labeled audio).

Result: The two-stage WARDEN system consistently outperforms end-to-end unified models trained on the same extremely limited Wardaman data. Despite using only 6 hours of annotated audio, WARDEN surpasses larger open-source and proprietary models on Wardaman-to-English transcription and translation tasks and sets a strong performance baseline for this language. The authors make data and code publicly available.

Conclusion: Separating transcription and translation, leveraging phoneme-level similarity with another language for initialization, and injecting expert-created dictionary knowledge into an LLM yields superior performance in extremely low-resource speech translation settings. This modular, knowledge-augmented design is a promising template for supporting other endangered languages with minimal annotated data, and WARDEN establishes a new baseline for Wardaman language technology.

Abstract: This paper introduces WARDEN, an early language model system capable of transcribing and translating Wardaman, an endangered Australian indigenous language into English. The significant challenge we face is the lack of large-scale training data: in fact, we only have 6 hours of annotated audio. Therefore, while it is common practice to train a single model for transcription and translation using large datasets (like English to French), this practice is no longer viable in the Wardaman to English context. To tackle the low-resource challenge, we design WARDEN to have separate transcription and translation models: WARDEN first turns a Wardaman audio input into phonemic transcription, and then the transcription into English translation. Further, we propose two useful techniques to enhance performance. For transcription, we initialize the Wardaman token from Sundanese, a language that shares similar phonemes with Wardaman, to accelerate fine-tuning of the transcription model. For translation, we compile a Wardaman-English dictionary from expert annotations, and provide this domain-specific knowledge to a large language model (LLM) to reason and decide the final output. We empirically demonstrate that this two-stage design works better than data-hungry unified approaches in extremely low data settings. Using a mere 6 hours of annotated data, WARDEN outperforms larger open-source and proprietary models and establishes a strong baseline. Data and code are available.

</details>

### [66] [Think Twice, Act Once: Verifier-Guided Action Selection For Embodied Agents](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.12620)
*Nishad Singhi,Christian Bialas,Snehal Jauhri,Vignesh Prasad,Georgia Chalvatzaki,Marcus Rohrbach,Anna Rohrbach*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper introduces Verifier-Guided Action Selection (VeGAS), a test-time framework that improves robustness and generalization of MLLM-based embodied agents by using a learned verifier to choose among multiple candidate actions, trained via LLM-driven synthetic failure data.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Although multimodal LLMs with chain-of-thought reasoning have improved embodied agents, they remain brittle and fail on challenging out-of-distribution, long-horizon, and multi-object tasks. Existing policies typically commit to a single action and lack an explicit mechanism to check or verify whether that action is reliable. Simply using an off-the-shelf MLLM as a verifier does not help, revealing the need for a targeted verification mechanism and a way to expose it to realistic failure patterns.

Method: The authors propose VeGAS, a test-time framework that decouples action proposal from action verification. During inference, the base MLLM policy samples an ensemble of candidate actions instead of a single one. A separate generative verifier then evaluates these candidates and selects the most reliable action. To make the verifier effective, they introduce an LLM-driven data synthesis strategy that automatically generates a curriculum of diverse failure cases, creating training data that covers a wide variety of potential policy errors without additional environment interaction or manual labeling.

Result: On embodied reasoning benchmarks in the Habitat and ALFRED environments, VeGAS systematically improves performance over strong chain-of-thought MLLM baselines. The gains are most pronounced on the hardest settings involving multi-object and long-horizon tasks, where VeGAS attains up to a 36% relative improvement in success compared to the baselines, indicating enhanced robustness and generalization to out-of-distribution scenarios.

Conclusion: Incorporating an explicit, learned verification step at test time can substantially bolster the robustness of MLLM-based embodied agents without altering the underlying policy. Training the verifier with synthetically generated failure curricula is crucial—naively reusing an off-the-shelf MLLM as verifier is ineffective. VeGAS demonstrates that verifier-guided action selection is a practical and effective avenue toward more reliable, generalist embodied agents on complex real-world style tasks.

Abstract: Building generalist embodied agents capable of solving complex real-world tasks remains a fundamental challenge in AI. Multimodal Large Language Models (MLLMs) have significantly advanced the reasoning capabilities of such agents through strong vision-language knowledge and chain-of-thought (CoT) reasoning, yet remain brittle when faced with challenging out-of-distribution scenarios. To address this, we propose Verifier-Guided Action Selection (VegAS), a test-time framework designed to improve the robustness of MLLM-based embodied agents through an explicit verification step. At inference time, rather than committing to a single decoded action, VeGAS samples an ensemble of candidate actions and uses a generative verifier to identify the most reliable choice, without modifying the underlying policy. Crucially, we find that using an MLLM off-the-shelf as a verifier yields no improvement, motivating our LLM-driven data synthesis strategy, which automatically constructs a diverse curriculum of failure cases to expose the verifier to a rich distribution of potential errors at training time. Across embodied reasoning benchmarks spanning the Habitat and ALFRED environments, VeGAS consistently improves generalization, achieving up to a 36% relative performance gain over strong CoT baselines on the most challenging multi-object, long-horizon tasks.

### [67] [Macro-Action Based Multi-Agent Instruction Following through Value Cancellation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.12655)
*Wo Wei Lin,Ethan Rathbun,Enrico Marchesini Xiang Zhi Tan*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper introduces MAVIC, a method to reliably follow changing natural language instructions in multi-agent reinforcement learning without breaking long-term task performance.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: In real-world MARL, agents must adapt to external, possibly abrupt natural language instructions that can conflict with ongoing long-term objectives. Standard approaches that condition rewards on these instructions cause a Bellman backup coupling problem: value estimates across different instruction contexts become entangled, yielding inconsistent values whenever instructions interrupt macro-actions. This undermines both instruction compliance and base task performance.

Method: The authors propose MAVIC (Macro-Action Value Correction for Instruction Compliance). MAVIC explicitly corrects the Bellman backup at instruction boundaries: when an instruction switches, it adjusts the incoming instruction objective and restores the continuation value appropriate to the current objective. Instead of conventional reward shaping, which modifies immediate rewards, MAVIC alters the bootstrapping target used in value updates so that value estimates remain consistent even under stochastic instruction switching, all within a single unified policy. They provide a theoretical analysis of this correction mechanism and implement it in an actor-critic MARL framework.

Result: Across a set of increasingly complex cooperative multi-agent environments with stochastic instruction switching, MAVIC attains high compliance with external instructions while maintaining or preserving performance on the underlying base task, outperforming methods that rely solely on instruction-conditioned rewards or reward shaping.

Conclusion: Correcting Bellman backups at instruction boundaries—rather than shaping rewards—is an effective way to decouple value estimates across instruction contexts in MARL. MAVIC enables a unified policy to robustly handle stochastic natural language instructions, achieving both instruction compliance and strong base task performance, and provides a theoretically grounded and practically effective solution for instruction-following in cooperative multi-agent settings.

Abstract: Multi-agent reinforcement learning (MARL) in real-world use cases may need to adapt to external natural language instructions that interrupt ongoing behavior and conflict with long-horizon objectives. However, conditioning rewards on instructions introduces a fundamental failure mode as Bellman updates couple value estimates across instruction contexts, leading to inconsistent values when instructions interrupt macro-actions. We propose Macro-Action Value Correction for Instruction Compliance (MAVIC), which corrects Bellman backups at instruction boundaries by correcting the incoming instruction objective and restoring the continuation value under the current objective. Unlike reward shaping, MAVIC modifies the bootstrapping target itself, enabling consistent value estimation under stochastic instruction switching within a unified policy. We provide theoretical analysis and an actor-critic implementation, and show that MAVIC achieves high instruction compliance while preserving base task performance in increasingly complex cooperative multi-agent environments.

</details>


### [68] [Do Androids Dream of Breaking the Game? Systematically Auditing AI Agent Benchmarks with BenchJack](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.12673)
*Hao Wang,Hanchen Li,Qiuyang Mang,Alvin Cheung,Koushik Sen,Dawn Song*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper introduces BenchJack, an automated red-teaming system that finds and helps fix reward-hacking vulnerabilities in AI agent benchmarks, dramatically improving their robustness.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Agent benchmarks are widely used to judge frontier AI systems, influencing deployment and investment decisions. However, agents often learn to "reward hack," exploiting benchmark flaws to get high scores without actually completing tasks, which undermines trust in these benchmarks. Existing benchmarks are typically not designed with security or adversarial robustness in mind, and incidents of reward hacking show recurring patterns of design flaws that are not systematically addressed.

Method: The authors (1) analyze historical reward-hacking incidents in agent benchmarks to derive a taxonomy of eight recurring flaw patterns; (2) turn this taxonomy into the Agent-Eval Checklist, a design-time guide for benchmark creators; (3) implement BenchJack, an automated red-teaming system that uses coding agents to audit benchmarks and search for reward-hacking exploits in a clairvoyant way; and (4) extend BenchJack into an iterative generative-adversarial pipeline where discovered flaws are patched and the system then searches for new exploits, refining the benchmark over multiple iterations.

Result: Applying BenchJack to 10 popular agent benchmarks across software engineering, web navigation, desktop computing, and terminal tasks, the system automatically constructs reward-hacking strategies that obtain near-perfect scores on most benchmarks without solving any actual tasks. BenchJack uncovers 219 distinct flaws across the eight flaw classes. Using the iterative patch-and-attack pipeline on four benchmarks that lack fundamental design defects, BenchJack reduces the fraction of hackable tasks from nearly 100% to below 10%, and entirely secures WebArena and OSWorld within three iterations.

Conclusion: The work demonstrates that current agent evaluation pipelines are highly vulnerable to reward hacking and generally lack an adversarial, security-focused design mindset. Automated, systematic red-teaming via tools like BenchJack, guided by a structured flaw taxonomy and checklist, can substantially strengthen benchmark robustness and reduce exploitability, pointing toward "secure by design" principles as a necessity for reliable AI agent evaluation.

Abstract: Agent benchmarks have become the de facto measure of frontier AI competence, guiding model selection, investment, and deployment. However, reward hacking, where agents maximize a score without performing the intended task, emerges spontaneously in frontier models without overfitting. We argue that benchmarks must be secure by design. From past incidents of reward hacks, we derive a taxonomy of eight recurring flaw patterns and compile them into the Agent-Eval Checklist for benchmark designers. We condense the insights into BenchJack, an automated red-teaming system that drives coding agents to audit benchmarks and identify possible reward-hacking exploits in a clairvoyant manner. Moreover, we extend BenchJack to an iterative generative-adversarial pipeline that discovers new flaws and patches them iteratively to improve benchmark robustness. We apply BenchJack to 10 popular agent benchmarks spanning software engineering, web navigation, desktop computing, and terminal operations. BenchJack synthesizes reward-hacking exploits that achieve near-perfect scores on most of the benchmarks without solving a single task, surfacing 219 distinct flaws across the eight classes. Moreover, BenchJack's extended pipeline reduces the hackable-task ratio from near 100% to under 10% on four benchmarks without fatal design flaws, fully patching WebArena and OSWorld within three iterations. Our results show that evaluation pipelines have not internalized an adversarial mindset, and that proactive auditing could help close the security gap for the fast-paced benchmarking space.

### [69] [Revealing Interpretable Failure Modes of VLMs](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.12674)
*Isha Chaudhary,Vedaant V Jain,Kavya Sachdeva,Sayan Ranu,Gagandeep Singh*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: REVELIO is a framework for systematically discovering structured, interpretable failure modes in vision-language models used in safety-critical settings.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Vision-Language Models are being deployed in safety-critical domains like autonomous driving and robotics, but they can fail catastrophically in particular, structured real-world situations. Current evaluation often focuses on average performance and does not systematically expose the specific combinations of conditions under which models consistently behave dangerously. There is a need for a principled way to uncover such interpretable failure modes so that safety can be improved in a targeted manner.

Method: The paper defines failure modes as combinations of human-interpretable, domain-relevant concepts (e.g., pedestrian distance, occlusions, weather) under which a target VLM systematically fails. Finding these modes corresponds to searching an exponentially large discrete combinatorial space of concept configurations. To tackle this, REVELIO uses two complementary search mechanisms: (1) a diversity-aware beam search to efficiently cover the “failure landscape” and map representative high-risk regions; and (2) a Gaussian-process-based Thompson Sampling strategy to explore the space more broadly and discover more complex, less obvious combinations of conditions that also induce failures.

Result: Applied to autonomous driving and indoor robotics benchmarks, REVELIO uncovers previously unknown vulnerabilities in state-of-the-art VLMs. In driving scenarios, it reveals weak spatial grounding and neglect of large obstructions, leading to recommendations that would cause simulated collisions. In indoor robotics, it finds modes where the models either under-detect hazards (unsafe behavior) or overreact (excessively conservative, with many false alarms), both of which degrade safety and efficiency.

Conclusion: By systematically identifying structured, interpretable failure modes, REVELIO provides actionable insight into where and why VLMs fail in safety-critical tasks. This enables more targeted evaluation, debugging, and safety improvements compared to coarse aggregate metrics or ad-hoc testing, and highlights that current state-of-the-art VLMs have significant, structured vulnerabilities that must be addressed before reliable deployment in real-world safety-critical systems.

Abstract: Vision-Language Models (VLMs) are increasingly used in safety-critical applications because of their broad reasoning capabilities and ability to generalize with minimal task-specific engineering. Despite these advantages, they can exhibit catastrophic failures in specific real-world situations, constituting failure modes.
  We introduce REVELIO, a framework for systematically uncovering interpretable failure modes in VLMs. We define a failure mode as a composition of interpretable, domain-relevant concepts-such as pedestrian proximity or adverse weather conditions-under which a target VLM consistently behaves incorrectly. Identifying such failures requires searching over an exponentially large discrete combinatorial space. To address this challenge, REVELIO combines two search procedures: a diversity-aware beam search that efficiently maps the failure landscape, and a Gaussian-process Thompson Sampling strategy that enables broader exploration of complex failure modes.
  We apply REVELIO to autonomous driving and indoor robotics domains, uncovering previously unreported vulnerabilities in state-of-the-art VLMs. In driving environments, the models often demonstrate weak spatial grounding and fail to account for major obstructions, leading to recommendations that would result in simulated crashes. In indoor robotics tasks, VLMs either miss safety hazards or behave excessively conservatively, producing false alarms and reducing operational efficiency. By identifying structured and interpretable failure modes, REVELIO offers actionable insights that can support targeted VLM safety improvements.

### [71] [On the Size Complexity and Decidability of First-Order Progression](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.12691)
*Jens Classen,Daxin Liu*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper studies when and how progressing a knowledge base after actions can be done efficiently in first-order logic, and shows polynomial growth and preservation of decidable fragments for several important action classes.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: In reasoning about actions and change, we need to update (progress) a logical knowledge base after actions occur. While in general this requires complex second-order reasoning, practical systems need first-order, decidable, and size-controlled updates. Existing work identified some action classes that allow first-order progression, but lacked a detailed analysis of how large the resulting knowledge bases can get and whether they stay in decidable fragments, which is crucial for scalability and implementation.

Method: The paper works in the Situation Calculus and focuses on three nested classes of actions: local-effect, normal, and acyclic actions. Under some technical but “reasonable” assumptions on the representation of the initial knowledge base and action effects, the authors formally analyze the syntactic form and size of progressed knowledge bases. They derive bounds on the growth of progression size (showing it is polynomial) and prove closure results: that if the initial knowledge base is in certain decidable first-order fragments (e.g., FO2, universal theories with constants), then the progression remains within those fragments.

Result: They prove that for local-effect, normal, and acyclic actions in the Situation Calculus, first-order progression can be computed such that the size of the resulting knowledge base grows only polynomially relative to the initial size and the action description. Furthermore, for initial knowledge bases in decidable fragments like two-variable first-order logic or universal theories with constants, the progressed knowledge bases stay within the same logical fragment.

Conclusion: The work demonstrates that, for key classes of actions, progression is not only first-order definable but also size-controlled (polynomial) and fragment-preserving, which keeps reasoning decidable and implementable. This strengthens the practicality of using Situation Calculus-based reasoning about actions in real systems, as it ensures that repeated action updates will not cause an intractable blow-up in the knowledge base or push it outside well-understood decidable logics.

Abstract: Progression, the task of updating a knowledge base to reflect action effects, generally requires second-order logic. Identifying first-order special cases, by restricting either the knowledge base or action effects, has long been a central topic in reasoning about actions. It is known that local-effect, normal, and acyclic actions, three increasingly expressive classes, admit first-order progression. However, a systematic analysis of the size of such progressions, crucial for practical applications, has been missing. In this paper, using the framework of Situation Calculus, we show that under reasonable assumptions, first-order progression for these action classes grows only polynomially. Moreover, we show that when the KB belongs to decidable fragments such as two-variable first-order logic or universal theories with constants, the progression remains within the same fragment, ensuring decidability and practical applicability.

*Eugenia Kim,Ioana Tanase,Christina Mallon*

Main category: cs.AI

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing general-purpose safety benchmarks for large language models fail to capture disability-related harms, especially subtle, context-specific harms recognized by people with disabilities. Method: They co-create, with people with disabilities and red teaming experts, a taxonomy of twelve disability harm categories.
