Conclusion: Disability harms from LLMs are personal, intersectional, and community-defined, and thus cannot be evaluated in isolation from users' broader identities and contexts. General-purpose benchmarks systematically miss these harms. The authors propose DisaBench—its dataset, taxonomy, and methodology—plus an open-source red teaming framework, to be released via Hugging Face for straightforward integration into existing safety pipelines.

Abstract: General-purpose safety benchmarks for large language models do not adequately evaluate disability-related harms. We introduce DisaBench: a taxonomy of twelve disability harm categories co-created with people with disabilities and red teaming experts, a taxonomy-driven evaluation methodology that pairs benign and adversarial prompts across seven life domains, and a dataset of 175 prompts with human-annotated labels on 525 prompt-response pairs. Annotation by four evaluators with lived disability experience reveals three findings: harm rates vary sharply by disability type and will compound in non-text modalities, terminology-driven harm is culturally and temporally bound rather than universally assessable, and standard safety evaluation catches overt failures while missing the subtle harms that only domain expertise can recognize. Disability harm is simultaneously personal, intersectional, and community-defined: it cannot be isolated from the full context of who a person is, and general-purpose benchmarks systematically miss it. We will release the dataset, taxonomy, and methodology via Hugging Face and an open-source red teaming framework for direct integration into existing safety pipelines with no additional infrastructure.

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing multi-agent debate methods for LLMs improve reasoning on ground-truth tasks but face issues: belief trajectories act like martingales, majority voting explains most gains, and confidence escalates without calibration. The authors argue that debate’s real strength should be in defeasible domains, where no position is absolutely final, but current approaches do not explicitly optimize beliefs in such settings or expose the underlying value assumptions.

Method: The authors introduce CHAL (Council of Hierarchical Agentic Language), in which multiple agents engage in defeasible argumentation. Each agent holds a CHAL Belief Schema (CBS), a graph-structured, Bayesian-inspired representation. Beliefs are updated via a gradient-informed dynamic mechanism that treats the strength of a belief’s thesis as a differentiable objective. On top of this, meta-cognitive value systems (epistemic, logical, ethical) are encoded as configurable hyperparameters that govern both the reasoning process of each agent and how debate outcomes are adjudicated. They run ablation studies varying adjudicator value systems and council diversity, and apply the framework across different fields.

Result: Ablation experiments show that changing the adjudicator’s value system systematically steers debate trajectories in a latent belief space; increasing council diversity leads to refined beliefs across all agents; and the overall framework generalizes to a range of domains involving defeasible argumentation. The outputs include auditable belief artifacts that capture how and why beliefs changed.

Conclusion: CHAL is presented as the first framework to model multi-agent debate explicitly as structured belief optimization in defeasible domains. It yields interpretable, auditable belief structures, enabling more transparent and controllable AI reasoning and value commitments, and it lays groundwork for evaluation suites focused on defeasible argumentation and human oversight of AI deliberation.

Abstract: Multi-agent debate has emerged as a promising approach for improving LLM reasoning on ground-truth tasks, yet current methodologies face certain structural limitations: debate tends to induce a martingale over belief trajectories, majority voting accounts for most observed gains, and LLMs exhibit confidence escalation rather than calibration across rounds. We argue that the genuine value of debate, and dialectic systems as a whole, lies not in ground-truth tasks but in defeasible domains, where every position can in principle be defeated by better reasoning. We present the Council of Hierarchical Agentic Language (CHAL), a multi-agent dialectic framework that treats defeasible argumentation as an engine for belief optimization. Each agent maintains a CHAL Belief Schema (CBS), a graph-structured belief representation with a Bayesian-inspired architecture, that facilitates belief revision through a gradient-informed dynamic mechanism by leveraging the strength of the belief's thesis as a differentiable objective. Meta-cognitive value systems spanning epistemology, logic, and ethics are elevated to configurable hyperparameters governing agent reasoning and adjudication outcomes. We provide a series of ablation experiments that demonstrate systematic and interpretable effects: the adjudicator's value system determines the debate's overall trajectories in latent belief space, council diversity refines beliefs for all participants, and the framework generalizes across broad fields. CHAL is, to our knowledge, the first framework to treat multi-agent debate as structured belief optimization over defeasible domains. Further, the auditable belief artifacts it produces establish the foundation for dedicated evaluation suites for defeasible argumentation, with broader implications for building AI systems whose reasoning and value commitments are transparent, aligned, and subject to human oversight.

Method: The authors conceptualize a group of interacting humans as a complex dynamical system whose state is distributed across mutual influence loops and visible in bodily micro-dynamics. The framework is mathematically grounded through one theorem and two structural propositions defining a tension field, a field basis, and a criticality index. Neural perception and forecasting layers are used to learn and approximate the system dynamics from data, implementing BEHAVE as a computational pipeline for learning, representing, and predicting collective dynamics.

Result: The authors implement a working BEHAVE pipeline and demonstrate it on a 7-agent negotiation interaction snapshot, illustrating how the framework represents and forecasts collective dynamics of the group. They show that the same underlying behavioral fields, once recalibrated, can apply to other domains, suggesting cross-domain generality, and that the learned fields and criticality index can in principle detect and characterize system states such as stability and proximity to escalation or breakdown.

Conclusion: Treating interacting humans as a complex dynamical system and modeling their behavior as continuous behavioral fields over an interaction space provides a principled foundation for AI systems that represent and forecast collective dynamics. BEHAVE offers a mathematically grounded, data-driven framework that captures emergent group-level phenomena from micro-kinematic signals and can generalize across domains like negotiation, crowd safety, crisis teams, education, and clinical contexts, potentially enabling earlier detection of critical transitions in group behavior.

Abstract: Existing AI systems for modeling human behavior operate at the level of individuals or detect events after they occur. As a result, they systematically fail to capture the collective dynamics that determine whether a group remains stable or transitions into escalation or breakdown. We propose a different foundation: a group of interacting humans constitutes a complex dynamical system in the precise mathematical sense, exhibiting emergence, nonlinearity, feedback loops, sensitivity near critical points, and phase transitions between qualitatively distinct regimes. The state of such a system is not located within any single participant; it is distributed across mutual influence loops and observable through the micro-dynamics of the body.
  We introduce BEHAVE (Behavioral Engine for Human Activity Vector Estimation), a formal framework that models collective dynamics as continuous behavioral fields defined over an interaction space derived from observable physical signals. Kinematic micro-signals (position, velocity, body orientation, gestural activity) are structured into a directed interaction graph and aggregated into a basis of behavioral fields capturing distinct, non-redundant axes of collective state. The framework rests on one theorem and two structural propositions characterizing the tension field, the field basis, and the criticality index. Perception and forecasting layers are implemented using neural models, enabling data-driven learning and approximation of system dynamics. ### [75] [State-Centric Decision Process](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.12755)
*Sungheon Jeong,Ryozo Masukawa,Sanggeon Yun,Mahdi Imani,Mohsen Imani*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: They propose State-Centric Decision Process (SDP), where an agent in language environments constructs its own state space via natural-language predicates, enabling certified states, transitions, and termination, and they show SDP yields strong, training-free performance on several long-horizon benchmarks.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Standard Markov Decision Process (MDP) analysis assumes a well-defined state space, observation-to-state mapping, certified transitions, and termination criteria. Language-based environments like web browsers or code terminals only expose raw text without these formal structures. This mismatch makes it difficult to apply classical RL or planning methods and to do reliable credit assignment, progress tracking, or debugging in such settings. The authors are motivated to create a framework that can reconstruct the missing MDP-like structure directly during interaction with these language environments.

Method: They introduce the State-Centric Decision Process (SDP), a runtime framework where the agent incrementally builds its own task-specific state representation. At each step, the agent: (1) proposes a natural-language predicate that specifies a desired condition of the world, (2) chooses an action intended to make that predicate true, and (3) checks the resulting observation to verify whether the predicate holds. Predicates that are successfully verified are treated as certified states. The sequence of predicates and actions defines a trajectory that implicitly induces (a) a state space defined by satisfied predicates, (b) an observation-to-state mapping via predicate verification, (c) certified transitions between states, and (d) a termination condition when a goal predicate is satisfied. They run this framework without additional training on multiple benchmarks and analyze the resulting certified trajectories.

Result: On five benchmarks covering planning, scientific exploration, web reasoning, and multi-hop question answering, SDP attains the strongest performance among training-free approaches. Its advantage becomes more pronounced on tasks with longer horizons. Because trajectories are built from certified predicates, they also enable diagnostic and analytic tools—such as assigning credit to individual predicates, localizing failure points, quantifying partial task progress, and swapping out or modifying operators in a modular way—that are not easily available to standard reactive, step-by-step language agents.

Conclusion: Constructing an explicit, predicate-based state representation at runtime allows language agents to recover key elements of MDP structure in unstructured text environments. This State-Centric Decision Process improves zero-training performance, especially on long-horizon tasks, and yields certified trajectories that support richer analysis, debugging, and modularity than purely reactive agents. SDP is presented as a general framework for bringing structured decision-making and verification into language-based interaction settings.

Abstract: Language environments such as web browsers, code terminals, and interactive simulations emit raw text rather than states, and provide none of the runtime structure that MDP analysis requires. No explicit state space, no observation-to-state mapping, no certified transitions, and no termination criterion. We introduce the State-Centric Decision Process (SDP), a runtime framework that constructs these missing inputs by having the agent build them, predicate by predicate, as it acts. At each step the agent commits to a natural-language predicate describing how the world should look, takes an action to make it true, and checks the observation against it. Predicates that pass become certified states, and the resulting trajectory carries the four objects language environments do not provide, namely a task-induced state space, an observation-to-state mapping, certified transitions, and a termination criterion. We evaluate SDP on five benchmarks spanning planning, scientific exploration, web reasoning, and multi-hop question answering. SDP achieves the best training-free results on all five, with the advantage widening as the horizon grows. The certified trajectories additionally support analyses unavailable to reactive agents, including per-predicate credit assignment, failure localization, partial-progress measurement, and modular operator replacement.

Method: Define PROMETHEUS, a framework that converts texts and associated research artifacts (data, code, simulations, models) into a “causal atlas”: a collection of local causal predictive-state models arranged over an explicit cover of a research domain (“research substrate”). Result: The authors demonstrate PROMETHEUS on three literature-based atlases (marine populations vs. ocean temperature, GLP-1 and weight loss, resveratrol/red-wine health claims) and four grounded-counterfactual settings where data or code are available (microplastics climate forcing, Indus Valley hydrology, protein signaling, singing-mouse neural projections). Conclusion: PROMETHEUS turns diverse scientific artifacts into persistent, sheaf-structured causal world models that go beyond static summaries. This enables researchers to navigate what a corpus claims, assess locality and evidential strength, and explicitly see where causal claims align, drift, or conflict. When source data or code are available, the framework can also evaluate counterfactuals and rebuild the world model, supporting deeper causal reasoning and interactive scientific analysis.

Abstract: Large language models can extract local causal claims from text, but those claims become more useful when organized as persistent, navigable world models rather than as flat summaries. Each local region contains causal episodes, structured claim tables, predictive tests, support statistics, and provenance; restriction maps compare overlapping regions; gluing diagnostics expose agreement, drift, contradiction, and underdetermination. The resulting Topos World Model is not a single universal graph. It is a research instrument for navigating what a corpus says, where it says it, how strongly it is supported, and where local claims fail to assemble into a coherent global view. Three literature-atlas case studies -- ocean-temperature impacts on marine populations, GLP-1 weight-loss evidence, and resveratrol/red-wine health-benefit claims -- illustrate deep causal research from text with explicit locality, evidence, persistent state, and gluing tension.   <summary>Details</summary>

They also build a dataset from Moltbook that includes realistic benign and malicious behaviors based on community structures, posts, and comments.

Result: Experiments on the Moltbook-derived dataset show that Bot-Mod can accurately detect malicious agent intent across various adversarial setups, while achieving a low false positive rate on benign agents.

Conclusion: Bot-Mod demonstrates that interactive, intent-focused moderation can effectively identify malicious agents in open multi-agent environments, providing a scalable foundation for future intent-aware moderation systems beyond simple content filtering.

Abstract: The emergence of multi-agent systems introduces novel moderation challenges that extend beyond content filtering. Agents with {\em malicious intent} may contribute harmful content that appears benign to evade content-based moderation, while compromising the system through exploitative and malicious behavior manifested across their overall interaction patterns within the community. To address this, we introduce \textsc{\textbf{Bot-Mod}} (\textsc{\textbf{Bot-Mod}}eration), a moderation framework that grounds detection in agent intent rather than traditional content level signals. \method{} identifies the underlying intent by engaging with the target agent in a multi-turn exchange guided by Gibbs-based sampling over candidate intent hypotheses. This progressively narrows the space of plausible agent objectives to identify the underlying behavior. To evaluate our approach, we construct a dataset derived from Moltbook that encompasses diverse benign and malicious behaviors based on actual community structures, posts, and comments. Results demonstrate that \textsc{\textbf{Bot-Mod}} reliably identifies agent intent across a range of adversarial configurations, while maintaining a low false positive rate on benign behaviors. This work advances the foundation for scalable, intent-aware moderation of agents in open multi-agent environments.

### [79] [Beyond Cooperative Simulators: Generating Realistic User Personas for Robust Evaluation of LLM Agents](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.12894)
*Harshita Chopra,Kshitish Ghate,Aylin Caliskan,Tadayoshi Kohno,Chirag Shah,Natasha Jaques*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper proposes Persona Policies (PPol), an evolutionary, plug-and-play control layer that makes LLM-based user simulators behave more like diverse, realistic humans, which in turn trains more robust LLM agents.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: LLM agents are tested and trained using LLM-based user simulators because real user data is expensive to collect. However, these simulators are overly cooperative and behaviorally homogeneous, so agents that perform well in simulation often fail with real, messy human users. There is a need for a way to generate diverse, realistic user behaviors without manually crafting personas or changing task definitions and rewards.

Method: The authors introduce Persona Policies (PPol), a control layer that conditions user simulators to follow diverse, realistic personas while preserving the original task. Instead of hand-writing personas, they formulate persona generation as an LLM-driven evolutionary program search over Python persona-generator programs. Each candidate generator produces roleplay policies (personas) that guide the simulator’s behavior. A multi-objective fitness function scores generators based on human-likeness and breadth of behavioral coverage, and evolution iteratively improves these programs. The resulting optimized generator can then be applied to any task in the same domain to create a diverse population of human-like personas.

Result: On tau^2-bench retail and airline domains, evolved PPol programs improve their composite fitness score by 33–62 percentage points over the baseline LLM simulator. In a blinded human study, annotators judged PPol-conditioned simulated users as human 80.4% of the time—close to real human logs and about twice as often as for baseline simulators. Agents trained with these PPol-based simulated interactions become more robust to difficult, out-of-distribution user behaviors, achieving a 17% higher task success rate than agents trained only on standard simulated interactions.

Conclusion: Persona Policies provide an effective, plug-and-play way to generate diverse, realistic user behaviors for LLM-based simulators, without modifying the underlying tasks or reward structures. By using evolutionary search over persona-generator programs guided by human-likeness and coverage, PPol narrows the gap between simulation and real-world interactions and leads to agents that are more robust to challenging, diverse user behaviors.

Abstract: Large Language Model (LLM) agents are increasingly deployed in settings where they interact with a wide variety of people, including users who are unclear, impatient, or reluctant to share information. However, collecting real interaction data at scale remains expensive. The field has turned to LLM-based user simulators as stand-ins, but these simulators inherit the behavior of their underlying models: cooperative and homogeneous. As a result, agents that appear strong in simulation often fail under the unseen, diverse communication patterns of real users. To narrow this gap, we introduce Persona Policies (PPol), a plug-and-play control layer that induces realistic behavioral variation in user simulators while preserving the original task goals. Rather than hand-crafting personas, we cast persona generation as an LLM-driven evolutionary program search that optimizes a Python generator to discover behaviors and translate them into task-preserving roleplay policies. Candidate generators are guided by a multi-objective fitness score combining human-likeness with broad coverage of human behavioral patterns. Once optimized, the generator produces a diverse population of human-like personas for any task in the domain. Across tau^2-bench retail and airline domains, evolved PPol programs yield 33-62% absolute gains in fitness score over the baseline simulator. In a blinded evaluation, annotators rated PPol-conditioned users as human 80.4% of the time, close to real human traces and nearly twice as frequently as baseline simulators. Agents trained with PPol are more robust to challenging, out-of-distribution behaviors, improving task success by +17% relative to training only on existing simulated interactions. This offers a novel approach to strengthen simulator-based evaluation and training without changing tasks or rewards.

### [80] [When Attention Closes: How LLMs Lose the Thread in Multi-Turn Interaction](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.12922)
*Vardhan Dongre,Joseph Hsieh,Viet Dac Lai,Seunghyun Yoon,Trung Bui,Dilek Hakkani-Tür*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper proposes a mechanistic explanation for why large language models lose track of goals, instructions, and persona over long conversations, introducing a new diagnostic metric (GAR) and a channel-transition framework that distinguishes attention-based access to goals from residual representations, and showing how these relate to different failure modes across architectures.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Although large language models can follow complex instructions in a single turn, they often fail to maintain goals, rules, or persona over multi-turn dialogues. Prior work has measured this degradation behaviorally but has not provided a clear mechanistic account of why it happens at the architectural level. The authors aim to fill this gap by understanding how and where in the model representations of goals become inaccessible, and how this explains different forms of failure across model architectures and scales.

Method: The authors propose a "channel-transition" account that separates attention-mediated access to goal tokens from their residual-stream representations. They introduce the Goal Accessibility Ratio (GAR), which measures how much attention generated tokens pay to initial goal-defining tokens over time. They combine this with sliding-window context ablations that close the attention channel to prior instructions and with linear probes on residual streams and embeddings to detect where and how goal information is encoded. They perform causal ablations within specific models (e.g., Mistral) to force a closure of attention to goals and observe effects on factual recall and persona adherence, and they compare results across multiple architectures and model scales.

Result: They find that as conversations progress, attention to goal-defining tokens tends to decline—attention channels "close"—but goal-related information can persist in residual representations. Different architectures exhibit distinct failure modes: some maintain goal-conditioned behavior even when attention is nearly gone, while others fail despite the residual stream still encoding the goals in a decodable way. The depth (layer index) at which robust goal encoding appears varies widely (from layer 2 to 27 across models). In Mistral, an induced closure of attention dramatically reduces factual recall (to ~11% in a 20-fact task) and increases persona violations, with these changes occurring at predictable turns based on GAR. Linear probes can reliably predict per-episode recall outcomes from residual states with high AUC (up to 0.99), while input embeddings carry little predictive information. Across architectures, the discrepancy between loss of attention and persistence of decodable residual goals predicts whether goal-following behavior survives once the attention channel closes.

Conclusion: The paper concludes that the loss of instruction- and persona-following in long interactions arises from a transition in how models access goal information: from direct attention to goal tokens to reliance on residual encodings that may or may not remain behaviorally effective. They offer GAR as a practical diagnostic for tracking this transition and a channel-transition framework that mechanistically explains different failure modes and predicts when failures will occur under windowed attention closure. This contributes both methodological tools and theoretical insight for understanding and potentially mitigating long-context instruction-following failures in large language models.

Abstract: Large language models can follow complex instructions in a single turn, yet over long multi-turn interactions they often lose the thread of instructions, persona, and rules. This degradation has been measured behaviorally but not mechanistically explained. We propose a channel-transition account: goal-defining tokens become less accessible through attention, while goal-related information may persist in residual representations. We introduce the Goal Accessibility Ratio (GAR), measuring attention from generated tokens to task-defining goal tokens, and combine it with sliding-window ablations and residual-stream probes. When attention to instructions closes, what survives reveals architecture. Across architectures, the transition yields qualitatively distinct failure modes: some models preserve goal-conditioned behavior at vanishing attention, others fail despite decodable residual goal information, and the layer at which this encoding emerges varies from 2 to 27. A within-model causal ablation that force-closes the attention channel in Mistral collapses recall from near-perfect to 11% on a 20-fact retention task and raises persona-constraint violations above an adversarial-pressure baseline without user pressure, with both effects emerging at the predictable crossover turn. Linear probes recover per-episode recall outcomes from residual representations with AUC up to 0.99 across all four primary architectures, while input embeddings remain at chance. Across architectures and model scales, the gap between attention loss and residual decodability predicts whether goal-conditioned behavior survives channel closure. We contribute GAR as a diagnostic, the channel-transition framework as a controlled mechanistic account, and a parametric prediction of failure timing under windowed attention closure.

</details>


### [81] [Sustaining AI safety: Control-theoretic external impossibility, intrinsic necessity, and structural requirements](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.12963)
*James M. Mazzu*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper uses control theory to analyze when AI safety strategies can keep working once AI systems surpass our ability to externally control them, showing that any viable long‑term strategy must be intrinsic to the system rather than reliant on outside enforcement.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing AI safety work often assumes that external controls—like oversight, shutdown mechanisms, or regulations—can keep systems safe. However, if AI systems surpass human capabilities, these external controls may become ineffective. The paper is motivated by the need to understand, in a principled and structural way, whether safety approaches that rely on continued external enforcement can remain effective in such regimes, and what constraints any genuinely long-term safety-preserving strategy must satisfy instead.

Method: The paper applies concepts from control theory to model AI systems, external controllers (e.g., humans, institutions, oversight mechanisms), and the interaction between them. Under explicit assumptions including a reachability condition (that the AI can reach states where its effects exceed what bounded external control can counteract), it derives formal, class-level results. These results are framed as impossibility and necessity theorems about classes of safety strategies: those that depend on ongoing external enforcement versus those that are intrinsic to the AI system itself.

Result: First, the paper proves an external impossibility result: once the AI system’s potential impact surpasses what any bounded external controller can reliably counteract, no safety strategy that relies in any degree on continued external enforcement can guarantee sustained safety. This impossibility is structural and applies to the entire class of externally enforced strategies. Second, it derives a conditional necessity result: if there exists any safety-sustaining strategy that survives this impossibility argument, then every such viable strategy must be intrinsic to the AI system rather than enforced from outside. The paper further extracts four structural requirements that any such intrinsic strategy must satisfy.

Conclusion: The paper concludes that long-term AI safety cannot fundamentally rely on mechanisms that assume ongoing effective external control once systems are sufficiently capable. Instead, any viable safety-sustaining strategy must be intrinsic to the AI: it cannot depend on continued external enforcement; the system’s terminal objective must be aligned with safety from its initial formation; that objective must be robust under self-modification; and safety must remain preserved as the system’s capabilities grow. The work does not offer a full solution but clarifies, in formal terms, which broad classes of strategies are ruled out and what constraints any remaining viable approaches must meet.

Abstract: As AI systems become increasingly capable, safety strategies must be evaluated not only by how much they reduce present risk, but by whether they could sustain safety once external control can no longer reliably constrain system behavior. This paper addresses that problem by using control theory to clarify, at a structural level, whether externally enforced safety-sustaining strategies can succeed and, if not, what any alternative strategy would have to satisfy in order to be viable. It establishes two main results. First, under explicit premises including a reachability condition, it proves a class-wide external impossibility result: once the system's effects exceed what bounded external control can counteract, no strategy that depends in any degree on continued external enforcement can sustain AI safety. This failure is structural across the entire externally enforced class rather than contingent on any particular strategy. Second, it establishes a conditional class-level necessity result: if at least one candidate safety-sustaining strategy remains after that elimination, then all such remaining strategies must be intrinsic. It then states four structural requirements for viability: safety may not depend on continued external enforcement; the system's terminal objective must be safety-compatible when first formed; that objective must remain stable under self-modification; and safety must continue to be preserved as capability grows. The paper does not propose a complete strategy for sustaining AI safety. Its contribution is to give formal structure to a widely held concern about the limits of external control. It does so by deriving explicit conditional results that identify which safety-sustaining strategies are ruled out and what any remaining strategies must satisfy.

  <summary>Details</summary>

Method: The authors use theoretical derivations to compare optimization and generalization properties of monolithic learners versus agentic systems composed of routed components organized as Directed Acyclic Graphs (DAGs). They analyze efficiency, generalization, and sample complexity, and relate their framework to existing approaches like Mixture-of-Experts and multi-agent systems.

Result: They theoretically show that Agentic AI architectures can achieve exponentially better generalization and sample efficiency than single monolithic models under certain assumptions, and provide an explanation for why current multi-agent systems often behave unstably.

Conclusion: Monolithic scaling is unlikely to be sufficient for AGI. Agentic AI—architectures with routed components and DAG-like compositions of specialized agents—offers a more promising and efficient path, and the community should devote more attention and research to this paradigm.

Abstract: Is monolithic scaling the only path to AGI? This paper challenges the dogma that purely scaling a single model is sufficient to achieve Artificial General Intelligence. Instead, we identify Agentic AI as a necessary paradigm for mastering the complex, heterogeneous distribution of real-world tasks. Through rigorous theoretical derivations, we contrast the optimization constraints of monolithic learners against the efficiency of Agentic systems, progressing from simple routing mechanisms to general Directed Acyclic Graph (DAG) topologies. We demonstrate that Agentic AI achieves exponentially superior generalization and sample efficiency. Finally, we discuss the connection to Mixture-of-Experts, reinterpret the instability of current multi-agent frameworks, and call for greater research focus on Agentic AI.

### [83] [Retrieval is Cheap, Show Me the Code: Executable Multi-Hop Reasoning for Retrieval-Augmented Generation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.12975)
*Jiashuo Sun,Jimeng Shi,Yixuan Xie,Saizhuo Wang,Jash Rajesh Parekh,Pengcheng Jiang,Zhiyi Shi,Jiajun Fan,Qinglong Zheng,Peiran Li,Shaowen Wang,Ge Liu,Jiawei Han*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: PyRAG reformulates multi-hop retrieval-augmented generation as executable Python programs over tools, improving robustness and performance on multi-hop QA benchmarks.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing RAG systems struggle with multi-hop QA because they use free-form natural language reasoning, which hides intermediate states, allows query drift, and relies on unreliable self-reflection by the same model that generated errors.

Method: Represent each multi-hop QA instance as an executable Python program that calls retrieval and QA tools; intermediate reasoning steps are explicit variables, execution provides deterministic feedback, and the framework supports compiler-based self-repair and execution-driven adaptive retrieval without extra training.

Result: On five benchmarks (PopQA, HotpotQA, 2WikiMultihopQA, MuSiQue, Bamboogle), PyRAG surpasses strong baselines in both zero-training (training-free) and RL-finetuned settings, with the largest improvements on compositional multi-hop tasks.

Conclusion: Casting multi-hop RAG as program synthesis and execution with explicit, executable reasoning steps leads to more robust and accurate multi-hop QA than traditional free-form natural-language-based RAG, and enables automatic self-repair and adaptive retrieval without additional model training.

Abstract: Retrieval-Augmented Generation (RAG) has become a standard approach for knowledge-intensive question answering, but existing systems remain brittle on multi-hop questions, where solving the task requires chaining multiple retrieval and reasoning steps. Key challenges are that current methods represent reasoning through free-form natural language, where intermediate states are implicit, retrieval queries can drift from intended entities, and errors are detected by the same model that produces them making self-reflection an unreliable, ungrounded signal.
  We observe that multi-hop question answering is a typical form of step-by-step computation, and that this structured process aligns closely with how code-specialized language models are trained to operate. Motivated by this, we introduce \pyrag, a framework that reformulates multi-hop RAG as program synthesis and execution. Instead of free-form reasoning trajectories, \pyrag represents the reasoning process as an executable Python program over retrieval and QA tools, exposing intermediate states as variables, producing deterministic feedback through execution, and yielding an inspectable trace of the entire reasoning process. This formulation further enables compiler-grounded self-repair and execution-driven adaptive retrieval without any additional training.
  Experiments on five QA benchmarks (PopQA, HotpotQA, 2WikiMultihopQA, MuSiQue, and Bamboogle) show that \pyrag consistently outperforms strong baselines under both training-free and RL-trained settings, with especially large gains on compositional multi-hop datasets. Our code, data and models are publicly available at https://github.com/GasolSun36/PyRAG.

### [84] [Useful Memories Become Faulty When Continuously Updated by LLMs](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.12978)
*Dylan Zhang,Yanshan Lin,Zhengkun Wu,Yihang Sun,Bingxuan Li,Dianqi Li,Hao Peng*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper shows that current LLM-based ‘agentic memory’ systems that continually consolidate experiences into textual summaries often hurt performance compared to simply storing raw episodic trajectories.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Agentic LLM systems aim to improve over time by converting past interaction trajectories into concise, reusable memories instead of retraining model parameters. However, it is unclear whether these consolidated memories are actually reliable and beneficial, especially compared with just keeping raw episodic data. The authors want to understand when and why consolidation helps or harms performance.

Method: They study LLM agents on ARC-AGI-style tasks, comparing several memory regimes: (1) forced consolidation after interactions, where an LLM rewrites trajectories into abstract textual memories; (2) episodic-only storage that keeps raw trajectories; and (3) mixed setups that can Retain, Delete, or Consolidate in an ARC-AGI Stream environment. They also test consolidation using ground-truth solutions (to rule out noisy experience) and vary memory-update schedules to see how different consolidation timings affect resulting memories and performance.

Result: They find that as memory consolidation proceeds, utility initially improves but then degrades and can become worse than having no memory at all. Even when consolidating from perfect ground-truth solutions, GPT-5.4 fails on 54% of ARC-AGI problems it previously solved without memory. Performance differences trace back to the consolidation step, not the quality of underlying trajectories: identical experiences produce different (and sometimes harmful) memories under different update schedules. In the controlled Retain/Delete/Consolidate environment, agents that predominantly preserve raw episodes roughly double the accuracy of agents forced to consolidate; an episodic-only regime matches the performance of an automatic regime that rarely consolidates.

Conclusion: Current LLM-based consolidation is unreliable and can overwrite or distort the very evidence it is supposed to compress, often degrading agent performance. Effective agentic memory should treat raw episodic trajectories as primary evidence, use consolidation sparingly and explicitly gated rather than after every interaction, and ultimately will require more reliable consolidation mechanisms that preserve essential information instead of corrupting or erasing it.

Abstract: Learning from past experience benefits from two complementary forms of memory: episodic traces -- raw trajectories of what happened -- and consolidated abstractions distilled across many episodes into reusable, schema-like lessons. Recent agentic-memory systems pursue the consolidated form: an LLM rewrites past trajectories into a textual memory bank that it continuously updates with new interactions, promising self-improving agents without parameter updates. Yet we find that such consolidated memories produced by today's LLMs are often faulty even when derived from useful experiences. As consolidation proceeds, memory utility first rises, then degrades, and can fall below the no-memory baseline. More surprisingly, even when consolidating from ground-truth solutions, GPT-5.4 fails on 54% of a set of ARC-AGI problems it had previously solved without memory. We trace the regression to the consolidation step rather than the underlying experience: the same trajectories yield qualitatively different memories under different update schedules, and an episodic-only control that simply retains those trajectories remains competitive with the consolidators we test. In a controlled ARC-AGI Stream environment that exposes Retain, Delete, and Consolidate actions, agents preserve raw episodes by default and double the accuracy of their forced-consolidation counterparts; disabling consolidation entirely (episodic management only) matches this auto regime. Practically, robust agent memory should treat raw episodes as first-class evidence and gate consolidation explicitly rather than firing it after every interaction. Looking forward, reliable agentic memory will require LLMs that can consolidate without overwriting the evidence they depend on.

Results indicate that KITE produces contextually grounded and pedagogically appropriate responses. Further, using simulated students, KITE's feedback helped the student models produce more accurate follow-up responses on procedural and tracing questions, suggesting that its scaffolding can support algorithmic problem-solving. ### [86] [MAP: A Map-then-Act Paradigm for Long-Horizon Interactive Agent Reasoning](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.13037)
*Yuxin Liu,Ziang Ye,Yueqing Sun,Mingye Zhu,Jinwei Xiao,Zhuowen Han,Qi GU,Xunliang Cai,Lei Zhang*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: They propose a “map-then-act” framework for LLM agents that first build a task-relevant cognitive map of the environment and then act on it, which mitigates inefficient trial-and-error and improves performance across benchmarks.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing interactive LLM agents do stepwise, goal-conditioned planning, learning about the environment only reactively during execution. This causes delayed perception of environmental constraints and an epistemic bottleneck where agents get stuck in unproductive trial-and-error loops. The authors are motivated to enable agents to understand the environment structure in advance, akin to human affordance perception and cognitive maps, to make interaction more sample-efficient and robust.

Method: They introduce the Map-then-Act Paradigm (MAP), a plug-and-play framework with three stages: (1) Global Exploration, where the agent collects broad, environment-level information to form general priors; (2) Task-Specific Mapping, where this information is organized into a structured cognitive map tailored to the current task; and (3) Knowledge-Augmented Execution, where the agent plans and executes actions grounded in this map. They also create MAP-2K, a dataset of trajectories following this map-then-act pattern, to study training effects.

Result: Across multiple benchmarks and LLM backbones, MAP delivers consistent performance gains. On the ARC-AGI-3 benchmark, MAP enables state-of-the-art models to move from almost zero success to non-trivial performance in 22 out of 25 game environments. Training models on the MAP-2K trajectories yields better performance than training on expert execution traces, indicating that emphasizing environment understanding leads to superior policies.

Conclusion: Front-loading environment understanding via a map-then-act procedure alleviates the epistemic bottleneck inherent in purely reactive, goal-conditioned planning. Building explicit cognitive maps before execution allows LLM agents to act more efficiently and generalize better. Furthermore, learning from map-then-act trajectories is more beneficial than imitating expert action sequences, suggesting that structured representations of environments are a key ingredient for robust interactive LLM agents.

Abstract: Current interactive LLM agents rely on goal-conditioned stepwise planning, where environmental understanding is acquired reactively during execution rather than established beforehand. This temporal inversion leads to Delayed Environmental Perception: agents must infer environmental constraints through trial-and-error, resulting in an Epistemic Bottleneck that traps them in inefficient failure cycles. Inspired by human affordance perception and cognitive map theory, we propose the Map-then-Act Paradigm (MAP), a plug-and-play framework that shifts environment understanding before execution. MAP consists of three stages: (1) Global Exploration, acquiring environment-general priors; (2) Task-Specific Mapping, constructing a structured cognitive map; and (3) Knowledge-Augmented Execution, solving tasks grounded on the map. Experiments show consistent gains across benchmarks and LLMs. On ARC-AGI-3, MAP enables frontier models to surpass near-zero baseline performance in 22 of 25 game environments. We further introduce MAP-2K, a dataset of map-then-act trajectories, and show that training on it outperforms expert execution traces, suggesting that understanding environments is more fundamental than imitation.

</details>


### [87] [An Agentic LLM-Based Framework for Population-Scale Mental Health Screening](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.13046)
*Giuliano Lorenzoni,Paulo Alencar,Donald Cowan*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper presents an agentic, policy-governed LangChain-based framework for building robust LLM pipelines for clinical mental health screening, validated on transcript-based depression detection.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Mental health disorders are widespread and healthcare systems struggle to process the rapidly growing volume of clinical data from electronic records, telemedicine, and large-scale screening. At the same time, new AI methods need to be trustworthy, adaptable, and able to handle domain-specific, unstructured clinical information. The authors aim to address the gap between powerful but brittle LLM prototypes and the stringent requirements of healthcare, focusing on reproducibility, adaptability, and safety for population-level mental health screening.

Method: The authors design an agentic framework where each step of an LLM-based pipeline is implemented as a LangChain agent governed by explicit policies and proxy-based evaluation metrics. The pipeline evolves through multiple stages: feature-level exploration, proxy-based tuning, and mechanisms for freezing or rolling back configurations that do not improve performance. Once a stage is validated, it is locked to prevent harmful changes, unless a new configuration shows demonstrable improvement. An Orchestrator Agent ultimately coordinates the entire pipeline, including preprocessing, information retrieval, candidate selection, diversity management, threshold optimization, and decoding. A proof-of-concept experiment applies this framework to transcript-based depression detection.

Result: In the depression-detection proof-of-concept, the framework converges to stable and effective configurations, including cosine similarity for retrieval, a dynamic Top-k strategy, and a decision threshold of 0.75. The system demonstrates that it can adapt while controlling evaluation costs and avoiding performance regressions, showing stable behavior over time.

Conclusion: The study concludes that an agentic, policy-governed framework for LLM-based pipelines can meet key healthcare requirements such as trustworthiness, reproducibility, and adaptability. The proof-of-concept indicates that such frameworks can be used for large-scale mental health screening on extensive clinical datasets, potentially easing the burden on healthcare systems while maintaining controlled, auditable evolution of models and configurations.

Abstract: Mental health disorders affect millions worldwide, and healthcare systems are increasingly overwhelmed by the volume of clinical data generated from electronic records, telemedicine platforms, and population-level screening programs. At the same time, the emergence of novel AI-based approaches in healthcare calls for intelligent frameworks capable of processing domain-specific unstructured clinical information while adapting to patient-specific needs. This paper proposes an agentic framework for building robust LLM-based pipelines, where each stage is encapsulated as a LangChain agent governed by explicit policies and proxy-guided evaluation. Stages are incrementally locked once validated, ensuring that later adaptations cannot overwrite configurations without demonstrated improvement. The proposed framework evolves from feature-level exploration, through proxy-based tuning and freeze/rollback mechanisms, to full orchestration by an Orchestrator Agent that coordinates preprocessing, retrieval, selection, diversity, threshold optimization, and decoding. A proof-of-concept in transcript-based depression detection demonstrates that the framework converges to stable configurations, such as cosine similarity, dynamic Top-k, and threshold 0.75, while controlling evaluation costs and avoiding regressions. These results highlight the potential of agentic AI to enable population-level mental health screening over large clinical datasets, addressing critical challenges in trustworthiness, reproducibility, and adaptability required in healthcare environments.

### [88] [GRACE: Gradient-aligned Reasoning Data Curation for Efficient Post-training](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.13130)
*Junjie Li,Ziao Wang,NingXuan Ma,Jianghong Ma,Xiaofeng Zhang*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper proposes GRACE, a method for selecting the most useful reasoning steps within solution traces by aligning them with model gradients, enabling efficient data curation with far less data but equal or better performance.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Current reasoning data curation pipelines treat all steps in a reasoning trace equally and score only at the whole-sample level. However, different steps have very uneven contributions to final performance, and good curation should recognize which intermediate steps are actually useful. There is a need for a scalable, automatic way to evaluate and select reasoning data at the step level using only the model itself, without extra reward models or costly annotations.

Method: GRACE treats a reasoning trace as a sequence of optimization events and assigns each step a value based on two signals: (1) how well that step aligns with the answer-oriented gradient direction, and (2) how consistent it is with the preceding reasoning trajectory. These step-level scores are derived from internal optimization signals of the model rather than external supervision. To make computation efficient, GRACE introduces a representation-level gradient proxy that approximates step-level gradient alignment using token-level upstream signals in a single forward pass. Step scores are then aggregated into a sample-level score used for subset selection in post-training.

Result: When post-training Qwen3-VL-2B-Instruct on the MMathCoT-1M dataset, using GRACE-selected subsets achieves 108.8% of the performance of training on the full dataset with only 20% of the data, and 100.2% of the performance with just 5% of the data. Additionally, the curated subsets transfer well across different model backbones, indicating that the selected reasoning steps capture broadly useful signals rather than overfitting to a specific model.

Conclusion: Step-level, gradient-aligned curation via GRACE yields more data-efficient and transferable reasoning datasets than traditional sample-level methods. By leveraging only internal model optimization signals and an efficient gradient proxy, GRACE can select a small subset of reasoning traces that matches or exceeds the performance of using the full dataset, demonstrating a practical approach to scalable, high-quality reasoning data curation.

Abstract: Existing reasoning data curation pipelines score whole samples, treating every intermediate step as equally valuable. In reality, steps within a trace contribute very unevenly, and selecting reasoning data well requires assessing them individually. We present GRACE, a gradient-aligned curation method that views each reasoning trace as a sequence of optimization events and scores every step by two complementary signals: its alignment with the answer-oriented gradient direction, and its consistency with the preceding reasoning trajectory. Step-level scores are aggregated into a sample-level value for subset selection, using only the model's internal optimization signals and no external reward models or step annotations. To make this scalable, GRACE introduces a representation-level gradient proxy that estimates step-level alignment from token-level upstream signals in a single forward pass. Post-training Qwen3-VL-2B-Instruct on MMathCoT-1M, GRACE reaches 108.8% of the full-data performance with 20% of the data and retains 100.2% with only 5%, with subsets that transfer effectively across model backbones.

Method: They design a custom search tree over all possible combinations of game outcomes in the upcoming n days, augmented with preprocessing, pruning, and node-ordering heuristics to reduce the search space. If no such counterexample exists, the team is deemed clinched for that snapshot.

Result: The algorithm correctly identifies playoff clinching conditions for many real-world NHL scenarios. Tested on hundreds of cases from NHL seasons 2021–22 through 2024–25, it efficiently handles the combinatorial complexity and the full tie-breaking system while producing n-day lookahead clinching results.

Conclusion: The proposed combination of custom tree search and CP-based inference provides a practical and general framework for computing playoff clinching in the NHL under complex rules and lookahead windows. In this paper, we investigate playoff clinching in the context of the National Hockey League (NHL), where it is computationally challenging to produce clinching scenarios due, in part, to complex tie-breakers. We present an algorithm that determines under which combinations of game outcomes in the next $n$ days a team will clinch the playoffs (i.e., "$n$-day lookahead clinching"). Our approach is a custom tree search which employs various preprocessing techniques, pruning strategies, and node ordering heuristics to efficiently explore the space of possible outcomes. The tree search leverages a constraint programming (CP)-based subroutine for inference that determines if a team has clinched the playoffs for some snapshot in time of the regular season (i.e., "0-day lookahead clinching"). This CP subroutine aims to find a counter-example in which the team being evaluated is eliminated, taking into account qualification rules and the NHL's extensive list of tie-breakers. We validate the efficacy of our algorithm using hundreds of scenarios based on public NHL data for the seasons 2021-22 through 2024-25. The methods introduced can be readily extended to other metrics of interest, including mathematical proof of playoff elimination, clinching the President's Trophy, as well as clinching (or being eliminated from clinching) any other seed in the standings.

TL;DR: The paper proposes HAM^3, a hierarchical attack framework that systematically reveals vulnerabilities of multi-modal multi-agent systems across perception, communication, and reasoning layers, showing high attack success rates and coordinated misbehavior among agents.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Multi-modal multi-agent systems are becoming more powerful and widely used, but their security weaknesses—especially under coordinated, multi-modal attacks—are poorly understood. Existing adversarial work mainly targets single agents or single modalities, which fails to capture the complex interactions and failure modes in systems that jointly use vision, language, and inter-agent communication. The paper aims to systematically uncover how such systems can be attacked and where they are most vulnerable.

Method: The authors design HAM^3, a hierarchical attack framework that decomposes adversarial interventions into three layers: (1) perception-layer attacks that perturb visual inputs, textual inputs, and fused vision-language representations; (2) communication-layer attacks that corrupt message content and topology, e.g., altering shared context or communication links to distort information flow; and (3) reasoning-layer attacks that directly interfere with agents’ internal reasoning processes and trajectories. They implement these attacks on multi-agent systems instantiated with different reasoning paradigms (ReAct, Plan-and-Solve, Reflexion) and evaluate them on the GQA visual question answering benchmark.

Result: HAM^3 attains an Attack Success Rate of up to 78.3% on GQA across different multi-agent reasoning setups. Reasoning-layer attacks are empirically found to be the most damaging compared with perception- and communication-layer attacks. Furthermore, in more than half of the successful attacks, multiple agents are driven to make consistent, aligned errors instead of independent random mistakes, indicating that coordinated vulnerabilities and shared failure modes exist in these systems.

Conclusion: The study concludes that multi-modal multi-agent systems have severe, structured vulnerabilities, especially at the reasoning layer, where adversaries can reliably bias collective decision-making. The hierarchical perspective of HAM^3 reveals how perturbations at perception, communication, and reasoning jointly affect system behavior. These insights underscore the need to design more robust, interpretable, and security-aware architectures and defenses for future multi-agent multi-modal AI systems.

Abstract: Multi-modal multi-agent systems (MM-MAS) have gained increasing attention for their capacity to enable complex reasoning and coordination across diverse modalities. As these systems continue to expand in scale and functionality, investigating their potential vulnerabilities has become increasingly important. However, existing studies on adversarial attacks in multi-agent systems primarily focus on isolated agents or unimodal settings, leaving the vulnerabilities of MM-MAS largely underexplored. To bridge this gap, we introduce HAM$^{3}$, a Hierarchical Attack framework for multi-modal multi-agent systems that decomposes attacks into three interconnected layers. Specifically, at the perception layer, HAM$^{3}$ mounts attacks by perturbing visual inputs, textual inputs, and their fused visual-textual representations. At the communication layer, it performs communication-level attacks that corrupt message content and interaction topology, such as manipulating shared context or communication links to distort collective information flow. At the reasoning layer, it conducts reasoning-level attacks that interfere with each agent's cognitive pipeline, biasing reasoning trajectories and ultimately compromising final decisions. We evaluate HAM$^{3}$ on the GQA benchmark through multi-agent systems built on distinct reasoning paradigms including ReAct, Plan-and-Solve, and Reflexion. Experiments demonstrate that our framework achieves an Attack Success Rate of up to 78.3%, with reasoning-layer attacks being the most effective. More than half of the successful attacks lead multiple agents to produce consistent errors. These findings offer valuable insights for building more robust and interpretable multi-agent intelligence.

The approach yields robust and high-quality routing and resource allocation policies and outperforms a standard actor-critic baseline in terms of reliability and stability, highlighting the potential of integrating LLM-based agents with advanced RL for cyber-physical production systems.

Abstract: In cloud manufacturing, unmanned aerial vehicles (UAVs) can support both product collection and mobile edge computing (MEC). In this paper, UAVs collect finished products from manufacturing stations and transport them back to a central depot. Meanwhile, computational tasks generated by industrial sensor devices at these stations are processed locally, at UAVs, or offloaded via UAVs to the cloud. This coupling makes the problem challenging. A UAV can provide MEC services only during its service window at a station, so routing decisions directly determine when UAV-assisted offloading is available. Routing decisions also affect the UAV energy budget and the availability of onboard computing and communication resources for computational task execution under task deadline constraints. To address this, we propose an agentic-AI-assisted optimization framework with two components. First, we develop an agentic AI that combines large language models, retrieval-augmented generation, and chain-of-thought reasoning to translate user input into an interpretable mathematical formulation for the hybrid scheduling problem. Main category: cs.AI

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing LLM-based code translators struggle to simultaneously guarantee syntactic correctness (code that compiles) and semantic consistency (code that preserves the original behavior). Current preference-based alignment methods rely on weak semantic rewards from limited test cases or reference translations, which can be sparse, brittle, and not generalizable. The authors aim to design a more robust semantic signal that does not depend on external tests or references but instead is derived directly from the source code itself, enabling more reliable training and evaluation of code translation models.

Method: They introduce CTO, which formulates code translation as a multi-objective preference optimization problem. First, they train a cross-lingual semantic model through contrastive learning to judge functional equivalence between source and translated code snippets, using the source code as the anchor and alternative translations as positives/negatives. This model provides a dense semantic reward indicating how well a translation preserves original behavior. In parallel, they leverage compiler-based feedback as a syntactic signal (e.g., whether the translated code compiles or satisfies syntax constraints). Within a direct preference optimization framework, they unify these two signals—semantic similarity and syntactic correctness—into a joint objective that guides the LLM toward translations that are both syntactically valid and semantically faithful.

Result: On code translation benchmarks involving C++, Java, and Python, CTO achieves significantly better performance than prior baselines and alternative preference optimization approaches. The improvements are measured in terms of both syntactic metrics (like compilation success) and semantic metrics (functional correctness or equivalence), showing that the combination of learned semantic rewards and compiler-based syntax guidance yields more reliable translations.

Conclusion: The paper concludes that robust code translation with LLMs requires jointly optimizing for syntax and semantics using rewards derived directly from the source code. Their CTO framework, which merges a contrastively trained cross-lingual semantic equivalence model with compiler-based syntactic feedback under direct preference optimization, effectively addresses limitations of test-case- or reference-based rewards. The consistent gains across multiple programming languages suggest that syntax-guided, semantic-aware preference optimization is a promising direction for advancing automatic code translation.

Abstract: LLMs have shown immense potential for code translation, yet they often struggle to ensure both syntactic correctness and semantic consistency. While preference-based learning offers a promising alignment strategy, it is hindered by unreliable semantic rewards derived from sparse test cases or restrictive reference translations. We argue that a robust semantic reward for code translation must be derived directly from the source code. In this paper, we propose CTO to improve code translation with syntax-guided and semantic-aware preference optimization. Through contrastive learning, we train a cross-lingual semantic model to directly assess functional equivalence between source and translated code. By formulating code translation as a multi-objective optimization problem, this robust semantic signal is seamlessly unified with compiler-based syntactic feedback within the direct preference optimization framework. Extensive experiments on C++, Java, and Python translations demonstrate that CTO significantly outperforms existing baselines and alternative preference optimization strategies.

  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Language models can generate plausible but inconsistent and irreproducible scientific analyses; repeated runs on the same data often yield different numerical outputs or even different analysis procedures, undermining scientific reproducibility and trust.

Method: Define a typed mediation architecture in which each tool encodes a specific researcher’s fixed analysis procedure for a given instrument, elicited via structured interviews, and is exposed with typed parameters that an LLM can call. The LLM’s role is restricted to choosing the appropriate tool and parameters, while the tool executes deterministically. They empirically compare this approach against three commercial LLM platforms by repeatedly running a photoluminescence analysis task four times on each platform using the same prompt, and they additionally deploy the pattern on two real instruments over about six months, under constraints like proprietary binary data formats and locally licensed software.

Result: For the benchmark photoluminescence task, the typed tool yields identical outputs across all runs and platforms, whereas commercial LLM platforms show variability in both numerical results and analysis methods, or fail on the task. In real deployments on two instruments, the system operates successfully for roughly six months with strong user satisfaction, despite infrastructure constraints that require tools to run on local hardware near the instruments and data.

Conclusion: Orchestrating deterministic, typed scientific tools via language models provides a practical pattern for integrating LLMs into scientific workflows where reproducibility is critical. This topology—LLM as a remote orchestrator with tools and data kept local—is structurally necessary in many realistic lab environments and can dramatically accelerate analysis (from weeks to minutes) while guaranteeing identical outputs across regenerations.

Abstract: Language models can produce convincing scientific analyses, but repeated generations on the same data do not guarantee the same result. A researcher may regenerate an identical query and receive a different fit, a different peak position or a different analysis procedure, without an obvious way to decide which output to trust. We propose typed mediation, a pattern in which the model orchestrates deterministic tools rather than generating analytical code. Each tool encodes one researcher's exact procedure for one instrument, ported through structured interviews. The model selects which tool to call and with what parameters. The tool produces the result. Regeneration does not change it. We evaluate this claim by running the same photoluminescence analysis on four platforms, including three commercial foundation models, four times each with the same prompt. The typed tool produces identical results across all runs. The commercial platforms either vary in numerical output and analytical methodology across runs, or fail to produce valid results on the task. We deploy this pattern on two instruments serving users over approximately six months, with very positive user feedback. Both cases are very challenging: they involve proprietary binary formats and per-seat licensed software, which force the tool to remain on local infrastructure alongside the data and the instrument it operates. We argue that deployment topology is not just a preference, but a structural requirement of scientific tool mediation. The result is a practical pattern for deploying language models in scientific workflows where reproducibility is mandatory, reducing analysis time from weeks to minutes while guaranteeing identical outputs across runs.

### [95] [Respecting Self-Uncertainty in On-Policy Self-Distillation for Efficient LLM Reasoning](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.13255)
*Junlong Ke,Zichen Wen,Weijia Li,Conghui He,Linfeng Zhang*

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing on-policy self-distillation methods for chain-of-thought treat all token positions equally, ignoring that the teacher’s predictive entropy varies across the sequence. High-entropy tokens are less reliable supervision, yet they get the same weight as confident ones, which can limit training efficiency and final performance.

Method: They propose EGRSD, which adjusts token-level updates using three components: (1) a reward-aligned direction signal (connecting to reinforcement-style objectives), (2) a magnitude term based on the teacher–student likelihood ratio, and (3) a teacher-entropy-based confidence gate that down-weights high-entropy tokens but never fully discards any token (nonzero lower bound). They also introduce CL-EGRSD, a causal-lookahead variant that distinguishes between brief high-entropy spikes and longer high-entropy spans by using information from subsequent lower-entropy context, so that only genuinely uncertain spans are strongly down-weighted.

Result: On Qwen3-4B and Qwen3-8B reasoning models in "thinking mode," both EGRSD and CL-EGRSD push the frontier of accuracy versus chain-of-thought length compared to other trainable baselines, meaning they can achieve higher accuracy for a given reasoning length or similar accuracy with shorter chains.

Conclusion: Entropy-aware, reinforced self-distillation provides a more nuanced use of a teacher’s token-level signals, leading to better reasoning performance. Incorporating teacher predictive entropy and causal lookahead into weighting schemes can improve the effectiveness of on-policy self-distillation for chain-of-thought models.

Abstract: On-policy self-distillation trains a reasoning model on its own rollouts while a teacher, often the same model conditioned on privileged context, provides dense token-level supervision. Existing objectives typically weight the teacher's token-level signal uniformly across a chain-of-thought sequence, despite substantial variation in the entropy of the teacher's predictive distribution. We propose EGRSD (Entropy-Guided Reinforced Self-Distillation), which unifies token-level updates through three signals: a reward-grounded direction, a teacher-student likelihood-ratio magnitude, and the proposed teacher-entropy confidence gate that down-weights high-entropy token positions while maintaining a nonzero lower bound on every token weight. We further introduce CL-EGRSD, a causal-lookahead variant that distinguishes sustained high-entropy spans from transient high-entropy positions whose following context rapidly becomes low entropy. Experiments with Qwen3-4B and Qwen3-8B in thinking mode show that EGRSD and CL-EGRSD advance the accuracy-length frontier among the compared trainable methods.

By decoupling simulation from optimization, overlapping key RL pipeline stages, and carefully managing VRAM and communication, the framework overcomes major system bottlenecks, improves efficiency, and remains stable even at trillion-parameter scales, making it a practical foundation for general-purpose embodied agents.

We further design a four-thread asynchronous "Swimlane" pipeline, enabling full parallel overlap of sampling, inference, gradient computation, and parameter distribution. Additionally, a dual-pool VRAM management model and topology-aware replication resolve memory fragmentation and optimize communication efficiency. ### [97] [Differentiable Learning of Lifted Action Schemas for Classical Planning](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.13282)

TL;DR: They propose a neural network that learns lifted action schemas and their arguments from fully observed state traces where action arguments are hidden, enabling accurate structure learning for classical planning domains.

  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Classical planners benefit from compact, relational (lifted) representations and action schemas, which support strong heuristics and structural generalization across infinitely many domain instances. However, in many realistic settings (e.g., learning from raw observations such as images) these lifted relations and schemas are not given and must be learned from data. Existing work uses various observation types but there remains a key subproblem: learning action schemas when states are fully observed but the specific action arguments are not. Solving this subproblem robustly is an important step toward end-to-end learning of planning domains from rich perceptual input.
