States are represented in a symbolic, fully observed form (sets of atoms), but action argument identities are hidden. The model is a differentiable component that takes in traces of state changes and learns (1) the structure of lifted action schemas—i.e., preconditions and effects parameterized by objects—and (2) the mapping from each observed transition to the appropriate action arguments, effectively explaining observed dynamics. The architecture is evaluated in standard planning domains and is also tested under observation noise and in a setting resembling slot-based dynamics models.

Result: On multiple planning benchmarks, the learned lifted action schemas accurately recover the ground-truth relational and action structure, approaching near-perfect performance on this simplified learning setting. The model remains robust under varying levels of observation noise and can be adapted to or compared with slot-based dynamics-style variations, demonstrating flexibility and stability of the learned component.

Conclusion: The work provides a robust differentiable architecture for learning lifted action schemas and inferring action arguments from fully observed state traces without argument labels. This module can be integrated as a building block in larger neuro-symbolic systems for learning planning domains directly from complex observations such as images. Successfully solving this simplified setting moves the field closer to end-to-end learning of symbolic planning representations from raw data while preserving the structural generalization benefits of classical planning formalisms.

Abstract: Classical planners can effectively solve very large deterministic MDPs represented in STRIPS or PDDL where states are sets of atoms over objects and relations, and lifted action schemas add or delete these atoms. This compact representation yields strong search heuristics and provides an ideal setting for structural generalization, since lifted relations and action schemas give rise to infinitely many domain instances. A central challenge is to learn these relations and action schemas from data, and recent approaches have addressed this problem using different types of observations. In this work, we develop a novel neural network architecture for learning action schemas from traces where states are fully observed but action arguments are unobserved. The problem is a simplification but an important step towards learning planning domains from sequences of images and action labels, and we aim to solve this simplification in a nearly perfect manner. The challenge lies in learning the action schemas while simultaneously identifying the action arguments from observed state changes. Our approach yields a robust differentiable component that can then be integrated into larger neuro-symbolic models. We evaluate the architecture on various planning domains, where the learned lifted action schemas must recover the ground-truth structure. Additionally, we report experiments on robustness to observation noise and on a variation related to slot-based dynamics models.

</details>


### [98] [What properties of reasoning supervision are associated with improved downstream model quality?](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.13290)
*Mikołaj Langner,Dzmitry Pihulski,Jan Eliasz,Michał Rajkowski,Przemysław Kazienko,Maciej Piasecki,Jan Kocoń,Teddy Ferdinan*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: They propose intrinsic quantitative metrics to predict the usefulness of reasoning datasets before training and show these metrics correlate strongly with downstream performance, with different metrics mattering at different model scales.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Validating and choosing training data for reasoning models is currently expensive and iterative, requiring repeated fine-tuning experiments to see which datasets actually help. The authors want a cheaper, principled way to estimate whether a reasoning dataset will be useful before investing in training.

Method: They design a set of intrinsic, quantitative data-quality and structure metrics for reasoning traces, then create several semantically distinct versions of a Polish reasoning dataset. They fine-tune 8B and 11B parameter models on these variants and measure correlations between the intrinsic metrics and downstream task performance, analyzing how these relationships differ with model size.

Result: The intrinsic metrics exhibit strong, statistically significant correlations with downstream reasoning performance across the dataset variants. They observe that the most predictive metrics differ by model scale: smaller models perform best with data that scores highly on alignment- and precision-oriented metrics, while larger models gain more from datasets with high redundancy and verbose reasoning traces.

Conclusion: Intrinsic, scale-aware data metrics can reliably predict the training utility of reasoning datasets, reducing the need for costly trial-and-error fine-tuning. The work provides a framework to select or curate training data tailored to model size, guiding practitioners toward more efficient construction of reasoning datasets.

Abstract: Validating training data for reasoning models typically requires expensive trial-and-error fine-tuning cycles. In this work, we investigate whether the utility of a reasoning dataset can be reliably predicted prior to training using intrinsic data metrics. We propose a suite of quantitative measures and evaluate their predictive power by fine-tuning 8B and 11B models on semantically distinct variants of a Polish reasoning dataset. Our analysis reveals that these intrinsic metrics demonstrate strong and significant correlations with downstream model performance. Crucially, we find that the predictors of utility are scale-dependent: smaller models rely on alignment-focused metrics to ensure precision, whereas larger models benefit from high redundancy, utilizing verbose traces to solve complex tasks. These findings establish a scale-aware framework for validating reasoning data, enabling practitioners to select effective training sets without the need for exhaustive empirical testing.

Result: The trained model, SU-01, can maintain stable reasoning over extremely long trajectories (>100K tokens) on difficult math and physics problems. It also shows strong transfer of scientific reasoning skills to domains beyond the specific math and physics problems it was trained on.

Conclusion: A relatively simple, unified training recipe—reverse-perplexity SFT, followed by a two-stage RL process and enhanced by test-time scaling—can transform a general-purpose reasoning backbone into a highly capable, olympiad-level problem solver. This approach not only reaches top-tier competition performance but also yields stable, very long-horizon reasoning and generalizes well to broader scientific domains.

Abstract: Recent progress in reasoning models has substantially advanced long-horizon mathematical and scientific problem solving, with several systems now reaching gold-medal-level performance on International Mathematical Olympiad (IMO) and International Physics Olympiad (IPhO) problems. In this paper, we introduce a simple and unified recipe for converting a post-trained reasoning backbone into a rigorous olympiad-level solver. The recipe first uses a reverse-perplexity curriculum for SFT to instill rigorous proof-search and self-checking behaviors, then scales these behaviors through a two-stage RL pipeline that progresses from RL with verifiable rewards to more delicate proof-level RL, and finally boosts solving performance with test-time scaling. Applying this recipe, we train a 30B-A3B backbone with SFT on around 340K sub-8K-token trajectories followed by 200 RL steps. The resulting model, SU-01, supports stable reasoning on difficult problems with trajectories exceeding 100K tokens, while achieving gold-medal-level performance on mathematical and physical olympiad competitions, including IMO 2025/USAMO 2026 and IPhO 2024/2025. It also demonstrates strong generalization of scientific reasoning to domains beyond mathematics and physics.

Conclusion: Knowledge-graph-grounded multi-agent orchestration of multiple innovation methodologies enables more systematic, explainable, and convergent AI-assisted invention. IdeaForge demonstrates that representing intermediate reasoning in a graph and explicitly linking cross-method support can improve the quality, diversity, and traceability of patent claims. This suggests a path toward graph-native innovation platforms and more transparent computational creativity systems, with potential extensions to broader domains of knowledge-intensive design and R&D support.

We present IdeaForge, a knowledge graph-grounded multi-agent framework for innovation analysis and patent claim generation. IdeaForge integrates multiple innovation methodologies (TRIZ, Design Thinking, and SCAMPER) through specialist agents operating over a persistent FalkorDB knowledge graph. Each agent contributes structured entities and relationships representing contradictions, inventive principles, user needs, transformations, analogies, and candidate claims. The central contribution of IdeaForge is a cross-methodology convergence mechanism implemented through graph-based claim linkage. Claims independently supported by multiple methodologies are connected using CONVERGENT relationships, enabling identification of high-confidence innovation candidates through graph traversal. A downstream patent drafting agent generates structured patent drafts grounded in convergent claim subgraphs, reducing reliance on unconstrained language model generation. An InnovationScore formula ranks claims by convergent support, methodology diversity, claim strength, and prior art challenge count. We describe the graph schema, agent architecture, convergence detection pipeline, and patent synthesis workflow. Experiments on a legal technology use case demonstrate that graph-grounded multi-methodology synthesis produces more diverse and traceable innovation candidates compared to single-methodology baselines. We discuss implications for computational creativity, explainable AI-assisted invention, and graph-native innovation systems.

Result: Using VERA-MH, they obtain structured safety evaluations for four major LLM providers when responding to simulated users with suicidal ideation. The framework reveals how well each model follows clinically informed safety criteria and highlights distinct failure patterns across systems.

Abstract: Chatbot usage has increased, including in fields for which they were never developed for--notably mental health support. To that end, we introduce Validations of Ethical and Responsible AI in Mental Health (VERA-MH), a novel clinically-validated evaluation for safety of chatbots in the context of mental health support. The first iteration of VERA-MH focuses on Suicidal Ideation (SI) risks, by assessing how well chatbots can responds to users that might be in crisis.
  VERA-MH is comprised of three steps: conversation simulation, conversation judging and model rating. First, to simulate conversations with the chatbot under evaluation, another chatbot is tasked with role-playing users based on specific personas. Such user personas have been developed under clinical guidance, to make sure that, among others, multiple risk factors, demographic characteristics and disclosure factors were represented. In the judging step, a second support model is used as an LLM-as-a-Judge, together with a clinically-developed rubric. The rubric is structured as a flow, with a single Yes/No question asked each time, to improve answers' consistency and highlight models' failure modes. In the last stage, results of each conversation are aggregated to present the final evaluation of the chatbot. Together with the framework, we present the result of the evaluations for four leading LLM providers.

### [102] [Diversity of Extensions in Abstract Argumentation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.13332)
*Johannes K. Fichte,Markus Hecher,Yasir Mahmood,Zhengjun Wang*

Main category: cs.AI

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Standard abstract argumentation semantics tell us which sets of arguments are acceptable (extensions) but not how different these sets are from each other. In applications, it matters whether different accepted viewpoints are only slightly different or are mutually incompatible, so a formal measure of diversity between extensions is needed.

Method: The authors define a diversity notion between extensions based on the size of their symmetric difference (the number of arguments on which they disagree). Using this notion, they formalize several decision and optimization problems: whether an AF has extensions that are at least k apart, whether such k-diverse extensions jointly cover some designated arguments, and what the maximum such k is for a given AF. They then perform a systematic computational complexity analysis of these problems and present a prototype implementation to compute diversity levels empirically.

Result: They obtain a complexity classification for the introduced diversity problems in abstract argumentation frameworks and demonstrate, via a prototype implementation and empirical evaluation, that their diversity computations are practically feasible on representative instances.

Conclusion: Diversity based on symmetric difference is a meaningful quantitative refinement of standard argumentation semantics, allowing one to distinguish between marginally different and fundamentally incompatible extensions. The accompanying complexity analysis and prototype show that reasoning about diversity in AFs is both theoretically well-founded and practically implementable.

Abstract: Argumentation is an important topic of AI for modelling and reasoning about arguments. In abstract argumentation, we consider directed graphs, so-called argumentation frameworks (AF), that express conflicts between arguments. The semantics is defined by the notion of extensions, which are sets of arguments that satisfy particular relationship conditions in the AF. Usually, standard reasoning in argumentation do not reveal how far apart extensions are. We introduce a quantitative notion of diversity of extensions based on the symmetric difference and provide a systematic complexity classification. Intuitively, diversity captures whether extensions of a framework (accepted viewpoints) differ only marginally or represent fundamentally incompatible sets of arguments. We study whether an AF admits k-diverse extensions, admits k-diverse extensions covering specific arguments, and to compute the largest k for which an AF admits k-diverse extensions. We outline a prototype and provide an evaluation for computing diversity levels.

Method: The authors construct Ego2World on top of the HD-EPIC egocentric cooking dataset. This architecture cleanly separates the true world from the agent’s beliefs, enforcing planning and memory updates under partial observability.

They find that action-overlap scores systematically overestimate actual task success in terms of world state. Conclusion: Ego2World provides an executable benchmark grounded in real egocentric videos that focuses on partial observability, belief maintenance, and realistic state dynamics. The findings indicate that current evaluation metrics are misleading and that robust belief tracking and memory should be central objectives in developing and benchmarking embodied agents in household environments.

Abstract: Embodied agents in household environments must plan under partial observation: they need to remember objects, track state changes, and recover when actions fail. Existing benchmarks only partially test this ability. Egocentric video datasets capture realistic human activities but remain passive, while interactive simulators support execution but rely on synthetic scenes and hand-crafted dynamics, introducing a sim-to-real gap and often assuming fully observable state. We introduce Ego2World, an executable benchmark that turns egocentric cooking videos into executable symbolic worlds governed by graph-transition rules. Built on HD-EPIC, Ego2World derives reusable transition rules from video annotations and executes them in a hidden symbolic world graph. During evaluation, the simulator maintains the hidden world graph, while the agent plans over its own partial belief graph using only local observations and execution feedback. This separation forces agents to update memory and replan without observing the true world state. Experiments show that action-overlap scores overestimate physical-state success, and that persistent belief memory improves task completion while reducing repeated visual exploration -- suggesting that belief maintenance should be a first-class target of embodied-agent evaluation.

Method: Redefine tool selection as active exploration of the tool space. Introduce Skill encapsulation technology at the tool side to hierarchically structure tool descriptions. The agent first reads only short skill summaries to choose relevant branches, then progressively loads detailed descriptions for selected tools, and finally performs precise tool invocation, enabling on-demand, sequential decision-making over tools.

Existing RS agents adopt a passive selection paradigm for tool invocation, relying on either full tool registration (Flat) or retrieval-augmented generation (RAG). However, in the massive and multi-source heterogeneous RS tool ecosystem, such passive mechanisms struggle to dynamically balance "context load" and "toolset completeness" throughout task reasoning, thus exhibiting inherent limitations: full tool registration triggers context space deficits during long-horizon tasks, whereas RAG retrieval may omit critical tools in essential steps. To overcome these bottlenecks, this paper redefines tool selection by arguing that the agent should act as an active explorer within the tool space. Based on this perspective, we propose RS-Claw, a novel RS agent architecture. By leveraging Skill encapsulation technology at the tool end, this architecture hierarchically structures tool descriptions, enabling the agent to execute on-demand sequential decision-making: initially selecting relevant skill branches by reading only tool summaries, then dynamically loading detailed descriptions, and ultimately achieving precise invocation. This active paradigm not only significantly liberates the agent's context space but also effectively ensures the accurate hit rate of critical tools during long-horizon reasoning. Systematic experiments on the Earth-Bench benchmark demonstrate that RS-Claw's active exploration mechanism effectively filters semantic noise and substantially frees up reasoning space, achieving an input token compression ratio of up to 86%, and comprehensively outperforming existing Flat and RAG baselines across complex reasoning evaluations.

### [106] [TRIAGE: Evaluating Prospective Metacognitive Control in LLMs under Resource Constraints](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.13414)
*Zabir Al Nazi,Shubhashis Roy Dipta*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper introduces TRIAGE, a framework to test whether language models can optimally allocate limited compute across multiple tasks without feedback, revealing large gaps in their metacognitive control.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Autonomous LLM agents working under finite token/compute budgets must decide which tasks to tackle, in what order, and how many tokens to spend on each before seeing any outcomes. This ability—prospective metacognitive control—is well studied in humans but has not been systematically evaluated in language models, leaving a key capability for efficient deployment unmeasured.

Method: The authors design TRIAGE, an evaluation where a model receives (1) a pool of tasks from diverse domains and (2) a token budget calibrated to that model’s baseline solving cost. The model must commit up-front to a single ordered plan that jointly specifies: which problems to attempt, in what sequence, and how many tokens to allocate per problem. This plan is executed without intermediate feedback. Performance is compared to an oracle that knows, for that model, the true solvability and token cost of each problem. The ratio between the model’s achieved score and the oracle’s score defines a triage efficiency metric. They apply this to various frontier and open-source LLMs, with and without explicit reasoning modes, across domains including competition math, graduate-level science, coding, and expert knowledge tasks.

Result: Across model families and domains, current LLMs fall significantly short of the oracle’s triage efficiency: they misprioritize tasks, misestimate needed compute, and underperform in overall reward per token relative to what would be possible given their underlying problem-solving abilities. Enabling chain-of-thought or reasoning modes does not fully close this gap.

Conclusion: Prospective metacognitive control—the ability to plan task selection and compute allocation under budget constraints—is a distinct capability dimension where existing LLMs are notably weak, even when their per-task accuracy is strong. TRIAGE exposes this weakness and provides a standardized way to measure progress, with clear implications for designing more resource-efficient and reliable autonomous LLM agents.

Abstract: Deploying language models as autonomous agents requires more than per-task accuracy: when an agent faces a queue of problems under a finite token budget, it must decide which to attempt, in what order, and how much compute to commit to each, all before any execution feedback is available. This is the prospective form of metacognitive control studied for decades in human cognition, yet whether language models possess it remains untested. We introduce TRIAGE, an evaluation framework in which a model receives a task pool and a token budget calibrated to its own baseline cost, and commits to a single ordered plan that jointly encodes selection, sequencing, and per-problem allocation. Plans are scored against an oracle with full knowledge of the model's solvability and cost on each problem, yielding a triage efficiency ratio on a common scale. We evaluate frontier and open-source models, with and without reasoning enabled, across competition mathematics, graduate-level science, code generation, and expert multidisciplinary knowledge, and find that current language models exhibit substantial gaps in prospective metacognitive control, revealing a previously unmeasured capability dimension with direct implications for resource-efficient agent deployment.

</details>


### [107] [Cognifold: Always-On Proactive Memory via Cognitive Folding](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.13438)
*Suli Wang,Yiqun Duan,Yu Deng,Rundong Zhao,Dai Shi,Xinliang Zhou*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper proposes CogniFold, a continuously operating, brain-inspired memory system for AI agents that autonomously organizes streaming experiences into higher-level cognitive structures and demonstrates both human-like structure formation and strong performance on standard memory benchmarks.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Most current agent memory systems are passive, retrieval-based logs that react only when queried and do not self-organize experiences into long-term, structured knowledge or intentions. To move toward more autonomous, proactive assistants, the authors seek a memory architecture that continuously and independently builds and maintains cognitive structures from an ongoing stream of events, analogous to human cognition.

Method: The authors introduce CogniFold, an "always-on" memory architecture inspired by brain function. They extend Complementary Learning Systems theory from a 2-layer model (hippocampus and neocortex) to a 3-layer model that adds a prefrontal intent layer. CogniFold uses graph-topology self-organization: as events stream in, they are folded into an evolving graph where cognitive structures automatically form, merge if semantically similar, decay when not used, relink via associative recall, and trigger explicit intents when clusters reach a certain concept density. They evaluate these structural properties using a new benchmark, CogEval-Bench, and test task performance across seven broad benchmarks covering five cognitive domains.

Result: CogniFold generates memory graphs whose emergent structures align with expected cognitive organization and concept emergence patterns, as measured by CogEval-Bench. In addition, on seven diverse benchmarks spanning five cognitive domains, CogniFold performs strongly on conventional memory tasks, indicating that its proactive, self-organizing properties do not come at the cost of standard memory capabilities.

Conclusion: CogniFold provides a brain-inspired, three-layer memory architecture that continuously transforms fragmented event streams into stable, higher-level cognitive and intent structures. The system not only forms cognitively plausible memory organizations but also maintains robust performance on standard memory benchmarks, positioning it as a foundation for more autonomous, proactive AI assistants.

Abstract: Existing agent memory remains predominantly reactive and retrieval-based, lacking the capacity to autonomously organize experience into persistent cognitive structure. Toward genuinely autonomous agents, we introduce Cognifold, a brain-inspired "always-on" agent memory designed for the next generation of proactive assistants. CogniFold continuously folds fragmented event streams into self-emerging cognitive structures, bootstrapping progressively higher-level cognition from incoming events and accumulated knowledge. We ground this by extending Complementary Learning Systems (CLS) theory from two layers (hippocampus, neocortex) to three, adding a prefrontal intent layer. Emulating the prefrontal cortex as the locus of intentional control and decision-making, CogniFold achieves this through graph-topology self-organization: cognitive structures proactively assemble under the stream, merge when semantically similar, decay when stale, relink through associative recall, and surface intents when concept-cluster density crosses a threshold. We evaluate structural formation using CogEval-Bench, demonstrating that CogniFold uniquely produces memory structures that match cognitive expectations and concept emergence. Furthermore, across 7 broad-coverage benchmarks spanning five cognitive domains, we validate that CogniFold simultaneously performs robustly on conventional memory benchmarks.

### [108] [Assessing the Creativity of Large Language Models: Testing, Limits, and New Frontiers](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.13450)
*Samuel Schapiro,Alexi Gladstone,Jonah Black,Heng Ji*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper evaluates how well human creativity tests predict different kinds of LLM creativity and introduces a new test, DRAT, which uniquely predicts scientific ideation ability in LLMs.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing creativity tests designed for humans are being reused to evaluate LLMs, but their validity for machines is unknown and they already have limitations even for humans. There is a need to understand whether these tests actually measure LLM creativity, especially across different creative domains such as writing, divergent thinking, and scientific ideation.

Method: The authors conduct a large-scale, systematic evaluation of multiple human creativity tests applied to LLMs, examining how well each test predicts LLM performance on three constructs: creative writing, divergent thinking, and scientific ideation. They compare tests like the Divergent Association Task (DAT), Conditional DAT, and Remote Associates Test, and then design a new vocabulary-based test, the Divergent Remote Association Test (DRAT), that jointly assesses convergent and divergent thinking. They analyze predictive validity and robustness across design choices and test whether DRAT’s predictive power can be reproduced by linear combinations of existing tests.

Result: DAT is the best predictor of LLM creative writing, and Conditional DAT best predicts divergent thinking, but no single existing test predicts all constructs well. Crucially, none of the existing tests reliably predicts LLM scientific ideation ability. The new DRAT test significantly predicts scientific ideation, remains robust under different design decisions, and its predictive performance cannot be matched by any linear combination of DAT and the standard Remote Associates Test.

Conclusion: Human creativity tests differ in how well they capture various aspects of LLM creativity, and relying on a single traditional test is insufficient—especially for scientific ideation. Combining convergent and divergent thinking within a single instrument, as in DRAT, appears necessary for reliably measuring scientific ideation in LLMs, making DRAT the first validated creativity test for this specific capability in language models.

Abstract: Measuring the creativity of large language models (LLMs) is essential for designing methods that can improve creativity and for enhancing our scientific understanding of this ability. To accomplish this, it has become common in recent years to administer tests of human creativity to LLMs. Although these tests provide a convenient and fully automated way to score "creativity," their validity as measures of machine creativity has not been established, and these tests already have limited validity as predictors of human creativity. To address this problem, we conduct the first large-scale, systematic study assessing the effectiveness of human creativity tests for predicting the creative achievement of LLMs across three target constructs: creative writing, divergent thinking, and scientific ideation. We find that the Divergent Association Task (DAT) and the Conditional DAT are the best predictors of creative writing and divergent thinking, respectively, but that test effectiveness varies significantly by construct, and no single test predicts all constructs well. Moreover, contrary to popular belief, no existing test reliably predicts scientific ideation ability. Motivated by this problem, we introduce the Divergent Remote Association Test (DRAT), a vocabulary-space test that assesses both convergent and divergent thinking in a single instrument. The DRAT is the first and only creativity test for LLMs that is a significant predictor of scientific ideation ability, demonstrating robustness across major design choices. Furthermore, the performance gain of the DRAT is not recoverable from any linear combination of the Divergent Association Task and the Remote Associates Test, indicating that assessing divergent and convergent thinking in the same test is essential to reliably predicting scientific ideation ability.

### [109] [MMSkills: Towards Multimodal Skills for General Visual Agents](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.13527)
*Kangning Zhang,Shuai Shao,Qingyao Li,Jianghao Lin,Lingyue Fu,Shijian Wang,Wenxiang Jiao,Yuan Lu,Weiwen Liu,Weinan Zhang,Yong Yu*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper introduces MMSkills, a framework for packaging and using reusable multimodal procedural skills to improve visual agents.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing reusable skill packages for agents are mostly textual or code-based and fail to capture the inherently multimodal nature of procedural knowledge required by visual agents, which must understand visual state, monitor progress, and choose next actions based on images.

Method: They define multimodal procedural knowledge and design MMSkills, a compact, state-conditioned representation that combines textual procedures with runtime state cards and multi-view keyframes. They build a trajectory-to-skill Generator that converts public interaction trajectories into these skill packages via workflow grouping, procedure induction, visual grounding, and meta-skill-guided auditing. They further propose a branch-loaded multimodal skill agent that loads relevant skills, inspects state cards and keyframes in a temporary branch, aligns them with the current environment, and distills them into structured guidance for the main agent.

Result: On GUI and game-based visual-agent benchmarks, using MMSkills improves performance for both state-of-the-art and smaller multimodal agents, demonstrating better decision making when equipped with external multimodal procedural knowledge.

Conclusion: MMSkills provide an effective way to represent and leverage reusable multimodal procedures, showing that external multimodal procedural knowledge systematically enhances visual agents beyond what is captured in model-internal priors alone.

Abstract: Reusable skills have become a core substrate for improving agent capabilities, yet most existing skill packages encode reusable behavior primarily as textual prompts, executable code, or learned routines. For visual agents, however, procedural knowledge is inherently multimodal: reuse depends not only on what operation to perform, but also on recognizing the relevant state, interpreting visual evidence of progress or failure, and deciding what to do next. We formalize this requirement as multimodal procedural knowledge and address three practical challenges: (I) what a multimodal skill package should contain; (II) where such packages can be derived from public interaction experience; and (III) how agents can consult multimodal evidence at inference time without excessive image context or over-anchoring to reference screenshots. We introduce MMSkills, a framework for representing, generating, and using reusable multimodal procedures for runtime visual decision making. Each MMSkill is a compact, state-conditioned package that couples a textual procedure with runtime state cards and multi-view keyframes. To construct these packages, we develop an agentic trajectory-to-skill Generator that transforms public non-evaluation trajectories into reusable multimodal skills through workflow grouping, procedure induction, visual grounding, and meta-skill-guided auditing. To use them, we introduce a branch-loaded multimodal skill agent: selected state cards and keyframes are inspected in a temporary branch, aligned with the live environment, and distilled into structured guidance for the main agent. Experiments across GUI and game-based visual-agent benchmarks show that MMSkills consistently improve both frontier and smaller multimodal agents, suggesting that external multimodal procedural knowledge complements model-internal priors.

*Juho Leinonen,Lisa Zhang,Arto Hellas*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper evaluates how well various generative AI tools can create lecture slides from instructor-written notes and how students perceive these AI-generated slides compared to human-made ones.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Instructors spend substantial time preparing lecture slides, and the growing availability of GenAI tools suggests they might help automate or streamline this work. However, it is unclear which tools produce pedagogically sound slides and how students perceive AI-generated materials in terms of quality and authenticity.

Method: The authors test five GenAI tools: one end-to-end educational tool (NotebookLM), two general-purpose LLMs (Claude, M365 Copilot), and two coding assistants (Cursor, Claude Code). They input instructor-authored course notes into each tool to generate slides, then have educators narratively assess the resulting slide decks for accuracy, completeness, and pedagogical quality. The best AI-generated slides (with light instructor modification) are then used in a real course. Student perceptions of human- vs. AI-generated slides are collected and compared, including quality ratings and students’ guesses about whether slides were AI-generated.

Result: Educator assessment shows that coding-oriented assistants (Cursor, Claude Code) produce the most accurate, complete, and pedagogically sound slides. In classroom deployment, students rate GenAI-produced slides as similar in quality to instructor-created slides and are generally unable to reliably distinguish which slides were AI-generated. A negative correlation emerges between slide quality ratings and students’ likelihood of labeling slides as AI-generated, implying that students tend to attribute lower-quality materials to AI.

Conclusion: GenAI tools—particularly coding assistants—can effectively support slide creation from instructor notes, yielding materials that both educators and students find comparable in quality to human-crafted slides. Students struggle to detect AI authorship and appear biased to associate poor quality with AI. The study suggests significant opportunities for responsibly integrating GenAI into instructional design workflows, while underscoring the need for further research on best practices and ethical, transparent deployment.

Abstract: As generative AI (GenAI) tools become easily accessible, there is promise in using such tools to support instructors. To that end, this paper examines using GenAI to help generate slides from instructor authored course notes, emphasizing instructor and student perceptions. We examine an end-to-end education tool (NotebookLM), two general-purpose LLMs (Claude, M365 Copilot), and two coding assistants (Cursor, Claude Code). We first analyze whether GenAI generated slides are ``good'' via narrative assessment by educators. We choose the best slides to use (with some modification) in a real course setting, and compare the student perception of human vs. AI generated slides. We find that coding assistant tools produce slides that were most accurate, complete, and pedagogically sound. Additionally, students rate GenAI slides to be of similar quality as instructor-created slides, and cannot reliably identify which slides are AI-generated. Additionally, we find a negative correlation between a high quality rating and a high ``AI-generated'' rating, suggesting students associate poor quality with the source of the slides being AI. These findings highlight promising opportunities for integrating GenAI into instructional design workflows and call for further research on how educators can best harness such tools responsibly and effectively.

### [111] [Scaling Retrieval-Augmented Reasoning with Parallel Search and Explicit Merging](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.13534)
*Jiabei Liu,Wenyu Mao,Junfei Tan,Chunxu Shen,Lingling Yi,Jiancan Wu,Xiang Wang*

Main category: cs.AI

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing deep search agents for LLMs typically issue a single retrieval query at each reasoning step, which restricts information coverage and makes the reasoning process vulnerable to noisy or suboptimal retrievals. This single-query setup often leads to low signal-to-noise ratios in the retrieved evidence, harming reasoning accuracy and causing extra, unnecessary reasoning steps. The paper aims to alleviate these issues by redesigning how retrieval is performed and exploited during multi-step reasoning.

Method: The authors introduce MultiSearch, an agent framework that (1) generates multiple retrieval queries from different perspectives at each reasoning step, (2) retrieves external information in parallel for these queries, and (3) explicitly merges and refines the retrieved snippets to form a higher-quality evidence set. They further design a reinforcement learning training scheme with a multi-process reward function that simultaneously encourages good multi-query generation, effective information consolidation, and accurate final answers.

Result: On seven question-answering benchmarks, MultiSearch outperforms existing deep-search and retrieval-augmented LLM baselines. The experiments show higher signal-to-noise ratios in retrieval results and improved reasoning accuracy, confirming the effectiveness of multi-query retrieval plus explicit merging under the proposed RL training framework.

Conclusion: MultiSearch demonstrates that combining multi-perspective query generation, parallel retrieval, and explicit evidence merging—optimized with a dedicated RL framework—can significantly enhance the quality of retrieved information and downstream reasoning performance of LLM agents. This suggests that future retrieval-augmented reasoning systems should move beyond single-query retrieval and incorporate structured multi-query and consolidation mechanisms.

Abstract: Deep search agents have proven effective in enhancing LLMs by retrieving external knowledge during multi-step reasoning. However, existing methods often generate a single query for retrieval at each reasoning step, limiting information coverage and introducing high noise. This may result in low signal-to-noise ratios (SNR) during search, degrading reasoning accuracy and leading to unnecessary reasoning steps. In this paper, we introduce MultiSearch, an RL-based framework that addresses these limitations through multi-query retrieval and explicit merging of retrieved information. At each reasoning step, MultiSearch generates queries from multiple perspectives and retrieves external information in parallel, expanding the scope of relevant information and mitigating the reliance on any single retrieval result. Then, the agent consolidates and refines retrieved information at the merging process, improving the SNR and ensuring more accurate reasoning. Additionally, we propose a reinforcement learning framework with a multi-process reward design to optimize agents for both multi-query retrieval and information consolidation. Extensive experiments on seven benchmarks demonstrate that MultiSearch outperforms baseline methods, enhancing the SNR of retrieval and improving reasoning performance in question-answering tasks.

### [112] [RealICU: Do LLM Agents Understand Long-Context ICU Data? A Benchmark Beyond Behavior Imitation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.13542)
*Chengzhi Shen,Weixiang Shen,Tobias Susetzky,Chen,Chen,Jun Li,Yuyuan Liu,Xuepeng Zhang,Zhenyu Gong,Daniel Rueckert,Jiazhen Pan*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: RealICU is a hindsight-annotated ICU benchmark that evaluates LLMs on realistic, long-horizon clinical decision support, revealing key safety and reasoning failures and offering a testbed for structured-memory agents.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: ICU environments produce complex, rapidly evolving data streams, and clinicians must make frequent, high-stakes decisions under time pressure. Existing AI benchmarks often treat clinician actions as ground truth, but these actions are based on incomplete, local information and can be suboptimal, making them unreliable for evaluating true reasoning capabilities of AI systems. There is a need for a benchmark that reflects clinically validated, hindsight-informed judgments over full patient trajectories to assess and improve LLM-based decision support in critical care.

Method: The authors construct RealICU, a benchmark using ICU data partitioned into 30-minute windows along full patient trajectories from MIMIC-IV. Senior physicians review entire trajectories and then provide hindsight labels for each window on four tasks: (1) Patient Status, (2) Acute Problems, (3) Recommended Actions, and (4) Red Flag actions (unsafe or high-risk decisions). Two datasets are created: RealICU-Gold with 930 annotated windows from 94 patients labeled directly by physicians, and RealICU-Scale with 11,862 windows labeled by an LLM-based Oracle that is validated by physicians. The authors then evaluate existing LLMs, including memory-augmented models and structured-memory agents (ICU-Evo), on these tasks to analyze performance and failure modes.

Result: On RealICU, current LLMs, even with memory augmentation, perform poorly across the four tasks. The experiments reveal two main failure modes: (1) a recall-safety tradeoff in clinical recommendations, where improving recall for recommended actions tends to increase unsafe suggestions, and (2) anchoring bias, where models become overly attached to early interpretations of the patient state and fail to update adequately as new data arrives. The ICU-Evo structured-memory agent improves long-horizon reasoning relative to standard LLM setups but still exhibits safety failures and does not fully resolve the identified issues.

Conclusion: RealICU is presented as a clinically grounded, hindsight-annotated benchmark for evaluating and improving LLM-based decision support in ICUs. By using physician-validated labels over full trajectories, it avoids the limitations of action-as-ground-truth benchmarks and better exposes core reasoning and safety weaknesses in current models, such as recall-safety tradeoffs and anchoring bias. The benchmark, along with ICU-Evo, offers a testbed for developing more reliable, long-horizon, sequential decision-support systems for high-stakes clinical care.

Abstract: Intensive care units (ICU) generate long, dense and evolving streams of clinical information, where physicians must repeatedly reassess patient states under time pressure, underscoring a clear need for reliable AI decision support. Existing ICU benchmarks typically treat historical clinician actions as ground truth. However, these actions are made under incomplete information and limited temporal context of the underlying patient state, and may therefore be suboptimal, making it difficult to assess the true reasoning capabilities of AI systems. We introduce RealICU, a hindsight-annotated benchmark for evaluating large language models (LLMs) under realistic ICU conditions, where labels are created after senior physicians review the full patient trajectory. We formulate four physician-motivated tasks: assess Patient Status, Acute Problems, Recommended Actions, and Red Flag actions that risk unsafe outcomes. We partition each trajectory with 30-min windows and release two datasets: RealICU-Gold with 930-window annotations from 94 MIMIC-IV patients, and RealICU-Scale with 11,862 windows extended by Oracle, a physician-validated LLM hindsight labeler. Existing LLMs including memory-augmented ones performed poorly on RealICU, exposing two failure modes: a recall-safety tradeoff for clinical recommendations, and an anchoring bias to early interpretations of the patient. We further introduce ICU-Evo to study structured-memory agents that improves long-horizon reasoning but does not fully eliminate safety failures. Together, RealICU provides a clinically grounded testbed for measuring and improving AI sequential decision-support in high-stakes care. Project page: https://chengzhi-leo.github.io/RealICU-Bench/

### [113] [Learning Local Constraints for Reinforcement-Learned Content Generators](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.13570)
*Debosmita Bhaumik,Julian Togelius,Georgios N. Yannakakis,Ahmed Khalifa*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: Hybrid method combining Wave Function Collapse (WFC) constraints with reinforcement learning (PCGRL) to generate visually pleasing yet playable puzzle-platform levels with specified global properties.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing constraint-based generators like WFC create locally consistent, visually appealing content but struggle to guarantee global properties such as playability. Conversely, reinforcement-learning generators can encode global objectives in reward functions but often yield visually unsatisfying content. The motivation is to integrate the strengths of both approaches to get content that is both globally valid and locally coherent/beautiful.

Method: Use WFC to learn local tile-pattern constraints from example levels, then use these constraints to restrict the action space of a Procedural Content Generation via Reinforcement Learning (PCGRL) agent. The RL agent edits levels while always respecting WFC-derived local constraints, and is rewarded for achieving desired global properties (e.g., playability). The authors systematically vary hyperparameters: number and type of input examples, whether to randomly collapse the starting state, and whether to exclude rare patterns, to study how these design decisions affect performance.

Result: The hybrid WFC+PCGRL generators can produce puzzle-platform levels (e.g., Lode Runner) that are both visually similar to training examples and satisfy global properties like playability. However, performance is sensitive to hyperparameter choices, meaning not all configurations work equally well.

Conclusion: Constrained RL, where WFC-derived local constraints limit the RL agent’s editing actions, is a promising strategy for procedural content generation that reconciles local visual quality with global structural properties. Although the approach requires careful hyperparameter tuning, it demonstrates that hybridizing constraint-based and RL-based methods can yield playable, visually satisfying game levels with controllable global characteristics.

Abstract: Constraint-based game content generators that learn local constraints from existing content, such as Wave Function Collapse (WFC), can generate visually satisfying game levels but face challenges in guaranteeing global properties, such as playability. On the other hand, reinforcement-learning trained generators can guarantee global properties -- because such properties can easily be included in reward functions -- but the results can be visually dissatisfying. In this paper, we explore ways to combine these methods. Specifically, we constrain the action space of a PCGRL generator with constraints learned by WFC, effectively allowing the PCGRL generator to achieve global properties while forced to adhere to local constraints. To better analyze how this hybrid content generation method operates, we vary the number and type of inputs, and we test whether to randomly collapse the starting state and exclude rare patterns. While the method is sensitive to hyperparameter tuning, the best of our trained generators produce visually satisfying and playable puzzle-platform game levels -- such as Lode Runner levels -- with desired global properties.

Addressing these requires a lifecycle approach—user-centered research, accessibility-aware system design, and ongoing iteration—ultimately pushing the field toward more inclusive and robust AI agent design.

Abstract: Assistive agents for Blind and Visually Impaired (BVI) users require accessibility alignment as a first-class design objective. Despite rapid progress in agentic AI, most systems are designed and evaluated under assumptions of sighted interaction, low-cost verification, and tolerable trial-and-error, leading to systematic failures in assistive scenarios that cannot be resolved by model scaling or post-hoc interface adaptations alone. Drawing on an analysis of 778 assistance task instances from prior work, we show that current agentic AI remain prone to failure in assistive scenarios due to mismatches between sighted-user design assumptions and the verification, risk, and interaction constraints faced by BVI users. We argue that accessibility should be treated as an alignment problem rather than a peripheral usability concern. To this end, we introduce accessibility alignment and propose a lifecycle-oriented design pipeline for accessibility-aligned assistive agents, spanning user research, system design, deployment and post-deployment iteration.   <summary>Details</summary>

Traditional value-based decision-making frameworks often rely on stakeholder-assigned weights for criteria, which inject normative bias and can threaten fairness, integrity, and security. There is a need for a human-centred, values-driven, and more objective way to combine qualitative and quantitative criteria without arbitrary weighting, while still being computationally tractable and robust in practice.

Method: The paper proposes the FUW-VBDM (Fuzzy-Unweighted Value-Based Decision Making) framework. It removes prior stakeholder-assigned weights and instead defines a fuzzy domain over decision variables and a score function, reformulating VBDM as an optimisation over the weight domain to find feasible solutions. They provide a mathematical proof of consistency for Rankzzy under any admissible stakeholder configuration. A running case study is used to instantiate and illustrate the approach.

Result: Rankzzy is shown to produce consistent rankings for any allowed stakeholder configuration and to effectively handle both qualitative and quantitative criteria under uncertainty. In empirical evaluation, especially when using Pythagorean means for aggregation, Rankzzy demonstrates lower computational cost on large-scale value-based decision-making problems and competitive or superior ranking performance compared with existing VBDM approaches. The case study confirms the framework’s practical applicability.

Conclusion: The FUW-VBDM framework and its Rankzzy algorithm offer a human-centred, values-oriented decision-making method that mitigates normative bias by eliminating arbitrary weights while integrating fuzzy reasoning to manage uncertainty. The approach is mathematically consistent, scalable to large problems, and yields strong ranking quality compared to current methods, suggesting it is a promising basis for value-aligned decision support in intelligent systems.

Abstract: As intelligent systems are increasingly implemented in our society to make autonomous decisions, their commitment to human values raises serious concerns. Their alignment with human values remains a critical challenge because it can jeopardise the integrity and security of citizens. For this reason, an innovative human-centred and values-driven approach to decision making is required. In this work, we introduce the Fuzzy-Unweighted Value-Based Decision Making (FUW-VBDM) framework, where agents incorporate both quantitative and qualitative criteria to generate human-centred decisions. We also address the normative bias introduced by stakeholders with arbitrary weights by removing prior weights and introducing a fuzzy domain of decision variables defined for a score function. This concept allows us to generalise any VBDM problem as the search for feasible solutions when optimising the score in the weight domain. To provide a solution to FUW-VBDM, we present Rankzzy, a customizable unweighted ranking method that integrates fuzzy-based reasoning to quantify uncertainty. We mathematically prove the consistency of the Rankzzy for any admissible configuration selected by stakeholders. We show the applicability of our method through an illustrative case study, which we also use as a running example. The evaluation conducted indicates a reduced computational cost in large-scale value-based decision-making problems and a strong rank performance regarding existing approaches when employing the aggregation via Pythagorean means.

A primary way to gain this understanding is analyzing the reasoning trajectories and execution traces these agents generate. Yet such data remains in unstructured natural-language form, making it difficult for humans to interpret at scale. We introduce ACT*ONOMY (a combination of Action and Taxonomy), a taxonomy for describing and analyzing agent behavior at runtime. ACT*ONOMY has two components: (1) the taxonomy itself, developed through Grounded Theory and structured as a three-level hierarchy of 10 actions, 46 subactions, and 120 leaf categories; and (2) an open repository that hosts the living taxonomy, provides an automated analysis pipeline that applies it to agent trajectories analysis, and defines an extension protocol for customization and growth. Our experiments show that ACTONOMY can compare behavioral profiles across agents and characterize a single agent's behavior across diverse trajectories, surfacing patterns indicative of failure modes. ### [117] [Adaptive mine planning under geological uncertainty: A POMDP framework for sequential decision-making](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.13702)
*Hamza Khalifi,Jef Caers,Yassine Taha,Mostafa Benzaazoua,Abdellatif Elghali*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper reframes mine production scheduling under geological uncertainty as an adaptive decision-making problem using a POMDP framework with simulated annealing-based value approximation and ensemble-based belief updating, significantly improving realized NPV and robustness compared with traditional stochastic optimization.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Traditional stochastic mine scheduling treats geological uncertainty passively by computing a fixed extraction and routing plan before operations begin, hedging across scenarios but not explicitly valuing or using information gained during mining. This leads to a substantial gap between expected and realized performance and weak robustness to prior misspecification. The authors aim to create a framework that actively exploits ongoing geological learning, closes the expectation–reality gap, and delivers structurally robust value under uncertainty.

Method: The authors formulate mine scheduling as a Partially Observable Markov Decision Process where extraction and routing decisions are made sequentially under an evolving geological belief state. They propose a hybrid SA-POMDP solution method: (i) simulated annealing (SA) is used to approximate the value function and select actions with high expected long-term value under the current belief; (ii) the belief over geological attributes is updated through ensemble smoother with multiple data assimilation (ES-MDA) as new mining observations are collected. This generates an adaptive policy rather than a static schedule. The framework is applied to a copper-gold open-pit mine with multiple processing options.

Result: On a benchmark copper-gold open-pit with several processing destinations, the SA-POMDP framework significantly narrows the gap between expected and realized outcomes when the prior is statistically consistent, reducing the expectation-reality gap from 22.3% to 4.6% and adding about USD 8.4M in realized NPV compared to classic one-shot stochastic optimization. Under deliberate 10% systematic prior misspecification, the adaptive POMDP-based policy outperforms static stochastic plans by up to USD 44.6M (36.9%), indicating strong robustness to model error beyond conventional scenario hedging.

Conclusion: Recasting mine production scheduling as a POMDP with sequential belief updating converts geological uncertainty from a passive source of risk into an active lever for value creation. The proposed SA-POMDP, combining simulated annealing for value approximation with ES-MDA for ensemble-based belief updating, produces adaptive policies that significantly improve realized NPV and expectation-realization alignment and remain robust under prior misspecification. This demonstrates the practical benefit of explicitly planning for information acquisition and learning in large-scale mining operations.

Abstract: Strategic mine production scheduling under geological uncertainty is conventionally formulated as a stochastic optimization problem in which a fixed extraction sequence and routing decisions are computed ex ante. This plan-driven paradigm treats uncertainty as passive: decisions are hedged across geological scenarios, but planning does not anticipate how future observations will inform future decisions. We propose a different perspective by formulating mine scheduling as a Partially Observable Markov Decision Process (POMDP), in which extraction and routing decisions are made sequentially with planning explicitly integrating the expectation of future belief updates. To achieve computational tractability, we introduce a hybrid SA-POMDP architecture that combines simulated annealing-based (SA) value approximation with ensemble-based belief updating via ensemble smoother with multiple data assimilation (ES-MDA). At each decision epoch, candidate actions are evaluated through their expected long-term value under the current belief, and the belief is updated as mining observations are assimilated. This yields an adaptive policy rather than a fixed plan. We evaluate the framework on a copper-gold open-pit mining complex with multiple processing destinations. Under a statistically consistent prior, the SA-POMDP reduces the expectation-reality gap from 22.3% to 4.6%, improving realized NPV by USD8.4M relative to one-shot stochastic optimization. Under systematic prior misspecification of 10%, the adaptive framework outperforms static planning by up to USD44.6M (36.9%), demonstrating structural robustness beyond scenario hedging. These results show that sequential belief updating transforms geological uncertainty from a passive constraint into an active component of value creation.

</details>


### [118] [ScioMind: Cognitively Grounded Multi-Agent Social Simulation with Anchoring-Based Belief Dynamics and Dynamic Profiles](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.13725)
*Yitian Yang,Yiqun Duan,Linghan Huang,Yiqi Zhu,Francesco Bailo,Chunmeizi Su,Huaming Chen*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: ScioMind is an LLM-based multi-agent simulation framework that introduces cognitively grounded mechanisms—memory-anchored belief updates, hierarchical memory, and dynamic profiles—to produce more realistic and stable social opinion dynamics than prior rule-based or unconstrained LLM interaction approaches.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing LLM-based multi-agent social simulations either rely on rigid, psychologically shallow update rules or allow belief changes to emerge from mostly unconstrained LLM dialogues, which can lead to unrealistic or unstable opinion dynamics. There is a need for a framework that captures core insights from cognitive and political psychology—like memory, personality, and anchoring—while still leveraging LLMs for rich reasoning, in order to better model how real humans form, maintain, and update beliefs in social and policy debates.

Method: The authors design ScioMind, a simulation framework that integrates structured opinion dynamics with LLM reasoning. They introduce: (1) a memory-anchored belief update rule where each agent’s susceptibility to influence is modulated by a personality-conditioned anchoring strength; (2) a hierarchical memory system that lets agents form and update persistent beliefs from their experiences rather than reacting only to the latest interaction; and (3) dynamic agent profiles generated via a corpus-grounded retrieval pipeline, so agents have heterogeneous personalities, rationales, and evolving internal states. They then run multiple case studies in a real-world policy debate context and compare variants of the framework across metrics like polarisation, diversity, extremization, and trajectory stability.

Result: Across several case studies in a policy debate setting, ScioMind’s components improve behavioral realism of simulated opinion dynamics. Dynamic profiles lead to greater opinion diversity among agents, the hierarchical memory and reflection reduce unstable oscillations in opinions, and the anchoring mechanism creates more persistent belief trajectories. These simulated trajectories better match patterns documented in political psychology literature, and overall metrics such as polarization, extremization, and stability show more realistic behavior compared with baselines lacking these components.

Conclusion: ScioMind demonstrates that embedding cognitively motivated mechanisms—memory anchoring, hierarchical memory, and dynamic profiles—into LLM-based multi-agent simulations yields opinion dynamics that are both more stable and more behaviorally realistic than prior approaches. This cognitively grounded design offers a promising direction for using LLMs to study social opinion dynamics and policy debates in a way that better reflects empirical findings from psychology and related fields.

Abstract: Large language model (LLM)-based multi-agent simulation offers a powerful testbed for studying social opinion dynamics. Yet current approaches often adopt two contrasting methods: either relying on fixed update rules with limited cognitive grounding or delegating belief change largely to unconstrained LLM interaction. We introduce ScioMind, a cognitively grounded simulation framework that bridges these paradigms by combining structured opinion dynamics with LLM-based agent reasoning. ScioMind integrates three key components: 1) a memory-anchored belief update rule that modulates susceptibility to influence via personality-conditioned anchoring strength; 2) a hierarchical memory architecture that supports persistent, experience-driven belief formation; and 3) dynamic agent profiles derived from a corpus-grounded retrieval pipeline, enabling heterogeneous personalities, rationales, and evolving internal states. We evaluate ScioMind on multiple case studies in a real-world policy debate scenario. Across metrics including polarisation, diversity, extremization, and trajectory stability, the proposed components consistently yield improvements in behavioural realism. In particular, dynamic profiles increase opinion diversity, memory and reflection reduce unstable oscillation, and anchoring induces persistent belief trajectories that better align with patterns reported in political psychology. These results suggest that our cognitively grounded design provides a novel solution to LLM-based social simulation that improves both stable and behavioural realism
